THE RED PAGE
ANDROMIDA
She is a snared and prisoned thing —-
A meek white moth with broken icing.
Life took her heart when it was get
Too young for grieving or regret.
And slowly tamed his prisoner —
That glowing ‘woman’s heart of her !
She did not guess what Earth could give ;
She did not know she did not live ;
Caught from the sun in Work's grey net
And in a gloomy office set,
Her breast sometimes forgot to sigh:
Some days she hardly missed the sky.
Her dewy gladness dull Work took
To write dead figures in a book;
And on her high stool, hour by hour,
She sits —a frail and long-stemmed flower !
And the days drag, each day the same:
She is so soft a thing to maim!
She, made for love, of love compact,
Has half-forgot the love she lacked ;
She waits, a harp of slackened strings:
One word of lore its music brings.
Each hour is but a death she dies :
One hand in hers is Paradise.
And when I kiss her lips at night
She is a pool of still delight,
Her low laugh a triumphant thing,
Her voice a bird on buoyant wing ;
And when I whisper low her name
Her soul is but a shaken flame !
Her soul that dreams it is alive
The grey ghouls take—from nine till five.
She adds up figures—who to me
Is a god-given mystery !
They shut her heart in ledgers up —
Her heart that is a thirsty cup !
So long her life has bled and bled,
They pay dead wages to one dead.
Ah, still we chain, our gods to mock,
Andromeda upon her rock !
Brut that young stifled heart of her.. .
Unbind me, gods, her rescuer 1
[From “London Streets,”
Adams.]
By ARTHUR H.
“LONDON STHEFTS”
1.
IN reviewing his own little book the in-
herent egotism of the author must needs
be sternly trodden under foot by the
heavy boots of the critic ; yet the
author, immolating the child of his inspiration
upon the smooth altar of criticism, may ask
indulgence for a divided mind. Abraham,
swayed by paternal love, might be pardoned
if, as he raises the sacrificial knife above
Isaac,, his arm trembles and his aim is not
sure. “ J. 8.,” whose criticism of the same
book is destined, on arrival, to adorn the
adjoining column, can doubtless be depended
upon to strike with an unperturbed and un-
faltering aim. Thus the spectator is assured
that the sacrifice will be completed ; and the
reader who watches the knife drop from the
author’s nerveless hand will see “J. 8.” in
a business-like way complete the butchery.
The critic speaks :
London Streets, by Arthur H. Adams,
(Foulis, London, per J. I. Lothian, Melb.,
2s. 6 d.), is the third book of verse
from the same pen. His Maori Land and
Other Verses, published by the Bulletin,
was his first essay in poetry; his Nazarene,
published in London, dropped from the
publisher’s into oblivion, like a stone into
mud. And now London Streets, an attempt
to give a vision of London, has been highly
approved by English critics. As is apparent
from the opening sonnet, the author has not
succeeded in unveiling the soul of that city,
which,
like a vast grey cob-web lies
Upon green England.
London is too vast, too inchoate, to be
caged behind the fourteen frail bars of a
sonnet; but when the author turns to the
streets of the metropolis, as in Fleet Street,
The Temple, and Bond Street, he has drawn a
picture that “looks like the place.” For
instance, he has struck the key-note of The
Temple in lines like these
It is a heart of silence in
The city’s heart. Slip from the din,
Knock at a little hidden door,
And peace and solitude begin !
Fleet-street’s long echoes fade away,
The cool, wide shadows drift and stay—
Staid pigeons dreaming on the flags.
And large green leaves against the grey...
There is one palpable defect inherent in
the structure of the book. Being designedly
an impression of London, it runs the
risk of becoming a mere descriptive
catalogue. It is as if the author sat down
with a map of London and “ wrote it up.”
[The author, who must perforce remain
silent, merely writhes.] The reader of
Tennyson will find here a new vocabulary—
'to her possibly repellent—a language of
‘“’buses” and “motors,” and “cabs,” and
“ brass-plates,” and “factories,” and “cos-
ters’ stalls,” and “ chimney-pots,” and
“hat-pins,” and “lifts,” and “ shops,” and
“ typewriters,” and “flats,” and “terraces,”
and “tram-cars”—surely a jargon for the
newspaper and not the poem ? Yet, how
else to picture London ? It is of these
commonplace bricks of language that the
author must perforce build verses than aim
to express modernity.
Sometimes the fog—that faery girl !—»
Her veil of wonder will unfurl,
And crescent gaunt and looming flat
Are sudden mysteries of pearl!
New miracles the wet streets show ;
On stems of flame the gas-lamps glow.
I walk upon : the wave and see
Another London drowned below.
The choice of medium is, after all, a minor
matter ; the question is not by what means
the impression is conveyed, but whether by
the chosen means the impression is adequately
conveyed. And it seems to the critic that
the balance is towards the author.
In another way the pitfall of the descrip-
tive catalogue has been avoided. Permeating
every poem is a recognition of the inalienable
romance of life—the romance of the streets,
of the shops, of the work-room, that “grimly
persistent through the grey,” uplifts and
humanises the whole sordid scheme.
In every face that passes mine
TJnfathomed epics I divine :
Lach figure on the pavement is
A vial of untasted wine !...
And so ere sleep I lie and mark
Romance’s stealthy footsteps. Hark !
The rhythm of the horse’s hoof
Bears some new drama through the dark !...
Romance lies there with outstretched hand.
It is a new and faery land
Where I, high on a lurching ’bus,
Go charioteering down the Strand !
It is in the interludes, one of which is above
printed, that this romance of the common-
place is most satisfyingly rendered. The
author s compression of phrase and reticence
in expression in this volume mark a greater
maturity in poetic technique. The charmin' r
cover design is from the pencil of George W &
Lambert.
The Author.
II.
THAT we may not be disappointed by
the rest of his book, Adams crowds
examples of his most prominent
faults into an introductory sonnet—-
The ' IP eb. \\ ith this precedent, I begin
captiously. Over-emphasis, a determination
to be forcible at any price, leads to grotesque-
ness or extravagance of phrase, excess of
heavy syllables, and abruptness of style.
Sometimes it even betrays the poet into
a comical confusion of imagery. The first
eight lines of The Web exemplify the ex-
clamatory style—the style of staring head-
lines—by leaving us gaping for a principal
verb. The line—
Are caught and strangled—and yet are not dead !
is not metrical unless we overweight the verb
are. Then take the next two lines
And at the centre, silent and full-fed,
A spider, old, contemplative and wise !
Is the spider St. Paul’s—elswhere described
as “an old Buddha”—or the Lord Mayor?
“Full-fed” rather inclines us to the latter
explanation. The opening lines of the
sestet, which begins badly with “Ah, far,”
introduce metaphorical “filaments” which
“weave wizardry” and. “claim tribute.”
Then there is a sudden, disconcerting change
of subject. The last line cannot bear the
weight of the three heavy syllables on its
tail :—
My alien heart, shrivelled and long sucked dry!
The Web is the poorest piece in the book,
but its faults are typical. There are tail-
heavy lines in Ihe Strcind. For mixture of
imagery take this :—
They shut her heart in ledgers up—
Her heart that is a thirsty cup !
Or this \ v
• • • 4 [metaphorical] thin pulse that is a spur '
io those far-sprawling limbs of her.
For imagery that is momentarily baffling or
unintentionally ludicrous, take the cabs that
Hurry like hapless driven souls :
or the “aorta” beneath Fleet-street, which
is not a sewer or a water-main, but a print-
ing press. And so forth. The headline
style characterised by a lack of verbs, and
ornamented with dashes and marks of ex-
clamation—is rampant on every page.
What has Adams tried to do ? And how ?
From the standpoint of a sojourner, he
treats various aspects of London life-
describing the streets and suggesting the
hidden human forces which find expression
■in each locality. He succeeds through vivid-
ness of description and illuminative fancy.
At his best and. his worst he is alike fanciful.
Some of the fancies, which are snatched up
•
and'strewn along his streets like confetti in
carnival, are extravagant, but many are ex
cellent. This of the milliner’s needle is
worthy of Herrick—
All day her restless needle goes,
Above those glowing flowers of dream,
Above the gossamer and gleam,
Glancing in haste and hovering nigh,
Swift as a silver dragon-fly !
The final! is the poet’s accustomed note of
admiration. But he is equally enraptured
with the last lines of this poem, which are
marred by the falseness of the concluding
rime. And this, of Hyde Park, is worthy of
remembrance :—-
It is a robe of green dropt down—
A gallant blossom-broidered gown,
Elung by the Country as she fled
Erom the grim onset of the Town.
But there are too many happy fancies for us
to attempt to do more than indicate their
nature, their quaint descriptiveness. For
example, the crowd in Regent-street—
Like bees upon the honeycomb
Upon the windows clustering !
Or the women of the pavement—
Waste souls cast out upon the heap—
The slag of passion’s furnaces !
Sometimes a well-chosen epithet—-an epithet
that for once is not “far-fet and dear
bought ’’—adds reality to the picture. We
see “slow nuzzling tugs,” for instance,
moving through the grey mist of Chelsea,
and we know that we have seen them. Of
similar quality is the description of traffic
held up by a policeman, when
.. . Patient ’buses herded wait
With squat, fierce-spitting motor-cars.
And occasionally, along with this cinemato-
graphic description, is a suggestion of some-
thing vital, the invisible sources of this
abundant life. We find it in the cart-horses
of The Strand :
Cart-horses, alien in the loud
Terror of traffic, patient, proud,
Remembering quiet meadows wide,
Superbly triumph through the crowd.
And here it comes in the revelry of the East
End :
And even here Joy beats his wings
And to drab skies his madness flings :
In these dull courts, exultantly
Life like a prisoned song-bird sings !
Solemnly rapt, on pavements wet,
The sombre children pirouette :
These are the fields of Paradise ;
The morning stars are singing yet!
These are Adams’s higher flights, when he
mounts through the breezy atmosphere of
fancy, and claps his wings at Heaven’s
gate. Bond Street is the best piece in the
little collection, and it ends on a similar
note. Throughout we have the familiar
shower of fancies, tinged with humor,
realism, _ modernity, and we draw up on the
border-line of imagination.
J.B.
The latest of the Australian bards to venture
upon publication within covers is “Gilrooney,”
whose work has delighted many a Bulletin reader.
The book, published by J. 1. Lothian, Melb.,
will be titled The Land of the Starry Cross ; and
the verses composing it will be chiefly from Steele
Rudd’s Magazine , 'The Sydney Mail , The Sydney
Worker , and The Bulletin.
The"lndependent Review, is dead, and The Albany
Review arises from its ashes. It is the same price
and the same shape, published by the same firm
(John Lane, London), and contains the same class
of articles. These have chiefly to do with insular
English politics, about which a continent can be
excused if it does not become over-enthusiastic.
The only perceptible difference in the dead and
the living review is that one had a grey skin and
the other has a red.
A volume that can be commended to all those
who fought for the Mongol in S. Africa is The
Colonials in South Africa, by Capt. John Stirling
(Blackwood). In this work the author completes
his earlier book, which dealt with the regulars in
the war. He traces the records, based upon the
despatches, of all the colonial forces engaged, and
gives prominence to the views of British regular
officers for two reasons:—(l) A colonial corps
must realise that such opinions carry infinitely
more weight than anything said about them by
themselves or by their correspondents ; (2) some
regular officers, chiefly among those who did not
take part in the campaign, have written and
spoken in a way which shows clearly that they
have failed to appreciate the importance of the
part played by the irregulars. “It is well to
answer these officers,” says the author, “ from the
mouths of their brethren in the profession of arms.
• • • It would be foolish to blink the fact that without
the irregulars the war would never have been
ended when it was.” Speaking' of the alleged
lack of discipline likely to be found in irregular
troops, Stirling remarks : “ Those who have been
associated with organised volunteers, or irregulars
raised for a special campaign, have generally
found that discipline—that is, the state of being
earnestly anxious to learn and obey—is the quality
in which such troops excel.” The book gives a
record of the work done by each corps—three-
fifths of the book being devoted to the work of
corps raised in S. Africa, the remaining two-fifths
to Canadian and Australasian troops. And the
records make brave reading, The “ colonial ” can
fight—if we give him the weapons.
THE ART OF SIYING “OH!"
The modern world does not read poetry—
but need it ? A writer in a recent Putnam’s
Magazine, Gerald Stanley Lee, has some
disconcerting things to say about what he
calls the “glamor-trade” of poetry, the
business of gilding the world.
The most important fact in modern life, which
concerns modern art, is that the world is dropping
its exclamation points. Poetry has ceased saving
“ Oh ! ” about life, and has gone into the business
of living. Poetry used to consist in saying things
and suggesting that they might be done. It con-
sists, now, in doing things, and in suggesting
while it does them, that they never can be said
. . . We may be somewhat slow in recognising that
it is neither necessary, nor desirable, nor beauti-
ful, nor possible that Oh should be said in this
modern world ; but if Oh must be said, and there
is no help for it, it takes a Tennyson to say it.
And the feat of being a Tennyson without look-
ing ridiculous is getting more complicated every
year. ... Perhaps there is no more glorious
or poetic fact about the modern world than
the fact that “poetry” is not being read
in it. A man who can take the dust from
beneath his feet and breathe his own soul
into it—a man who every day of his life makes
the air about him speak with tongues—does not
feel the need of reading poetry. ... A man who
reads Walt Whitman for two hours feels like a
poet. If he rbads Tennyson for two hours he
feels that Tennyson is a poet. The first specifica-
tion for the next poet of this modern age is that
his poetry must be smokeless poetry, and that it
must not say Oh. The modern man already feels
so much like a poet, with the wonder of his
new world —his subways, and air ships,
and wireless telegraphs about him, day and night
—that he is less likely, as time goes on, to read
poetry—except when he is tired, or when the
poetry lifts him over to his real self, the one he is
living with every day, and makes him sure he is a
poet. . . . The poem on a compost heap may or
may not be a poem, but the bare fact that a man
has been born in the world at last who can write
a poem on a compost heap, or who wants to, is a
poem in itself.
«
The statement made on this page that the late
Lafcadio Hearn was an applicant for the chair of
English at Victoria University, M.L., though
made on apparently excellent authority, is evi-
dently without basis. Charles Wilson writes
“I was a member of the Council at that time,
and am still, and when I read your statement
failed to recall Hearn’s name as having ever been
mentioned as that of an applicant for the post
referred to. Through the kindness of the Regis-
trar, I have just gone through the two lists of
applicants for the various professorships. Neither
on the list of those who applied in or to London,-
nor on the list of applications made direct to
Wellington, does' Hearn’s name appear. He
might have thought of applying, but no applica-
tion was actually made, and, as far as I can
ascertain, no member of the Council can remember
Hearn’s name ever having been mentioned. Your
implied charge of Philistinism against the Uni-
versity authorities here is the only fault I
can find in what is a very pleasant and in-
teresting article on a charmingly original and
intellectually vigorous personality.” As the
writer’s authority, though appealed to, re-
mains silent, the writer herewith, with humility,
withdraws his alleged implied charge of alleged
imputed Philistinism. The University, which
has apparently been writhing under the imputa-
tion, may now cease to writhe.
One wonders what becomes of the enormous
number of worthless novels that pour out of the
modern publishers’ presses. The solution of the
problem seems to be that somebody must read
them. It seems that there is a vast public that
demands a new love-story every week. It doe 3
not matter whether the story is good or bad: all
that public asks is that it is new. To meet this
demand a horde of magazines devoted solely to
fiction has sprung into existence. These maga-
zines have no room for illustrations; and such
publications can be produced and sold extremely
cheaply. The latest of these is The Story-Teller ,
published by Cassell’s.' The procedure of the
editor of a new magazine in England seems to be
to capture as many of the old names for his con-
tributors as he can crowd into one number. The
Story-Teller begins by Hall Caine and ends with
Nat Gould, and those who ask for popular fiction
of the latest brand should be satisfied with the
quantity provided by this magazine for sixpence.
Copies have reached us from Gordon and Gotch
and the publishers.
“In populous Melbourne there was for years
not a single Scandinavian-born grocer, and at the
present time only one.” This statement, apolo-
getically made by Jens Lyng in The Scandinavians
in Australasia (Geo. Robertson and Co.), seems
to us the proudest thing that can be said of the
Scandinavian settler. , A nation that has pro-
duced only one grocer in Melbourne, and only one
oyster-saloon-keeper, only two hotel-keepers, and
not one ironmonger, nor a single draper, is a race
that deserves the respect of Australia. For this
sturdy element in our population goes on to the
land —the place where we want him to go, while
the Dago diligently cultivates the banana and the
oyster in the city. From Dr. Solander, the
Scandinavian who landed with Captain Cook at
Botany Bay, the author traces the immigrants
from his country to Australasia down to the pre-
sent day, when the total number of Scandinavians
in Australasia is between 22,000 and 25,000. The
Commonwealth would welcome any increase in
the infusion of such an excellent and sturdy racial
strain. “ The majority marry Australian, British,
or Irish girls. The Scandinavian tongue is con-
sequently shelved; the children grow up Austra-
lians, and the father’s environment becoming more
and more British [? Australian], he gradually gets
denationalised.” Jens Lyng’s recri'd of a race
that produced vikings but doeiji not'- produce
drapers, is well worth perusal.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Land of Spain.
I’d give a year or two of life,
And count the giving gain,
To be an idle fellow
In the lazy land of Spain.
I would not care a fig for Fame,
For fortune not a rap ;
I’d wear a velvet jacket
And a feather in my cap,
My cares should leave me like the leaves
That meet their destined hour
In Vallombrosa falling—
A red, autumnal shower.
By noon I’d sing a woodland song,
By night a lord I’d reign
In some delightful castle
I built of old in Spain.
I’d ask no groaning banquet-board—
A modest meal would please—
Goat’s flesh and wine and walnuts
To eat and drink at ease.
Full-stretched upon a couch of leaves,
When birds and bees were mute,
I’d turn my heart to music
And breathe it from a lute.
A dark-eyed damsel drawing nigh
Would voice a soft refrain—
Ah, maids are for the wooing
In the passion-land of Spain !
The people here run up and down,
They sell their souls for gold ;
And all that’s worth the having
Is either bought or sold.
The blue-eyed maiden sells her heart,
The man his youth and brain—
I’d love to be an idler
In the generous land of Spain.
N.S.W.
RODERIO QUINN.
The Girls of the Morning.
We have sung our songs of the Girls of Night—
The belles of the blazing bar;
Let us sing how bright, how pure and white
The Girls of the Morning are !
Let the hansom swirl with its midnight girl—
Leave the dude with his wine-flushed dove ;
For the girls of noon are a gracious boon,
And they are the best to love !
Aye, the Girls of the Morning shine like stars
Hung out in a cloudless sky ;
Leave the scented bars and the stale cigars—
For the Girls of the Morn go by ! •
Lo, the full red lip, like a carmine strip
Laid light on a field of cream ;
The eyes that flash, and the skirts that plash
Like the waves of a wanton stream !
They are lithe and tall, and are straight withal,
Like the stems of the soaring trees;
Tis a splendid fate that would haply mate
All men with girls like these !
And a warm, rich life—if each man’s wife
Had the grace of the waving corn—
If each, like wheat, curved soft and sweet,
Like the blessed Girls of Morn !
Lo, their voices thrill with a deep, rich trill,
Most gentle and debonnair ;
Each breast is a throne for a King to own—
And bronze are their wastes of hair.
We have sung too much of the Girls of Night—
The belles of the blazing bar ;
Let us sing how bright—how pure and white—
The Girls of the Morning are !
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
Ballade of Farewell.
Out of the twilight a lost wind heaves,
Mocking and rocking it swims and veers,
Waking the little folk under the eaves —
Strange little folk with low-whispered fears.
Sullen and silent the night uprears,
Flooding my soul with a potent spell.
Come to me, star of my loneliest years,
Here shall the trysting be—here farewell 1
Low on the threshold a grey Thing grieves,
The tree at the casement leans dowri and peers.
Hist! like the rustle of autumn leaves,
White as a moonbeam her faint form sheers;
Eyes far-shining, as drenched with tears,
Out of the tumult, and stress, and swell,
Safe from the highway that points and jeers,
So, in mine arms for a long farewell.
Hotly my bosom her face receives ;
Wherefore the scorn, dear heart, and the sneers ?
Since I must love you, no pang bereaves,
Though the gods are careless, and parting nears,
There shall be light when the shadow clears,
Halcyon heights when the mists dispel.
Death is outside, so bar your ears,
That your heart hear never the last farewell.
L’Envoi.
Sire, when out of the sun that cheers,
I take the tide to a slow-swung bell,
Grant that the Boatman who bravely steers
Find her who kissed me farewell, farewell I
M.L.
O. ALLAN MARRIS.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise•
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,

THURSDAY, MAY 16, 1907.
CARRUTHERS’ SURPLUS, AND HIS BOR-
ROWINGS, AND HIS ALLEGED POLICY.
New South AVales has now enjoyed the blessings
of the Carruthers Government for close on three
years—ever since Waddell threw up the sponge
without a fight, and went away to his corner,
licked when the beginning of the first round
was only visible through a telescope. About
three months hence the State will have an election
to decide whether it wants a continuance of the
blessings of Carruthers administration. Prob-
ably, by reason of the garishness of Owe’Sullivan
andtheheavy-goingordinarinessof McGowen, who
is ordinary beyond all reason, Joseph will get a
new lease of office. It is impossible to suppose that
he would do so if anybody who amounted to even
half or one-third of a statesman opposed him, but
the impossible slowness of McGowen would give
anybody a chance. A caterpillar could keep
ahead of McGowen. There is a splendid chance
just now in N.S.W. for a party which would
come out as the Federal party as against
Joseph’s sour, petty and malignant parochialism,
but the bulk of the Parliamentary Labor party
follows Joseph in his parochialism. There is
a still grander chance for a party which would
come out as the Anti-Loan crowd —the people
who would stand for sound and honest finance—
but the bulk of the Labor men haven’t the sense
to recognise the opportunity to be honest or the
need of honesty. There are brilliant exceptions—
honest men like Dacey, and a few others—but in
the main the Laborites are just sluggish borrowers
and drifters like Carruthers. There is so little
difference between the drifting Carruthers, and
the drifting Owe’Sullivan, and the drifting
McGowen, that it would be difficult for anyone
except themselves to tell one from the c }er.
Everything in N.S.W. State politics just now is
old— dreadfully old, and mouldy, and moth-
eaten, and worn-out, as one of Lytton’s
characters described England before the Conquest.
Never since there was any N.S.W. was there
more need of some one with a policy—a big man
with a big policy for preference, but even a small
man with a big policy would do. The Labor
leader, if he could get some financial common-
sense and shake off something of his unutterable
sluggishness, would do, but the curse of mental
Fat is on .Tames Sinclair Taylor McGowen.
*•**■*•
There is enough, and more than enough, in the
present situation of N.S.W. to furnish a real
leader with a rousing battle cry. Last financial
year the State had a revenue of £12,283,082—the
biggest revenue by far in all its history. Yet its
debt showed an increase during the year, and as
Carruthers has promised huge remissions of
taxation and many items of increased expendi-
ture, it is likely soon to show a much larger
increase. The State received last year about
£2,300,000 more revenue than it did in 1900, and
as the Commonwealth Government has relieved it
of fully £1,000,000 a year of necessary expenditure
since 1900, it is really about £3,300,000a year better
off. But the expenditure under the management of
the incredible Carruthers has risen up like an
armed man and swallowed all this increase. Never
in all the history of N.S.W. did the expenditure
reach the huge total that Carruthers has
brought it to. In 1900 the State pulled through
with an outlay of £10,729,741, of which about
£1,000,000 was postal, telegraph, and defence
expenditure, leaving the other items at about
£9,730,000. In 1906 the State expenditure was
£11,386,864, and as postal, telegraph, and defence
charges are now defrayed by the Australian
Government, this amount has to be set against
the £9,730,000 of six years ago. An increase
of £1,656,000 in six years is a serious item,
especially when it was a wildly-extravagant
Government with Owe’Sullivan and all his garish
ideas in it that spent the £9,730,000, and it
is a profoundly economical and “ Reform ”
Carruthers Government which spends the
£ll, 386,864. That kind of economy and “lieform”
seems about the most impudent confidence or
three-card or pea-and-thimble trick this side of
Jordan.. The Bulletin has always regarded E.
W. Owe’Sullivan as a fearsome monetary irres-
ponsible, but there may yet come a time when
N.S.W. will have to recall the cash-irresponsible
Owe’Sullivan to prevent the land being ruined
by the cash-maniac Joseph Hector Owe’Neill
Carruthers.
» • • •
Last year, it may be mentioned, N.S.W. had a
surplus—on paper anyhow. The country was
blessed with an excellent season. Many of its
minerals brought record prices. A Federal Par-
liament, which is by no means all it should be
but which is miles ahead of any State Legislature
that N.S.W. ever had, did something by its tariff
to foster manufactures, and by rigid economy was
able not only to return to N.S.W. the full three-
fourths of the tariff revenue to which the State
was entitled, but £371,023 more. So, despite the
record expenditure, N.S.W. had a fine surplus of
£1,200,000 odd—on paper. It was arrived at in
three ways : (1) By spending about £1,000,000 of
the proceeds of land sales—of money derived by
the sale of public assets —as if it were ordinary
revenue ; (2) by spending about £1,300,000 of loan
money; (3) by cutting down the old See and
Waddell sinking fund. The net result was that,
despite the payments nominally made in reduc-
tion of the public debt, and despite about
£1,000,000 derived from the sale of State lands,
and despite the great paper surplus, and despite
the splendid times, N.S.W. was more deeply in
debt at the end of the year than at the beginning.
* * * »
Therefore the questions which The Bulletin
puts are these : Is borrowing for public works to
be the normal condition of N.S.W. ? Is there
never to be any time when the State will leave off
borrowing ? Is there never to be any imaginable
revenue which will enable it to do without
borrowing ? And are there any conceivable
circumstances under which it will begin to reduce
its debt ? The Bulletin asks these questions of
the people and Parliament of N.S.W., and
especially of the fatuous Carruthers and the big
and slothful McGowen. N.S.W. will require
public works for 1000 or more years yet, and if
they are all to be built out of loans, what kind of
prospect is ahead ? It is mere hypocrisy to say
that remunerative works should be paid for out of
loans because our children will get the benefit of
them as well as ourselves, and therefore
our children should bear part of the
cost. Under the loan policy our child-
ren get the works when they are in a
partially worn-out condition, and they are left to
bear all the cost. And, in addition, this genera-
tion is selling the State lands, an asset that can
only be sold once and which belongs as much to
our children as to ourselves ; and it is spending all
the proceeds. This generation gets the land sale
money, and the use of the public works when they
are new and at their best. Future ones will get
no land sale money, will get the public works
when they are partly or wholly worn out, or
obsolescent, or entirely obsolete—and it will get
the bill. Also, it will probably have to pay for
the big war which comes to every country soon
or late, and will have to face it when the country’s
credit has been exhausted by its loafing fathers
in time of peace. The loan policy, even for re-
munerative works, is pure swindle, cowardice and
blackguardism. It is the policy of those who
care not in the least what may befall their
children and their grand-childr.j so long as
things are easy in their time.
* * * *
The public works policy, as practised in
IN.S.W., is a drifting, futile business. In very
good years it sometimes happens that a few works
are paid for out of the proceeds of land sales, and
the rest of the money is borrowed. In ordinary
years nothing is paid for out of the proceeds of land
sales, and all the money for public works is bor-
rowed. Nothing is ever paid for public works
except out of the land sale money (very rarely),
and out of loans (all the time). There is no rule
or law or principle laid down. The Government
is expected to find the money for interest on the
public debt, and for civil service salaries and
other things, out of revenue, whether times
are good or bad. If the revenue is insufficient
it is expected to find more revenue somehow.
In these matters there is a recognised principle.
As regards public works there is no principle
at all, and so much has the loan disease demoral-
ised the community that even anything like a
definite public opinion is wanting. Everything
depends on the Government of the moment. It
may charge renewals, repairs, patches, and even
replacements of broken crockery to loans, as the
drhadful George Reid did, and no statute pro-
hibits the fraud. So much is the borrowing curse
recognised as part of the system of State affairs
that last financial year, despite his surplus,
Joseph Carruthers borrowed all the money for
public works. Out of the revenue surplus (created
by land sales) some of the floating debt was paid
off, but it was evidently felt that to pay for
works directly out of the revenue would be
to create a disastrous precedent. It would be
an attack on the loan-joss. It might raise an
impression that public works might be built with-
out loans. This is so serious a matter that
Carruthers is already taking active steps to
ensure a more rapid increase of the public debt
for the future. He has handed over the proceeds
of the land-tax to local bodies. He has also pro-
mised them vast endowments. He promises to
subsidise benefit societies, to abolish the bulk of
the income-tax, to provide pensions for invalids
arid cripples, to abolish some stamp duties, to
greatly reduce railway rates, and otherwise to
disencumber himself of the assets which almost
threatened to make loans superfluous. Plainly,
Joseph Carruthers won’t be dragged away
from his loan if he can help it.
■# * m *
It is proposed to take a referendum at the
approaching State election on some futile ques-
tion about the Federal capital, and another
re the further reduction of the State legis-
lature. It is a pity that there should not be a
third one on a larger issue. The people might
be asked to state whether they consider that
there should be any reduction of taxation while
the State is, apparently, in such a beggarly
condition that it must borrow constantly, and
constantly use the proceeds of land sales as
revenue, to keep itself afloat; whether they pro-
pose that borrowing should go on for ever—or at
least till the State bursts—and, if not, when they
think it should stop $ and whether they consider
that this country has any ghost of a show to raise
the huge sums which will be necessary when war
comes this way, seeing that its futile people have
practically exhausted its credit in time of peace.
There are a few more questions which might be
put, but these three would do to begin with.
Unfortunately, it is difficult to imagine who will
propose the putting of such questions. In such a
matter the lazy, somnolent, mentally-heavy,
mentally-dragging MoGowen is just as hopeless
cis Carruthers himself. The Labor party will
doubtless drift away sufficiently from its oainful
resemblance to Carruthers to lodge a pro-
test against the proposed abolition of the
income-tax, as an objectionable attempt to relieve
the rich from their share of the public burdens ;
but in that matter it has already wasted its oppor-
tunity, and it is a back number. It is a borrow-
ing party, and a party that looks kindly on
deficits, like all the rest—there are a few bright
exceptions, of which Dacey is the most con-
spicuous, but in the main the description is
accurate. If N.S.W. is to raise loans for its
public works as a matter of habit and
principle, then, in the present condition
of its finances, the income-tax disappears quite
naturally and as a matter of course. The only
party which can complain much about its disap-
pearance is one which demands that the pro-
ceeds of the tax shall be used for public works
purposes instead of loans, and the Labor party,
even when MoGowen was awake, has never done
that. And MoGowen seems to be so very seldom
awake, and even when he is wide awake he isn’t
nearly so much awake as a man like Kingston
was when he was asleep.
SHOP SLAVES.
More than 60 years ago Thomas Hood wrote
“ The Song of the Shirt,” and that eloquent cry of
misery stirred the great heart of England. It
was such a piercing lamentation, and such a
pathetically true picture, that the great heart of
England stirs by reason of it to this day, and
every fresh generation sheds new tears over the
slave of skin and bone whom Hood depicted.
Yet for want of State interference, and for lack
of an Arbitration Court such as N.S.W. possesses,
or a Wages Board like that in Victoria, the only
difference is that the London seamstress now gets
about or Id. per day less than she did then.
The great, warm, ramshackle heart of England
doesn’t count for a straw while it only moves by
a process of tumultuous and spasmodic public
opinion, and while it doesn’t put its emotions on
the statute book. The Tory person knows this,
and outof thatknowledgehespeaksbitterlyagainst
“the tendency to over-legislation,’’and holds forth
eloquently about the advisability of “ trusting to
the silent irresistible force of public opinion.” He
understands perfectly well that the force of public
opinion will never do him much permanent harm,
or get in the way of any of his profitable oppres-
sions for more than a day or so. Except when
public opinion goes round armed with a rope,
after the fashion of Georgia or Mississippi, it
never abolished a slum, cleaned up an offensive
back-yard, restrained a seller of tuberculosis cow,
or raised the wages of a singer of “The Song of
the Shirt ”by one halfpenny per month; and
when public opinion goes round with a rope it
ceases to be opinion and becomes law—unwritten
law, Lynch law, but.still law. For thirty years
the public opinion of all the United Kingdom has
been struggling to keep the survivors of the 607
who charged at Balaclava out of the poor-house
and for want of a simple Old Age and Infirmity
Pensions Act it has failed. It always does.
* * * *
A little while ago, some of the miserably ill-
paid drapery assistants of Sydney grew tired of
waiting for the pressure of public opinion and the
warm impulses of the national soul to help them
and went to the N.S.W. Arbitration Court!
These slaves represent Commerce. They are the
rank and file of the great Importing Industry, and
the army of Calico Jimmyism. They serve in a
trade which grinds out some of the biggest fortunes
in Australia a trade in which, even when there
is a cheap sale on, the profits run to 50 per cent,
on quite a multitude of lines. And their pitiable
story of genteel poverty, of decently-clothed desti-
tution, of endless struggles, of lives without a
gleam or semblance of hope on the farthest
horizon, show that the Shirt is the same old curse
both to maker and to seller. Here is some of the
evidence condensed:—
Clerk in universal providery: Wages, 30s. a week
Has wife and five children. Pays 10s. a week rent and Is!
a week tram-fares, which leaves 19s. to support seven
persons. Occasionally receives charitable aid. Evidently
needs it badly.
Woman at same place: Has been 11 years in the firm.
Is cashier at 15s. a week. Got a whole £1 as a present
last Christmas. Lives in a room by herself and prepares
her own food. Sort of hermit.
Women in mantle department: Served a year at ss. a
week ; five years at 12s. 6d. a week ; about five years at 17s.
6d. ; and is nowin receipt of the garish remuneration of
on J?. w^°^ e il Qnid ” with about 3s. 3d. premiums on sales.
Clerk to same firm, in charge of a department, with 13
subordinates to look after: Gets 455. a week.
Saleswoman at same providery: Has been nine years
without a rise. Pay £1 a week.
Saleswoman in another big providery : Is getting 12s.
6d. a week after being 4J years in the position.
Woman in charge of a large department at same big
emporium : About 255. 6d. a week after 51 years with the
firm.
Shopwalker (the kingly apparition in the frock coat
who bobs to you when you go in): Receives 325. 6d. a
week after four years shopwalking.
ri ! i ,' er< rV'T re very niany more witnesses, but they
all told the same yarns of polite and tidy misery.
The only reason why The Bulletin doesn’t give
the names of the firms which paid these wretched
wages is that they are described as about the best
ancl the most liberal in the Sydney trade. It is to
be hoped that the names of the worst and least
liberal will be available for publication next week.
* ■* *
. The wealthy owners of the great rag emporiums
m which these wages are paid rank among the
least useful members of the community. Sydney,
with its craze for imports and its preference for
commerce rather than manufactures, has a huge
respect for the great overgrown shopkeeper. His
early struggles, and the way he “rose,” and how
he built up his sky-towering emporium till his
topmost sign can be seen almost halfway to
Maoriland, are much dilated upon. But the man
who creates a great new emporium for buying and
selling doesn’t add a penny to the country’s wealth
or productiveness. He doesn’t even increase the
amount of buying and selling that there is to be
done. He is simply a successful cannibal who
has made his big emporium by eating up smaller
ones. And this cannibalism has become a fine
art. To quote from the evidence of one witness
The protits of the clothing and mantles, if bought from
a warehouse, were between 45 per cent, and 50 per cent,
while if the goods were indented by the firm 50 per cent!
or 60 per cent, might be made. There was no chance for
a man working in a shop to save enough to start business
for himself. Witness explained at some length the way
in which the big houses were able to crush the small
shops by means of their large capital. A small man buy.
ing in Sydney would have to pay 20 per cent, more than
a firm indenting. The big houses could sell one line at
cost price, and another at a huge profit to make up the
loss on the cheap line. This cheap line would be one
handled by a small firm they wished to crush, while the
line they sold at the higher price would be one the
smaller firm could not secure.
The number of small shops is said to have greatly
decreased in Sydney of late years, they being
eaten by the big cannibal with the sky-high ware-
house. And as the chances of the small shop
become less and less hopeful, the chances of the
employee who wants to start for himself vanish
away. He degenerates into a polite slave with a
life job at 30s. a week or so. The fact that
he is. fixed for life helps to keep his wages
down. Then they are also kept down by
the struggle between the great commercial
eater and the small commercial eaten; the big
man sweats his employees because he wants lots of
money to put another 10 storeys on his warehouse,
and the small man does the same because, by reason
of the big man, he expects the bailiffs in next
week. Then wages are miserable because the
fiction of “genteel employment”—the unsoiled
hands and the black coat and so on—makes
drapery slaves plentiful. They are low also
because there was never a revolt of drapers’
assistants. They haven’t the muscle. Iron-
workers, miners, navvies, farmers, peasants,
sailors, have made a Jacquerie before now, but
never the drapery men. And they have only
begun to learn trades-unionism. The fact that
they have begun to learn that much is something.
They have grown weary—especially the woman
who gets 15s. a week after 11 years’ toil, and lives
in a room by herself—of waiting for the boss
drapers to do something for them, and have begun
to shift for themselves. The writer of this, by
the way, knows of a drapery girl in a Sydney
suburb who got |d. extra after nine hours’over-
time. She is also weary of waiting.
* • * •
The Socialist party demands, as one of the items
of its programme, the nationalisation of the means
of distribution and exchange, and when super-
cilious bagmen hear about this they scoff. Yet,
taking the draper as a single example, there is a
staggering amount of logic in the Socialists’ case.
First, they say, there are far too many drapers
living on the game. There is the great, successful
cannibal draper, who is eating up his rivals.
There is the smaller and non-cannibal draper, who
will be eaten by-and-bye. There is the partially-
eaten draper, whose remains are still hanging out
of the great cannibal draper’s mouth. Also, there
is the newly-eaten but not wholly-digested draper.
If the business was run simply to supply the
public with the drapery it required, half the
drapery emporiums, with their rents, taxes,
gas and repairs, their quarrels and struggles,
their miscellaneous expenses, their big fortunes
and big insolvencies, their buyers, bagmen,
bosses, and sundries, could be dispensed with.
Then the drapery business, which now often
shows a gross profit of 50 or more per cent, despite
all this confusion and clamor, would probably,
with the superfluous burdens eliminated, show 60
per cent. And if the business was run by the State
—the same thrifty institution which manages to
collect the Customs and Excise at the amazingly
low rate of under 3 per cent, of the takings—and
the State was content with 10 p.er cent, on its
capital, it might easily be possible to increase
wages at least one-half, and yet sell drapery 20
per cent, cheaper than at present. The big
wealthy draper would disappear, but the Socialists
say frankly that they don’t care if he does.
Landlords, left with many empty drapery shops
on their hands, would also suffer, but only
for a time; the increased prosperity and
increased population brought about by better
wages and cheap drapery would create some other
demand for their premises. Newspapers would
suffer through the loss of the profitable drapery
advt., and their trouble would probably be per-
manent. And there would be other changes. But
Socialism contends that the vast benefit accruing
to the multitude of the people would be worth
this many times over. It is out after the greatest
good of the greatest number—the same as
Christianity.
* * • •
The Bulletin often wonders if the universal
provider realises how fast he is manufacturing
Socialists—or how fast he will manufacture them
just so soon as his employees realise what Socialism
means. When every shopman had a chance, or
a hope, of starting for himself in a small way arid
growing larger in time, Socialism didn’t ap-
peal to him. Now universal providing and
low wages fast bring along the day when
it will take hard economy, celibacy, and the
savings of two centuries to give the shopman
a chance of beginning business hopefully for him-
self, and few shopmen live so long as two
centuries. When the salesman fully realises that
he will be under a boss all his life, and that the
theory of Socialism is to provide him with a boss
who will be content with much smaller profits,
and will pay much better wages, and who, being
the State, will at least be partly the shopman him-
self, he will turn Socialist. It is part of the
natural evolution of things. The three stages
are: (1) The small shop; (2) the universal pro-
videry; (3) Sosh.
JOSEPH SAYS “IT WAS NEVER IN-
TENDED.”
It is difficult to tell when N.S.W. State Premier
Carrcthers reaches his lowest depth of political
meanness in his policy of grumbling and snarling
at the Australian Government, and in his inven-
tion of sham gi’ie vances and bogus “wrongs.” There
are dark deep cellars in Joseph’s soul, and it is
hard to tell when the subterranean Joe has got so
far down that there isn’t another depth to which
he can attain. But this extract from his “policy”
speech at Kogarah, last week, indicates that
Joseph is down fairly deep just now :
Another part of the Government’s policy was
tha.t it intended to ask the Parliament and the country to
assist the Government in usinq all constitutional means
to force the Federal Government to continue to return
to the State three-fourths of the Customs revenue for
State Government purposes. (Cheers.) That was the


jaw to-day, but it would expire in 1910. It was never in-
tended, when New South Wales entered into Federation,
to hand over to the Federal authorities the whole of the
Customs duties, and that they should get control of the
whole of those duties.... The Government made it part
of its policy to urge the Federal Parliament to have a
CONTINUANCE OF THE BRADDON CLAUSE OR ITS EQUIVALENT IN
a new Constitution. (Cheers.)
■it * * *
“It was never intended when N.S.W. entered
the Federation,” says Joseph. One would almost
imagine, from the constant repetition of remarks
like that, that no other State entered the Federa-
tion ; or that the other States dragged N.S.W.
into the Union, instead of N.S.W. asking them
to come in. But leaving that aspect of the case
out of the question, the main fact is this : The
original Constitution—the one which the people
of N.S.W. accepted by 71,000 votes against
65.000, but as regards which the N.S.W. Par-
liament annulled the popular decision—differed
from the present Constitution in two great essen-
tials. It left the question of the capital quite
open, and it provided, under the Braddon clause,
THAT, FOB, ALL TIME, THREE-FOURTHS
OF THE CUSTOMS AND EXCISE BE-
VENUE SHOULD BE RETURNED TO THE
STATES, OR SHOULD BE USED BY THE
COMMONWEALTH TO PAY INTEREST
ON THE STATE DEBTS. But at that time
Carruthers and Reid were in office in N.S.W.
And the CARRUTHERS-and-BEiD Government de-
clared against this proviso with unexampled fury,
and demanded that it should be utterly abolished.
Carruthers’ leader, George Reid, stumped
N.S.W. in a frenzy of vituperation, and urged
the people to reject the measure because of the
Braddon clause. The Parliament led by Car-
ruthers and Reid had already done its best to
annul in advance any popular decision in favor of
the Constitution with the Braddon clause
in it, by raising the minimum vote required
for the acceptance of the measure from the
56,000 which had been agreed upon in the
bargain made with the other States, to 80,000.
But the Carruthers-Reid Government seemed
to greatly fear that the 80,000 vote might be forth-
coming after all, and it implored the people to
reject the Constitution because the proviso THAT
THREE-FOURTHS OF THE CUSTOMS AND
EXCISE REVENUE SHOULD BE PER-
MANENTLY RETURNED TO THE STATES
was utterly foolish and ruinous. And it had its
way. and the 80,000 minimum vote was not
reached.
Then G-eorge Reid went to the Premiers’
Conference to bully the other States into abolish-
ing the clause which provided that three-fourths
of the Customs and Excise revenue should be per-
manently returned to the States. He, as repre-
senting the Reid-Carruthers Government, made
it one of the conditions on which N.S.W. would
join the Commonwealth that the very thing Car-
ruthers now denounces should be done, and that
the very thing he now approves should be undone.
In these days, Reid and Oarruthers didn’t call
this proviso a clause providing for the return of
three-fourths of the tariff revenue to the States ;
they turned it the other way up and called it a
Blot which compelled the Commonwealth, when-
ever it wanted £l, to oppress the people by raising
£4, and out of that £4 to give the States £3 which
they didn’t require, and which would lead them
into extravagance. Reid, of the Reid-Carruthers
Government, was so far successful that he bullied
the other States into limiting the Braddon clause
tolOyears, after which time the Commonwealth, ac-
cording to the arrangement which Reid fixed up,
could keep all the Customs and Excise revenue.
Reid returned from that conference with great
pride and bingie, and amid the applause of the
Sydney morning papers, and (aided by Car-
ruthers) told the people that this was so vast
an improvement that the Bill might now be
accepted, and on his assurance, backed by Carru-
THERS, it was accepted.
* * * *
And out of this amendment, which the N.S.
Wales Government of Reid and Carruthers
harried the other States into accepting, and which
they made one of the conditions of N.S.W.’s.
adhesion to the Commonwealth, Carruthers
now makes a new Federal grievance. He asks
the people of N.S.W. to “force” the Australian
Government to put back permanently into the
Constitution the very clause or Blot which, accord-
ing to the old Reid-Carruthers Government,
made the Constitution intolerable. He adopts
the same hectoring tone about getting the clause
restored that his Government adopted a few
years ago about getting it abolished. Instead of
stating that he and his old political partners
made a grievous error when they demanded
that the Braddon clause should be obli-
terated, and asking the Commonwealth to
undo the work of those two dreadful in-
capables, Reid and Carruthers, he holds
forth blatantly as if their work had been
somebody else’s error, or somebody else’s con-
spiracy against the Ma State. He doesn’t
apologise to the other States for helping to force
the present allegedly-disastrous arrangement upon
them. Rather, he howls as if they had forced it on
him. He also says that the very thing the old Reid-
Carruthers Government demanded before the
first referendum “was never intended.” And he
hasn’t even the manliness to say a kind word for
poor old Edward Braddon in his grave, though
the case certainly calls for something of the kind.
The proviso over which the. Reid-Carruthers
Ministry and the Sydney morning papers which
barracked for it, used such language, and which
they got removed out of the Constitution (except
as a mere temporary expedient to lapse in 1910)
was what they then called the Braddon Blot, and
the coarse vituperation which Reid, Carruthers’
at-that-time chief, heaped on old man Braddon,
by reason of the Blot, was a memorable sample
of political Billingsgate. Braddon went down
to his grave with that Billingsgate still ringing in
his ears. Now Carruthers almost threatens
secession if the Blot isn’t fully restored to him at
once, and Braddon, unfortunately, is too dead to
say tohim the things that his case deserves.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Queensland’s Election: A Last Word.
On Saturday, Queensland has to decide whether
it will return Premier Kidston or go back to
Philp and the old bankruptcy of the Continuous
Ministry. Queensland is so vast, and the present
issue of The Bulletin will have wandered over
so small a section of it by election day, that it is
hardly worth while filling a great amount of space
with impassioned appeals to the State not to be-
have next Saturday like one with new rum-leaves
in his hair, and not to be a gratuitous and unpaid
fool, and not to commit suicide without remunera-
tion. So that little portion of the State which
this paper will reach by Saturday is briefly
exhorted not to be more of an ass than it can
help. Robert Philp, on his record, represents
political suicide to an extent that no other Premier
or ex-Premier or party leader in Australia does.
There is nothing against Philp’s political charac-
ter except that he hasn’t one quality which makes
a statesman. There is nothing against his political
history except that he never did the right thing by
any accident. His huge and incredible deficits;
his immense taxes, which never seemed to produce
any result in the way of keeping his accounts
square ; his huge borrowings, which were mostly
frittered away round Townsville and did mighty
little good even to Townsville ; his cutting-down
of salaries, which also never seemed to bring his
accounts any nearer solvency ; his unbounded
capacity for bringing the State to the verge of
bankruptcy, even in the best of years and with
the biggest of revenues ; his utter inability to
understand that there could be anything objec-
tionable about spending half-a-million more than
his income ; his habit of letting everything drift,
and the easy-going way in which he lent his good-
natured ear to every political bounder and axe-
grinder—-these are the things by which Philp lives
in the minds of those who take the trouble to read
up his political history. And his future, if he
should get back to power, promises to be even
wilder than his past. Roughly speaking, he
seems to be in favor of knocking something like
£500,000 a year off the revenue by abolishing taxes,
and adding about £500,000 a year to the expendi-
ture by giving everybody anything he likes to ask
for. If Philp comes back to power and lives up
to his programme, the year 1910 should see
Queensland’s credit stopped, and its securities
selling in London by the pound weight, and the
world’s financiers holding a commission de lunatico
inquirendo over a community with a wild look in
its eye and straws in its hair. For it must be
remembered that Queensland has already the
Biggest Debt per head on Earth, bar that of S. A.,
and as long as Philp was in office he kept
it ahead of S. A. Its credit is already balanced
on a razor edge, and it only requires
another year or two of the old Philp
finance, or the promised new Philp finance, to
send it over the precipice. New South Wales or
Victoria might go on another financial razzle of
the old type, and pull round ; one-tenth of the
razzle that would only give them a bad head in
the morning would make Queensland insolvent.
A State like "V ictoria, with about £45 per head of
debt, and a record of 13 years of careful financing,
is one thing; one like Queensland, which has
nearly £BO per head of debt and which only left off
a fearful financial “jag ” three years ago, is some-
thing quite different. And if it goes straight back
to the worst financial “ jagger” it ever possessed,
it will be something exceedingly different.
Round Philp are gathered all the fine old public
hogs who used to have their snouts and their
front feet in the Treasury trough in the old Philp
days—the hogs that Morgan, and Browne, and
Kidston drove away with contumely. They are
wealthy and influential hogs. They own papers,
and they manufacture public opinion. The trough
was very empty when Philp ceased to be in
charge of it in 1903, so they didn’t very much
mind being driven away, and some of them even
spoke violently about Philp because there was
nothing left in the trough save the bottom and
sides. The same receptacle is pretty full now,
though Philp didn’t fill it, and the old drove of
hogs is desperately anxious to get back to it, and
it has recovered its high opinion of Philp as the
proper man to be in charge of the swill. It was
only the lack of swill that made the drove’s
opinion about Robert of Townsville drop so
heavily four years ago. But it is Queensland’s
business to show that its opinion of the hogs
hasn’t risen, whatever has happened to the hogs’
opinion of Philp.
Nakamura and the “ Hide ” of Japan.
When recently interviewed in Wellington (M.L.)
Jap. journalist Nakamura said that he had been
round most of the White Man’s world, making
investigations and taking notes. Japan/ he said,
was anxious that all countries should admit its
subjects on terms of absolute equality, and if this
concession were not granted, there would be con-
siderable likelihood of something getting broken
in the scrimmage that was sure to eventuate.
Japan has no alternative, according to Nakamura.
Having no empty territory itself, it must find an
outlet for its surplus population; and if other
nations will not admit the Japs on terms of
equality, then territory must be found for them
by other means. This is what is known in the
vulgar tongue as “ the straight griffin.” Naka-
mura said nothing about Japan admitting white
people on terms of perfect equality. At present
the only thing a white man may do in Japan is
spend money. Property of all sorts he is barred
from owning, and in the land of the Mikado there
is a fixed determination to keep the country for
the subjects of the Mikado, and there is also
a strong determination that all subjects of the
Mikado shall have Japanese parents. With regard
to the alleged surplus population, that is quite a
new discovery, and will not stand looking into.
Millard, who spent a lot of time round the
Yellow Sea littoral, and went into the figures of
the Japanese population, reports in February,
1906, that, according to the latest statistics com-
piled by the Department of Agriculture and
Commerce, the present total area of cultivated
fields in Japan forms only 13g per cent, of its entire
surface. (The land cultivated in Europe covers
between one-third and one-half the total land
area.) This means that, of Japan’s cultivable
area, only about 48 per cent, is cultivated, and the
remaining 52 per cent, is lying idle. Therefore
Japan is capable of supporting much more than
its present population, even if it works its avail-
able land on the same comic-opera lines as at
present. But the land thatisatpresenteultivated
does not produce anything like what it is really
capable of. The “fields” are tiny enclosures,
more like children’s gardens than anything else on
earth, and, being worked by antiquated and
wasteful methods, they yield only an annual
value of 20 yen per head of the population. A
yen is equal to about 25., so the amazing Jap
manages to get only equal to £2 per head of his
population out of his land at present. Roughly,
it takes one person to cultivate one acre—in
Japan. The total area of the country, excluding
Formosa and the Pescadores, is 147,655 square
miles, and the population of 46,732,138 works out
at an average of 317 to the square mile. Belgium
carries an average of 622 ; Holland. 436 ; Italy,
303 ; England, 558 ; Saxony, 727 ; Massachusetts
(U.S.A.), 349 ; Rhode Island (U.S.A.), 407. The
most crowded portion of Japan is Western Hon-
shiu, which is staggering under the weight of 503
people to the square mile; but in Hokkaido, which
is 36,299 miles in area, there are only 23 people on
each mile. There is room for a lot of colonisation
in the great Jap province of Hokkaido. Com-
pare this with the figures of Holland and Bel-
gium, which have no surplus population.
South Holland carries 1083 people to the
square mile, North Holland 984; in Belgium
Brabant has 996, East Flanders 891, Hainault
795, and Anvers 749. Nakamura’s tale about
the surplus population will not stand investi-
gation, for it must be remembered that the
Japanese figures are further reduced by th e
island of Formosa, which has an area of 13,458
square miles, and the south end of the island of
Saghalien, which gives another 12,000 square
miles at least. Also, the Jap has taken Korea
and Southern Manchuria. The trouble is that
the Jap wants a tremendous lot for nothing, and
the present appearances point to the fact that
either the British empire or the United States
will have the unpleasant task of convincing him
that the white races do not do business on those
lines. If the job falls to the British empire, Aus-
tralia’s position will be far from pleasant.
The Decay of Tasmania.
Tasmania is a small fcstate, and old and backward
in its methods, and, as might naturally be ex-
pected under these conditions, it has the land
trouble in an aggravated form. There are only
about people to the square mile in Tasmania, and
yet from that fertile, well-watered isle, eminently
fitted to carry a dense population, the exodus of
young men, driven out by the impossibility of
finding land for settlement, started definitely 22
years ago, and has been almost continuous ever
since. The year 1885, by the way, was not abso-
lutely the beginning of the exodus—the outward
movement appeared spasmodically in many pre-
vious times, and even GO years ago there were
periods when the population seemed to have
decided that misgoverned Tassy was a good place
to get away from; but 1885 marked the be-
ginning of an exodus which has become almost
a permanent feature. And because it is the
best and sturdiest and most energetic of
tho population which has the energy to
gather up its traps and strike out for a better
land, and the poorest in mind and body who are
content to remain under unfavorable conditions,
and be the fathers and mothers of the new genera-
tion, it comes to pass that some elderly men, who
can compare the Tasmanian of to-day with the
one of years ago, declare that they trace a con-
spicuous deterioration, mental and physical, in the
people. They are becoming, by reason of dread-
ful old men like William Dodery, ex-M.L.C.,
and dreadful land-monopolists like the V.D.L. Co.,
a residuum, and the sons and daughters of a
residuum, just as the Victorians will be in a few
more years if their great estates are not broken
up, and the outflow of their best citizens in search
of land is not checked. In a faint sort of way
Jack Evans, State Premier of Tasmania—the
terribly ordinary and pottering Evans —has
begun to have glimpses of the evil. He
hasn’t the courage to break up the big estates,
and keep them broken up, with a graduated land
tax. Nor has he the courage to put a limit on
the amount of land which a new proprietor may
acquire, so as to at least prevent the great estate
disease becoming any worse than it is. But he
does a little at cutting up State lands, and de-
posits farmers on them, and calls this process
land settlement.” And while he is doing this,
the big land-grabber is buying out and evicting
small farmers and converting their holdings into
big estates and great sheepwalks at just about a
sufficient pace to counteract all Jack Evans’ faint
little efforts. Here it may be appropriate to
quote the wail of J. W. Cheek, the unusually
liberal and progressive person (as such terms go
in Tasmania) who has just been elected for
Hodery’s old Upper House constituency :
Very few people have grasped how very much of the
land of the State is being absorbed in large, unwieldy
estates of 50,000 acres and over. It is estimated that
more than half the alienated land in Tasmania is held by
300 persons or corporations. The trouble is really getting
more acute every year. For instance, 173,000 acres of
Crown lands were taken up last year. That should mean
at least 700 more land-owners, yet the returns issued
show there is only an increase of 15 persons owning' land
this year. The explanation is that some hundreds of
small farms have been absorbed in large estates. It is a
matter for discussion as to whether the amount of land
owned by any one person should not be limited by law.
This, however, should not apply to land already acquired,
but no harm will be done to any individual if owners of, say
£20,000 value of land are prevented from acquiring more.
No man holding such large areas of land can possibly put
such to its best use.
This brings Tasmania up against a conundrum.
If it cuts up, say, 173,000 acres of State lands in
a year among small settlers, and at the same time
small settlers are shoved oif, say, 150,000 acres of
land so that that area may be added to the great
estates, what will happen token the cultivable State
lands arc all disposed of ! For then, if the futije
Jack Evans policy endures, the settlement of
small farmers will practically cease, while the
absorption of small holdings by big land-grabbers
will continue. Then the exodus of the best class of
the population will go on faster than ever, and the
process of mental and physical degeneration will go
on faster, and the names of William Dodery and
Jack Evans and people of their class will become
more and more an execration. But Evans —poor
fleeting Evans —has at least an idea. He is
hunting for a curious class of immigrants. The
great inspiration that has occurred to Evans is
that old civil and military Anglo-Indians, who
are retiring from their labors in the land of curry
and niggers, and who fear to face the English
climate again, should be induced to settle in
Tassy. While the young and sturdy and enter-
prising go forth to hustle in Queensland under
better conditions, Evans proposes to replace
them by an influx of elderly, broken-down, bad-
tempered gentlemen with huge livers—many of
them widowers, most of them either childless or
men whose children have grown up and
are making their way elsewhere men
who are too old to add to Tasmania’s
rising population, or too feeble to do much
for the country, even if one here and there should
become the father of a rickety Benjamin in the
sunset of his life. Evans, in short, is advertising
for invalids, valetudinarians, superannuated
nigger-drivers, and the like. And meanwhile
young male Tasmania, driven out by the land
famine, still packs its bag and takes its ticket for
the mainland. And young female Tasmania
stays behind and becomes an old maid to a degree
unknown in any other of the Australasian States.
And meanwhile the news comes to hand that the
Hon. A. Hean, Minister for Lands and Works,
won a twopenny prize, the other day, for guessing
the weight of a fat cow at St. Mary’s, which
event was strikingly suggestive of the ways of
Tasmania’s Ministry when there is a great work
waiting to be done.
Concerning a Eeferendum.
One really good idea sticks out of the Cak-
RUTHERS programme—that of taking a popular
vote at the coming State election on a proposal to
reduce the N.S.W. Assembly to a body of 60
members. The pity is that the proposal doesn’t
include the abolition of that gathering of useless
nominees, the Upper House. One House of 60
members is quite sufficient in these days, when
State Legislatures are mere glorified municipal
councils ; and the reduction of the State Legisla-
tures should be merely a prelude to their complete
abolition and to the Unification of Australia, with
one great Australian Parliament to attend to
large national matters, and an extensive system
of Local Government to potter about small details.
Meanwhile, as a step towards abolition, the State
Legislatures should certainly be reduced, and the
money saved by the abolition of 30 members
might reasonably be applied to increasing the
pay of the remaining 60 in the hope that better
wages may bring along better men. The last
referendum taken on the subject in N.S.W. found
the people enthusiastic for reduction, but by an
impudent fake, of which the See Government
and the Labor party shared the responsibility,
they were not permitted to vote for any smaller
number than 90 members. They voted for the 90
with an energy which plainly hinted their great
willingness to vote for a still lower number if the
chance had presented itself. Exactly howmuch
energy they would display in voting for the aboli-
tion of the Upper House it is difficult to say, but
The Bulletin is convinced it would be some-
thing great. Will Carruthers make the experi-
ment? The experience of New Brunswick,
Ontario, Manitoba, British Columbia, Prince
Edward Island, Alberta and Saskatchewan (seven
Canadian provinces out of nine) which all get
along with only an Assembly, proves that in a
mere State Legislature an Upper House is just
as much an atom of needless frill as would be an
Upper House in a municipal council. The only
excuse for the retention of a thing that is need-
less and useless is that it is ornamental,
and no State Upper House in Australia has
even a remote claim to be ornamental. State
Premier Carruthers has, so far, left no footprint
on the sands of history that the next tide won’t
washout. If he abolished the pathetically useless
State Upper House, he would at least rescue him-
self from oblivion. The man who first broke
The Lay of the Last Digger-Hunt.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the Past we build the Present—
Yea, the Future, iridescent,
Is the profit fat To-morrow draws from jaded
lean To-day:
They who bore Eureka’s burden
Left the gain and all the guerdon
To the men who steer Australia towards the com-
ing battle-fray !
From those rebels we inherit
Might of arm and strength of spirit—
Ha! What grim, courageous fathers we possessed
in days of yore !
Rest ye well, ye sleeping Titans,
For the flame ye kindled brightens—
We shall raise once more the banner ye unfurled
in ’Fifty-four.
Sacred day of dim November—
Let Australians all remember
How the starry cross was lifted half-a-century
ago!
How the driving of the diggers
Set the fingers to the triggers—
How our fathers died defiant with their faces to
the foe!
Stormy days of red rebellion,
When our star in perihelion
Blazed at last in all the splendor of thy light, O
Liberty !
Final day of digger-driving—-
’Midst the clamor and the striving,
Let Australians heed its lesson, lest they taste
adversity !
Flash of scabbard, glint of sabre —
Hear the cry of hunted Labor :
“ ’Ware the traps ! ” “ The Joes are coming !”
hear the jingling spurs and bits ;
Skirmishers in front advancing—
Jacks-in-office proudly prancing—
O’er the flat the troopers gallop towards the
swarming Gravel Pits.
Speed the clay-bespattered diggers
From their claims, like hunted niggers—
Tubs and cradles are neglected. Myriad voices
throb and thrill !
Down the Main Road rolls the battle—
Stones are flying, gunshots rattle —
Lo ! the starry Flag of Freedom flutters forth on
Bakery Hill !
Round that Flag all humbly kneeling,
Pray’rs to God they lift, revealing
Strength to stand against oppressors—strength to
suffer and to die ;
Gathered there in grim alliance,
Hear their vows of stern defiance—
Vows to stand like men together, lifting Freedom’s
standard high !
O that we, all kneeling lowly
Round that banner pure and holy,
Might to God lift supplication, pledging heart and
pledging hand;
O that we, in place of tailors.
Might produce a thousand Laloks —
O that we might swear allegiance like that
vanished digger-band!
Hear the anvils faintly ringing—
Hear the digger-cohorts singing,
Bivouacked upon the hillside in the brave old
days of yore ;
Hear the midnight voices thrilling—
’Tis the sound of diggers drilling—
’Tis the voice of Freedom calling as it called
in ’54 !
Shall the years be great in number
Ere Australians, roused from slumber,
Seize their rifles as they seized them in the hours
of early dawn;
Shall their fingers, like the diggers’,
Press too late upon the triggers
As they hold their White Australia ’gainst grim
Asia’s yellow spawn ?
Came the red-coats, softly creeping,
When the digger folk were sleeping—
’Twas the bayonet-blade that stirred them from
their injudicious rest;
They were rushed without a warning
In the dim, grey hours of morning—
’Tis the side which rises early that survives the
battle test !
Must we learn once more in sorrow
That To-day decides To-morrow—
Must we write a fresh Eureka on the page of
history ?
Might of arm and strength of spirit
From our fathers we inherit;
But we’ll need to rise at daylight if we'd keep Aus-
tralia Free /

Decay of the House of Dodery.
A letter from Doderyland :
The House of Dodery, in Tasmania, has had another
shingle knocked off it. The removal of the roof began a
c<*uple of years ago, when semi-millionaire William
u RT j ed ’ and Adyk Douglas, aged 90, was dis-
charged. Then Peter M’Cracken, the working man’s
friend, and Charlie Russen, from the bakehouse
in Welhngton-street, were sent to the House of
the little Fat Man to say that the world was
going to have a change of rulers, and let the horny-
handed have a show at hammering out laws and
things. Last week there were three more elee-
tions for the Council. One man, from New Norfolk, was
allowed a walk-over, but the Tasmanian madhouse is at
New Norfolk, so Dean’s return wasn’t wondered at.
Dodery himself, who had sat in the House for 40 years,
! een ,k° ss chairman for three or four, retired rather
than be kicked out, and a chap who writes about spuds and
cottages under the name of “Farmer Will,” and whose
real name is Cheek, offered to take Dodkry’s place, and
lend a hand to McCracken and Co. to make things
hot in the Council. “Where’ll ye sit if ye get in?"
said an Irishman to him at one of his meetings. “ The
nearest answer I can give,” said Farmer Cheek, “is
that I’ll be close to McCracken, Russen, and Morrisby,
wherever they are.” “ I’m satisfied ; yehavemeblessin’,’’
remarked Patrick. In 40 years, Dodery had been opposed
only once not within living memory—and then the man
who opposed him lost his deposit. But Dodery thought
he could assign his seat to his nephew ; so out came
William Hartnoll, a bigger Tory (if possible) than
Dodery himself, to claim the vacancy. Also, a milder type
of Tory, one Gibson, a land-owner, appeared. The triangular
tw wound up at a place called Blessington, and there
J. W. Chee x got his majority and Blessington’s blessing.
Now, the Vandemonian House of Landlords has in it a
man who digs his own ground, and sells ploughs and
harrows—more power to him. The third seat
vacant was for Tamar, a district, like Westmoreland,
just outside Launceston. Here, old Adye Douglas’s step-
son had misrepresented the people for years. A whole
stretch of country had never elected this man at all. A
member of the House of Dodery, with the suitable name of
Rooke, died a few years ago, and then “Parliament”cuthis
constituency into halves, and gave one to Dodery and the
other to Collins ! So, last week, Collins went into that
part of the country for the first time, and the people had
never seen their member rill then, and he told them his
name was Collins, and that, though they mightn’t think
so he represented them in Parliament; and he wanted
to keep the billet. Well, this raised the ire of a village
butcher at George Town, named Brown, who swore
a great oath that he’d crush Collins, and go
into the House of Landlords himself, and not
allow them to chop and slaughter progressive
measures any more. So he uprose, and went round the
country and talked Radical politics to the farmers, and,
though the election came off in a week, butcher
}l oU , ed one , for ever y two that Collins
polled. With more time he would have done better.
Cheek says he has a dual mission—to represent
Westmoreland in the Council as it ought to be
and (secondly) whenever an election for the Council is
going to happen, to go into that district and arrange for
opposition, so that there will be no more walks-over
Cheek, the ploughman, is well equipped for the billet,
and, by-and-bye, the House of Dodery will be quite un-
roofed, and the Cheeks and Samsons will give the rotten
walls a good shake and stand from under.
