The N.S.W. Coalition Negotiations.
The proposed coalition in N.S.W. is “off.” The
problem of conciliating 14 Progressives with one
portfolio proved incapable of solution. All the
Progressives” were so very progressive towards
the portfolio that its fate threatened to be like
that of an early martyr thrown to wild beasts at
Jtphesus. The Bulletin is open to confess that
it is rather sorry that the coalition negotiations
tailed, and that only Waddell and a few
camp-followers have gone over. There is so
Jittle difference between the Waddell and
Garruthers parties that they really should
unite and be two hearts that borrow as
one. It would be of advantage indeed if
the rage for coalition continued further, and
the section of the Labor party which shares the
Laddelluthers passion for borrowing and giving
cheek to the Australian Government joined hands
too, and left an honest remnant with a habit of
taking a decent Australian view of things to form
the nucleus of an organisation which would really
represent the sounder thoughts of N.S. Wales (for
N. S. Wales isn’t all undignified whine and reckless
finance and blind following of the linen-drapers’
organs). There are two issues of prime importance
in N.S. Wales State politics. The first is finance.
.No party is satisfactory on that point. The
Waddelers represent the old Owe’Sullivan burst
of extravagance. The Carruthersites are the
preachers and practisers of an equal extrava-
gance, flavored but not disguised by an odious
hypocrisy and profession of Reform. They
represent the shepherd who takes his rum
hot with peppermint, and then flourishes his
mendacious breath in the advocacy of teetotal-
lsm. The L'aborites are the backsliders, and the
men who call woe on their own heads because
they are wilfully blind. On the eve of last elec-
tion they were flogged into professing some sym-
pathy with honest finance, and put a half-and-half
honesty plank on their platform, and then, after
the elections, dropped the question down a drain-
pipe, and allowed a leading member of their
party scoff at it publicly with im-
punity. The second important issue of
mu ” State politics is Federalism.
There is grievous need for a party which will
courageously protest against this dismal policy of
whine and whimper and bluff and political
spelling, to which Carruthers endeavors to com-
mit the “Mother State,” a party which will
declare for Australia and against the petty
parochialism which disgraces Sydney and puts it
in the position of a sulky infant lying about sham
grievances. So far, there is no such party
Neither the Waddellers, the Carruthersites nor
the Laborites come up to the specification. Each
organisation is infected with Anti-Federalism and
provincialism, though probably in each there
is a remnant which is really Australian
and courageous enough to say so, in defiance
of the linen-drapers’ organs. The Bulletin then
would welcome a coalition which would knock all
three unsatisfactory parties into one horror, and
clear the way for an honest organisation, pledged
not to further pawn the country’s fortunes, and
williiig to be Australian and dignified in the
State’s relations with the Commonwealth. The
majority of the people would support such a
party, if it could but be materialised.

Darlington (Sydney) Municipal Council is, of all
the toadstool institutions that block the estab-
lishment of a Greater Sydney with the tin pot
dignity of their mayors and aldermen, absolutely
the worst. At a recent meeting of the council
this happened :
A letter was from the proprietor of a hat factory,
stating that he was placed at a disadvantage in not having-
suitable power to work his machinery. He had applied
to the City Council for a supply of electricity from their
main along the City-road. The council was agreeable
to supply the current, but could not do so until the
Darlington Council gave permission to carry the current
into the borough. He asked the council to give the
necessary consent. . ..
Alderman Cover was against the proposal. If the
Wa f adowed > it meant the introduction into the
Dorough, not only of one main, but it was quite probable
that the borough would be honeycombed with wires.
It the man wanted electricity, let him generate his own.. .
The Mayor (Alderman Spring) said they must be
very careful that they did not allow the city to
get the upper hand of them in any shape or
Ib ! vas a h66le b > 6 of humbug—a lot of rot, a lot of
twaddle—to consider that a hat factory had to be run by
p He was opposed to anything in the shape of
Greatei Sydney, and the proposal was only a “ leg in.”
And consider it well—this municipality which
towards Sydney as Joe Caruuthers wants
iN.b. w . to do towards the Commonwealth, covers
45 acres . It is the smallest municipal bumble-bee
in Australia. It would take 14 of it to make an
ordinary selection. It is carefully divided into
one ward because there isn’t room for two. It
would make a square about 450 yards each way.
? n . a “ te 9 ould g et across it in 491. seconds.
And it hires its gas from a private co., but it won’t
hire electricity from the Sydney Council lest its in-
dependence—the independence of 45 acres—
should thereby be endangered ! Ye gods !

There is never an Act passed for ensuring clean
cow-yards healthy cows, or sanitary slaughter-

and “unnecessary,” or as an

session the Victorian Parliament passed a splendid
Pure Food Act, and already this Act has done
excellent service in Melbourne. Yet this is what
the Mayor of such an important country town as
Warrnambool has to say about it:—

dug out of their burrows ; and very often an in-
fluential citizen gets hurt in the process. Some-
times even a grocery alderman is damaged. So
the average alderman is content to be unclean
and to eat impure food. Also, for the sake of the
symmetry of things, he seems to take a delight in
making his fellow-citizens unclean animals like
himself.

“One of Them ” writes :
Some evidence given at the Easby Marine Inquiry
throws considerable light on the working conditions
obtaining* aboard a larg*e proportion of steamers eng’a2*ed
in the inter-State trade. The master stated that he had
been on duty for 22 consecutive hours, and then lay down
to sleep ‘ all standing.” This, of course, is the usual cus-
tom when the weather is thick or dirty. The master
according to the reading of the Merchant Shipping Act’
is always in charge and responsible, no matter How lon £
he may have to be on his feet. At no time, as things are
at present, can he legally say to his chief or any other
ofhcer, I’m played out, and going to sleep. You are in
full charge till I’ve had a rest.” The law takes no cognis-
ance of physical limitations.
But it’s not only the master who has long hours of
duty. Every officer on every vessel trading on this coast
can tell a similar story of lengthy hours worked at that
most monotonous occupation, the taking in of cargo and
then of going to sea and standing a watch right on top of
it all. In some isolated instances, arrangements are
made whereby the officer taking charge of the first watch
at sea has at least the preceding four hours to rest. But
in the majority of small vessels, where one or, at the
most, two officers besides the master are carried, such a
relief system is unknown. The wonder is not that acci-
dents occur, but that they happen so infrequentlv
In the recent case cited at the Arbitration Court one
of the points on which particular stress was laid’was
the provision of a proper chart-room in every vessel—the
room to be devoted to this purpose only—to be open at
all times to the ofhcer in charge, and to be provided with
all necessary appliances for the safe navigation of the
vessel (At present, many owners refuse to supply
charts.) \et this point was calmly waived aside by the
Court as one of little importance. The reason given was
that owners had promised to make the necessary altera-
tions when new vessels ivere built.
Over and above the futility of putting any faith in
owners promises where employees are concerned, what
about the hundreds of vessels already running in which
the court contemplates no alterations?
Accommodation of officers was another point mentioned
in the Guilds case against the shipowners, and here
again the Court was content to rest on the owners’
promise of improvement. The improvements, if left to
owners’ sweet will, are likely to happen in about 100
years time, when by consistent meanness the mercantile
marine officer has been driven off the sea. Improvements'
wdl come only when owners have to <ro round hat in
hand begging* certificated men to take charge of and run
their ships. This sounds an exaggerated statement, but
e l en ”° w 6bose 111086 kkely to know, hear of a shortage
of certificated men in various parts of the world and for
this owners have only themselves to blame
S.A. is elated. In 1906 it gained 794
people by excess of arrivals over departures, and
this makes the dry State feel good. With hardly
any coal, and a grievous scarcity of gold, silver,
lead, and other minerals, and with a great
reliable drought, the Middle State has always had
a fearsome row to hoe. Its departures exceeded
arrivals in 1882, 1885, 1886, 1887, 1888. 1889 1891
and then every year from 1894 to 1905. So the
turn of the tide makes the battling Crow-eaters
feel glad.

At Sydney Palace, on Saturday, “ The
Walls of Jericho ” were reared once more, to be
once more thrown down by strong man Flemming
playing as Jack Frobisher. They were jerry-
built walls anyway, but the name is a good one.
.Like the last play, “ What WouldaGentleman do?’*
t * ie ® rou &h-Flemming Co., the
Walls have as hero an Australian of the rough-
diamond description. He isn't exactly potch; rather
lie is opal as it comes from the ground. Our men
would seem to be largely set by English play-
wrights as rough gems spilin’ to get into High
society. Jack Frobisher might be an Australian
mining capitalist, but Dicky Hook in “What
Would is an English linen-draper, not an Aus-
tralian either rough or smooth. Miss Beatrice
Day has a close-fitting part in the rble of Lady
Alathea, and she plays with ease and grace. She
is an artiste who “grows ” on the public, and her
musical voice has many inflections. Mr. Norman
M Keown takes the part of Hanky Bannister
and Mr. Gregan M‘Mahon that of Hannaford,
whose bark is worse than his bite, and who
doesn t seem to have the necessary dog-license
Apparently even his bite wouldn’t hurt
much. Mr. Carter Pickford long ago fixed him-
self as a favorite with the public in the rdle of the
cold would-be-wicked Dallas, and the remainder
of the co. plays up fairly and squarely to the
principals. Quality-Street ”is being produced
there vv ednesday, Thursday and Friday nights, and
on SaJurday “Olivia” is to be presented for the
Fme m Australia. “ Olivia” is adapted from
ihe vicar of Wakefield,” which is one of the
books which everybody admires and very few
read. When it is mentioned to the ordinary
person he is so much enraptured with it, and
paws the air so wildly in the effort to express his
admiration, that he can’t get down to details.

Australia has got to choose between a black population
and a Jewish white one.—ISRAEL ZANGILL.
“ It'll have to be me or Thambo, Mith."
Public interest in the ghastly doings that
occur nightly at Sydney Criterion remains keen.
‘Honor Thy Father,” the current Bilanderson
hair-raiser, has not ceased to bring alternately the
cold shiver of terror to the spine, and thegentle’tear-
drip of pity to the eye of the audience ; neverthe-
less it will not continue after Saturday next. On
that date “The Squatter’s Daughter” will be
revived. This is a home-made drama, which has
put up an Australian record for popularity.
* * -:|f *
That sugary bit of sentimentalism, “ Beau-
caire,’ was revived at Sydney Royal on Saturday,
and all the gilded girleens flocked to see Julius
Knight’s superb limbs, and the gilded youths
followed them to weakly insinuate that perhaps
they are not real. “Beaucaire” is such an
impossible and sickly flower of romance that it
needs the best of playing to make it tolerable. In
the first presentation, with Miss Maud Jeffries
and Julius Knight it had that assistance; in
subsequent revivals there has been always one
conspicuous weakness in the cast. Last time the
trouble was one of Sydney’s aristocratic youths,
who undertook to show how Julius Knight’s part
could be “played by a gentleman,” and gave a
very good idea of how it might be played by a
stable-boy. On this occasion it is the grace and
charm of Miss Maud Jeffries that are lacking in
the Lady Mary Carlyle of Miss Orton. Apart
from the “star, ’ the only players who stand out
are Harry Plimmer as the Duke of Winter-
set, the British rascal who acts as so
fine a foil to the heroic virtues of the
French dandy; and Hubert Willis as Beau
Nash, the supreme snob of history, who, himself a
man of low degree, by sheer impudence became
the social tyrant of an epoch. But the mounting
and stage-management of “Beaucaire ” are splen-
did ; and if the minuets are not well danced, they
are magnificently dressed. On Saturday next
“ The Silver King” will be staged.
* .* * *
“House Pull” is a notice not often
flaunted outside the Town Hall, Sydney. Yet
after 7.J0 on Saturday night that was the legend
displayed. The Besses o’ the Barn had drawn as
if nuggets were to be given with the programmes.
One hundred and fifty bygone years of ripened
melody have worked their value into the bands-
men’s performances of to-day. It is said that the
“Besses” are hereditary bandsmen. Their
great-grandfathers were the original Barn
players. Anyway, their brazen music is
like fine old liquid sunshine. Nary a reed
or string in the band. Brasses, brasses all
the wav. and two drums. The programme was a
popular” one, and the encores were often well-
known hymns, or songs nearly approaching
sacredness. “The Lost Chord ” was given as an
organ rolls it out when a master’s hand is on the
keys. And the “soprano cornet ” rose and bowed
to an audience that was making more noise than
several cataracts. In “ The Turkish Patrol ” the
pomp and circumstance of the approach of a
regiment is so superbly described by the band as
to almost visualise the incident. Tschaikowskv
and “ Tannhauser ” selections balanced the “Lead
Kindly Light,” and “Alice, Where Art Thou?”
encores. A new programme will be given every
night, and there is every indication that the
Besses will pack the Barn as long as they stay.
Miss Josie Miles’ concert, under the
management of Mr. A. E. Balch, was a vocal and
financial success. In “Abide With Me,” the
pretty youngster gave a good account of herself,
and her honeyed voice ought to be capable of big
things in the not far future. The Garcia School
of Music assisted with the programme, and Mr,
Lawrence Campbell recited.

West’s Pictures are still booming at Sydney
Lyceum, and, in place of the Bresoians, who have
departed, de Groen’s band plays melodies that
cause the public to say, “That is good; give us
another.”

This is a year of plenty for Melbourne
showmen. The early door catches the early cus-
tomer. Also it is a wise father who books his
own seat. At the Royal a full house has made a
contented Bland, and the bloated treasurer has
been declaring a large surplus ever since “The
White Heather ” was brought back to uotico three
weeks ago. It is only a revival, and an old one at
that, but a sometimes discerning public recognises
the “Heather” as a revival of the fittest. And
because this handsomely-appointed melodrama is
quite free from comic relief of the silly, antiquated
kind, the present scribe begins to hope there
may be a glad time coming when theatre
audiences will always take their laughter
reasonably, instead of deriving it from an incred-
ible stage policeman, a pert parlor maid, and a
private soldier who sasses the wicked captain.
One may laugh at what is meant to be humorous
in “The White Heather” without losing one’s
self-respect. It rises superior to comic relief, and is
richly endowed withgirl, asarule. Allthisxsbyway
of coming slowly to the fact that the scene of the
Amateur Rehearsal of “Lady Godiva”seems a
little brighter than ever. The vision of Jennie
Pollock Godiva displaying an excellent figure
under the protection of the Goddess of Purity
(who is Mrs. Bland Holt) will be repeated every
week until further notice. To all bald-headed
male citizens who are leery and heavy laden the
present writer would say—Don’t miss it.
* * sis *
The task of following Miss Tittell Bruue
in her good-bye round of characters at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne) is a rather arduous under-
taking. The play that happened last Saturday
was “Leah Kleschna,” the story of a larcenous
young woman, who stole her way into a wealthy
philosopher’s heart and home, and eventually cap-
tured him. for better or for worse in a reformatory
cabbage-garden, where the size and frequency of
the vegetables would have melted a Chinaman to
tears. But “ Leah ” is already a sinner of the
past, and “ Mary Ann ” is droning in her stead.
The slavey at Miss Susie Vaughan’s lodging house
is said to work hard, but she drones nevertheless.
Miss Brune will shake her curls at Romeo on
Saturday, and for two subsequent nights they
will point a warning against early marriages.
After which Juliet will resolve herself into
Camille, and Romeo will be Armand, and their
love will know no matrimony whatever.

formance on the rink suggests that an Austral
Skate Race.is a sporting event of the future.

The location of Melbourne’s Musical Boom
(late of the Princess’s) will be shifted to the Town
Hall on Saturday, and for some few months there-
after the municipal building will be a place of
intermittent furores. On Saturday afternoon
(18th) Mrs. Carreno is to play the piano to an
Australian audience for the first time in her
famous career. She will subsequently give a few
evening recitals, but on this initial matinee
occasion the foreign lady is to appear as the soloist
in an orchestral concert conducted by Marshall
Hall. The tremendous musician last, mentioned
will conduct seven Saturday afternoon orchestrate
in all. This will be the first of them, and what
with the welcome to Marshall Hall, and the wild
excitement incidental to Mrs. Carreno, the con-
cert now impending is expected to leave the
audience in a state of nervous collapse. At such
times the Town Hall stands in sore need of a
refreshment bar.

A musical enthusiast writes :
The “ All Blacks,” at Melbourne Town Hall, advertise
themselves as “versatile,” and they are. Pa, and Ma,
and Miss Nellie (who stars), with little sister Elsie (a
pretty flautist), and brother Bert, all flock on to a cosy
room-like platform, where they wade through a maze of
violins,’cellos, and brasses, and find themselves places
to sit on among the colored ribbons of mando-
lins and guitars. Then they play just what-
ever they can lay their hands on. Occasionally, when
the melody gives one time to gasp, a he or a she of
the party snatches up a new instrument and drops the
other—they don’t seem to mind what they drop or what
they snatch—and the orchestra goes on its even tinkling
way. The whole thing has a homelike feeling, as if one
were “ out to tea.” Ma is faithful to the piano, and sits
there in a prim little way, steadily playing an overture or
accompanying a song, or violin or flute solo. The com-
pany lives up to its advertisement in everyway. “Miss
Nellie ” (in big letters) is advertised for “ tricks on the
violin,” and her tricks are real gymnastics.

At the Princess’s there is nothing audible
just now save the lingering echo of a frantic
demonstration which marked the fifth perfoim-
ance of “The Valkyrie,” and the close of the
season —on Friday last. The Opera Co. has gone
to. South Australia, where a largely German
population will be made happy fora week or two.
It was not necessary to be a German in order to
appreciate the recent respectable performances of
German operas in Melbourne, but it was hard for
any non-German ear to be fully satisfied with
the company’s singing of non-German works.
Moreover, Miss Sara Anderson, the most accom-
plished artist of the lot, appeared only in the
three Wagner operas. During the first weeks of
the season a number of thoughtless people failed
to understand why Miss Anderson’s comparative

The Opera House added King and Benson
to its programme last Saturday, and the further
addition of the Svengalis is promised for next
Saturday. Re these “ telepathic ” strangers, The
Bulletin has already taken some amazing allega-
tions for gospel. Therefore, if they do not duly
amaze in accordance with the allegation this
journal will adopt an injured tone on the subject.

Mrs. Tom Fitzgerald’s circus company
arrived at Fremantle last week, but the manager-
ess was not with it. She was left behind at
Colombo, too sick to continue the voyage. An
Australian who saw the circus in India states
that Mrs. Tom has picked a strong company, and
should do very well in this country.
*■ * * *
Cup week in Adelaide has seen Miss
Palotta and co. frivolling at the Royal (a filly
named Palotta won a race at Morphettville too).
Two plays were crowded into the one week. ‘ ‘ Hop-
kinson” is “The Walls of Jericho” on lighter
lines. There is no one good in London, saith the
prophet of these Jeremiads ; no, not one, saith
the family solicitor. The author is to be praised
for the pluck with which he has made his bounder
bound to the end, instead of playing fairy god-
father to everybody, as is the habit of bounders in
usual last acts of drama. “The New Clown” is
a problem play on “ Charley’s Aunt ” principles ;
put a small humorous person into an un-
usual setting, and see what will happen.
Hugh Ward is less small than the general type
of exponent—Penley, Grrossmith, Thornton—but
not less funny. There has been a whole team of
clowns (Pierrots, they call themselves) at the
Town Hall, but cheirsis clowning of the drawing-
room order. However, the imminent arrival of
the opera—on a Monday evening, of all uncanny
times—overshadows everything else, and Adelaide
is shaken, so to speak, by the tread of a reported
17-stone Lohengrin on its very doorstep.
“THE VALRYRIE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sat me—that’s the German form—
In the amphitheatral bulge,
Just over Slap, and all his myrmidons,
Fifty in number.
Men of chortle and of rootle-tootle,
Of rasp and twitter, brassy blare,
Of tweet-tweet, and of hurly-thrumbo—
Men who puff, like rage of Boreas,
At trombone and the mystic horn.
Some lay white handkerchief on shoulder
And apply the suasive fiddle.
Some—but, in short, ’twas a band.
Muzzy’s command, “ Lights down for overture,"
Is faithfully obeyed. The great Slap, crawls
Through aperture beneath the stage,
And there is a mighty accolade
From ghosts that fill dress circle,
Stalls, and heaped-up amphitheatre.
Whether of bald heads or faces matters not—
All flesh is ghost
Mith th electrics downed in playhouse.
Silence ! Ye two thousand ghosts
(Shadows we are and shadows we pursue)
For the worship of St. Wagner.
Two taps from Slap, and he is in it !
Behold how rapt his face
In reflection from the illuminant music.
He seems to have four eyes ;
His eyes and hands embrace the fifty.
Then he lays a characteristic finger on his nose.
Go! By thunder, they are off !
Xot with more sweep go the Cup horses
When the ropy starting gate flies up
Than starts this band,
With the prize storm of “ Vaachner I"
Each bandsman of the Fifty
.Must now be multiplied by fifty
To the dazed imagination of the ghosts.
Suppose that Gulliver in Brobdignag
Had been assailed by mosquitoes
As big as cows, such would be their buzz
Mingled with a circular saw at work
In diameter of Dandenong’s highest stringy hark,
Reputed to be five hundred feet,
According to veracious Baron Mueller.
Wagner says, “ This is a storm.”
I thought it was Gehenna.
A thousand bookies on the Flemington Hill,
Whose barking sounds far off like that of dogs,
Would not be in it.
To applaud would be impertinence ;
Besides, Wagner always takes away your breath.
The green plush curtain, with its golden lyre,
Rustles and shrivels up
Sam pause in the music.
Before us is .a sort of wool-shed
Dwelling of the Gotterdammerung times ;
A fat man garbed in skins of goats
Rushes through a big barn-door
And flops upon an antediluvian sofa.
When you’re told what the orchestration means
It explains all—rather roundabout.
But it hurdy-gurdies on,
Like stringing Frankfort sausages
By the million.
Fatty and Sara Anderson
Mingle fluty notes for half-an-hour.
The barn-door opens and shows Hubby.
Only understand the Leit-Motifs
And you’ll perceive the sense of nonsense,
Fatty pulls a sword out of a tree
And hooks it like a Lothario lodger
With the spouse.
Twenty-five minutes interval for lager.
Up goes the green baize, or rather velvet.
Coleman, Upward, and the other painters
Show you what Walhalla’s like—
Sot the Gippsland one, but that
Where Jupiter Wotan rules—
A rocky place with lots of stones,
Moulded like huge kidney-beans ;
But when Wotan kicks one
It has the celestial lightness of a pillow.
Wotan and his pet. Amazon or Valkyre,
OrWalkure, masculine Brunhilde,
Are great on helmets, structures three feet high,
With feathers like the Squaw Man’s Indian Chief.
Then there’s Wotan’s wife, Queen Frieka,
Riding in a chariot drawn by sheep.
Music ! Music by the furlong ;
Rot one instant’s pause for Herr Slapotfski,
Who wears out three flannel shirts per night—
One for each act,
Fierce Brunhilde never heard of Love.
I’d not like her to hear while I was by.
She comes on with a livery-stable horse,
The most amiable that e’er you saw,
And yet it carries her in flight right through the clouds.
Sara Anderson asleep—the Fat Man watching.
Behold how Brunhilde learns of Love !
Like another Eve she falls
At mere sight of the apple.
For this must Wotan then degrade her
From Am zon to Woman.
He stands at top of the back scene,
Between them lots of little fires.
They hem her till a hero comes
In the next opera, which won’t be seen
Till 1908, and maybe not then.
Meanwhile the shocked Valkyrie Amazons—
Eight of them, all Melbourne girls—
Shout “ Hu, lu ! Ho, la ! Whroo ! whroo ! ”
In Wagnerian screams and shrieks.
How for the Ride of the Valkyries !
Slapoffski pegs in like
The eight Amazons, or something like them,
Gallop on bareback horses through the clouds.
And these things hustle
From seven till eleven-thirty.

THEORBO.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Lucifer ” : I do adore mummer Kingston. He reminds
me of my barber. Every time I observe him my eyes are
drawn irresistibly' to his crown, and the tiny, carefully-
parted ravines of curls. I fancy that he must put his
locks in curl-papers before bed-time, and oil them
religiously with the sun. But he does seem so unfor-
tunate in his female partners. For instance, Tittell
Brune is a mite in comparison, and, in an embrace, there
is precious little Tittell. But I have seen an embrace
where there was mighty little Kingston. I refer to
“ Fedora,” played here some years ago by the voluminous
Nance O’Neil. Kingston was male lead. The lovely
mountain of flesh cast herself at him many times to em-
brace him. But she didn’t. She enveloped him, leaving
a little bit of his boot showing to the observer. It would
be only fair to Kingston to pit him against a heroine who
stands 5.7, and weighs about 10.3.
“Blister” : Ada Fraud’s conversion seems like a bit of
stage mechanism. “Presently I became aware of a
dazzling white light, which grew larger and larger as it
approached me, and then all at once it unfolded, and
there in the centre was the blessed figure of the Saviour.”
That yarn is a relic of old dramatic days; then
also the dazzling light used to approach and grow larger
and unfold, and in the midst of it was George Rignold
declaring his innocence and denying all knowledge of the
document which the base miscreant had put in his pocket.
At Ballarat, Ada Ward, the preacher actress, had a
dolorous attack of self-commiseration, and pictured
manager J. C. Williamson and parson E. S. Hughes as
miscreants who had attacked her —a poor, long-suffering
woman—and grievously manhandled her in a servile
press. Ada shed tears over her wrongs, and then be-
stirred herself. She raked J.C.W. with a hot fire of vitu-
peration. She started on the Rev. Hughes, whom she
accused of beguiling youths into the champagne habit,
and of many other things. “Heis a self-conceited fop,”
cried the poor, sorely-tried lady. “He spends hours
before the mirror, tittivating himself before going into the
pulpit. Thank God for making me humble and real.
These are nice men to attack a defenceless woman.” Tim
Bulletin recalls the case of the other woman who was
torn off her prostrate husband after she had almost slain
him with a mangle-roller. “ That’ll teach l.iin to pull faces
at a poor, weak woman ! ” said the indignant wife.
In Ballarat (Vic.) they got large doses of Ada Ward, and
the medicine did not agree with even the Methodists
who brought her there. The voluble Ada is too much
of a business woman for the local saints. She stipulated
that she was to get all the collections, without having to
pay any of the expenses, and the Methodies agreed, lest the
Salv’army should rush in, after all, and take away their star.
But after a while Ada engaged the biggest hall in Bal-
larat on her own account, and advertised her willingness to
tell the awful story of her theatrical past at a bob a time.
She raked in the shekels, but that isn’t the trouble ; the
Ballarat Methodists—most of them Cornish—have a feel-
ing that they have been used and thrown over. Miss
Faded Ward evidently finds lecturing easier and more
profitable than battling for stage jobs. Anyhow, Bal-
larat Wesleyans reckon that whether she is sincere or not
(they still admit the “ whether ”), she is too keen for
them, and her end was more welcome than her beginning.
If an actress cannot be a Christian, she can at least be
an evangelist. But if an actress becomes an evangelist,
should she undye her hair ?
Some facts about the late J. A. Delany. His early
boyhood was spent in Newcastle (N.S. W.), and, during his
teens, he came to Sydney, where his instructor, VV. J.
Oordner, who was also choirmaster at St. Mary’s, reported
him as a musical genius. At the age of 14 he was scoring
for orchestras. He composed a large quantity of religious
music, including two Masses ; and, curiously enough, the
first Mass he composed was the last he conducted. On the
morning of his death, while in the delirium of pneu-
monia, he tried to get out of bed, protesting that he
must go to St. Mary’s that evening. And he did, but it
was his dead body they took down to the g reat Gothic
cathedral. Mr. Delany was a widower, and leaves two
daughters, one of whom is at present in England. His
range of instruments included the grand organ, piano,
violin and viola. A few months ago he was presented
with an illuminated address to mark his leadership of the
choir for 25 years. He was a musical enthusiast as well
as a genius, and kept himself absolutely poor through
helping aspirants over the difficulties that usually beset
the beginning of a musical career.
THE LATE J. A. DELANEY.
“ J.A.C.” : By the death of J. A. Delany, on Saturday
last, music in Australia has sustained a great loss. It is
not too much to say that his death is a loss to music all
the world over. Although born in London, he was
essentially an Australian musician ; all he knew of music,
and few have ever known more, he learned in this
country. He came to Australia with his parents when
quite a child, and passed his useful life here. His father,
J. D. Delany, was a journalist who was associated with
Henry Parkes in the founding of the Empire. He studied
music principally with VV. J. Cordner. It is no disparage-
ment of other Australian musicians to say that he stood on
the apex; he was a genius, and his talent would have made a
way for him anywhere on earth. As an executant, violinist,
pianist and organist, as a composer and conductor he was
equally at home. I have seen him score music as quickly
as another man would write a letter. His production of
Dr. Elgar’s “ Dream of Gerontius,” in Sydney, close on
four years ago, was a work of monumental labor and
efficiency. liis compositions would have been creditable
to even the classic composers. He was for a long time
conductor of Sydney Liedertafel, and for about a quarter
of a century organist and choirmaster at St. Mary’s
Cathedral. It is now 27 years since we first met and
travelled together with Simonsen’s Opera Cos., and from
that time we maintained an unbroken friendship. He
was one of the most generous, honest, and, withal,
humorous of men.
“ S.S.” : From the view-point of the person who is not
capable of being thrown into ecstasies by the magnificence
of Wagner’s wind, thunder, lightning, and atmospheric
effects as produced by the augmented orchestra, “The
Valkyrie” is the most trying Wagnerian opera j'et pro-
duced in Australia. On my night of tribulation the
performance began at 7 o’clock, and was carried on till
about 11.20, and for nearly the whole of that time two
people were singing at each other. On occasions their
dialogues of song threatened to be interminable. The
singing may have been very fine, but the thing, regarded
from the dramatic side, is absurd. Even the attempts at
stirring - “business” in Act 111., with the war maidens
ambling over the clouds on their steeds, and the imprison-
ment of a lady in a mountain of fire, if put forward
as attractions in a Christmas pantomime, would be re-
garded as painfully insufficient, and would provoke the
scoffs of the gods. Yet these things are dwelt upon with
enthusiastic rapture by the daily critics. Even a minor
spectacular sensation is something- after four hours of
solid, wild, whirling Wagner.
“A.L.”: Re Venusberg music in “Tannhauser.” I
think it would be more correct to say that prior to a cer-
tain event in Wagner’s life he had no genius for writing
amorous music. That event was his meeting with
Matthilde Wesendonck, and its outcome was the music
of “ Tristan und Isolde,” and the very amorous strains of
the fi.st act of “ Die Walkiire.” In the early ’ forties,
when “Tannhauser” was written, the first Mrs. Wagner
was making Mr. Richard’s life a burden to him, and the
peculiarities of the love music in the earlier operas may
be put down to the fact th it he had quite forgotten what
love was like, and therefore couldn’t very well describe
it. Personally, I wish Mr. Musg'rove had bronyht
“ Tristan ” out here. It is easily the finest of Wagner’s
works, while the “ Meistersinger ” comes second. Both
these operas are broadly human, and don’t deal in the
supernatural.
“Melb.”: “The Valkyrie” churned up music before
me for 4J hours, allowing time for drinks for the blow-
hards in the orchestra. This preciosity is only one of
four acts on the same theme, and the whole “ Ring of
the Nibelunger” requires as much time for its perform-
ance as is occupied on the run from Sydney to Melbourne.
Still it is beaten, one way, by the historic drama one sees
at a Canton theatre. There, after much tom-tomming,
gonging, shoutingand fighting, and after imitation women
have shaken long black chevelures up and down in agony
for three hours. Manager Hung Fat explains that it is
only One-Eightieth part of the play, which goes on day
and night for six weeks. Some Brahmin Wagner called
Gollah Chunder will yet tackle his “Gotterdam-
merung”—this is not swear, but “Twilight of the
Gods.” However, descending to this little opera
of “The Valkyrie.” The overture is performed by
a million buzzing mosquitoes, to the accompani-
ment of 13 circular saws at work in the karri forest.
The story is too ridiculous to describe. The orchestra

An important proof of the value of spraying for
clearing ground of woeds appears in the Daily Telegraph,
May 8th, 1907. Everyone should have a “ Tyree, Spray,”
for the above purpose, and for painting, white-washing,
disinfecting, fruit growing, etc. “The following- letter
has been addressed by the Town Clerk, Bowen (Q.), to the
Manager of the Oxford Downs Station, Nebo ‘ In reply
to your inquiry as to the method followed by this council
in eradicating prickly pear, I have to inform vou that a
mixture of equal quantities of arsenic and caustic soda
was used by making them into a solution, using the heat
created by the caustic to get them to unite, or hy boiling
them together. The solution can then he reduced.by
adding sea water or salt and water, and if used fairly
strong by spraying on the plants, which should be first
bruised with a long-handed shovel or billhook, it will kill
them within a few days. Unless there is any hurry it is
much more economical to use the solution fairly weak, as
the same quantity will cover more ground, and will be as
effective, only taking a longer time to render the plants
nt for burning*. After they have been burned off, and
rain has fallen, it is advisable to go over the ground and
spray any small shoots which may have escaped the first
dose. About 100 allotments and a number of streets and
reserves have been effectually cleared of prickly pear at a
cos u of about £50.’ Send for printed matter. Tyree
Limited, 314 George-street, Sydney, tUS-W.”’
Teutonias can act, but they don’t this time. Wagnef
seems to have said : “You must not distract attention
from the incessant orchestration.” The performers
stand still, while the music tells you what is going on in
their minds. All is statuesque and other-worldly.
Tenor Studemund is an indescribably eccentric, big fat
fellow, who sings like a flute. Clad in skins, he is like a
walking lager barrel. He mostly keeps both arms
extended, and expresses emotion by rowing a boat. Mrs.
Heinze has a helmet, with white plumes and, la the Indian
chief in “ The Squaw Man,” and they are about half as
high as she is, furnishing a Minerva that appals. The
ride of the Valkyries is of Amazons astride on bare-back
horses, rushing through the clouds. It was terrifically
done at the Paris Opera.
Miss Heinze, the Brunhilde of “ Die Walkure,” bad an
exciting experience, the first night in Melbourne. At the
climax of a great scene she rushes off among the rocks,
leading her winged charger in an impetuous departure.
The charg'er did nor. fill the bill in appearance in any case,
and when Miss Heinze brandished her spear the effect
was too much for the gee-gee, which tossed its head,
broke loose, and knocked Brunhilde’s imposing helmet
off. Brunhilde lost her head as well as her head-gear, and
rushed off the stage in a panic, leaving an unbridled
horse to wreak vengeance on the scenery and to tread on
the stage-fainting Sara Anderson. It was interesting to
see the awesome Brunhilde, when she had recovered her
equilibrium and hurriedly readjusted her back hair, look
shyly out from her place of rocky vantage to see if her
horse had eaten anybody, and then take her courage in
both hands and scoot back to recapture her neddy—just
like a good Australian countrv girl rounding up her
mount.
“Adelaide”: One pities some of you other theatre-
goers for not getting the full flavor out of “Mrs. Gor-
ringe’s Necklace.” In Adelaide we had Miss Beatrice
Day as Mrs. Gorringe, and Mrs. Brough as her unwilling
hostess, and the way the two played up to each other
was something not to be excelled. How this co. does
chop its parts about! I see that in Sydney Miss Gordon
Lee is heroine in “ What Would a Gentleman Do? ” Miss
Beatrice Day is the slangy female, and Miss Major the
comic aunt. Well, we saw Mrs. Brough as the aunt, Miss
Temple as the slangy one, and Miss Day as the gentle
heroine.
The visit of the Besses o’ the Barn Band to Maoriland
has had an influence for good upon the various noises
that arise from the gale-swept islands. The Salv’army
bandsmen attended the concerts in dense numbers, and
were astounded to find that blare was not music, and that
a cornet could be played without a red, apoplectic face,
with an almost-burst expression, looking like a full moon
behind it. The local band at the wayside townlets,
which w T as angered because the fat and comfortable
Besses would not march behind it from the railway
station, listened also, and was impressed. Result is a
stilly atmosphere, no longer torn asunder by the madden-
ing blasts of the first trombone, and unshattered by the
loud reverberation of the big drum. Pianissimo is the
ruling key, and the disordered nerves of the inhabitants
are recovering from a long course of brazen debauchery.
Melbourne Herald reprints from London Chronicle a
yarn of Clive Darrill, “an Australian giant, Bft. Bin.
high, dating from Wagga Wagga, who fell in love with
Miss Mariedi, the Tyrolean giantess, appearing at a
theatre in Vienna.” The lady, whose height is not
stated, would not accept his love, so, during the perform-
ance, he proposed to her from the stalls. The audience
was with him, but he had to follow her about for a long
time before she consented. “ Mr. Darrill has left two
sisters in Australia who are each 7ft. 6in. in height. His
father was one of the tallest men in Australia, standing
Bft. 3in.” But the yarn is taller than all of ’em.
According to New York World, per cable, the ever-
green Ellen Terry makes another bid for connubial feli-
city with a member of her American touring co. The
husband is described as 32. Ellen is 59. This is her
third matrimonial venture. Her first essay, made when
she was about 18, was with artist G. F. Watts, then a
man of 40. The Ellen Terry temperament wouldn’t mix
with the Watts asceticism, however, and they soon
parted. Later, the R.A. got his divorce. Then she
married E. A. Wardell, whose chief title to fame lay in
that episode. This writer is hazy as to whether he was
mortally, or only judicially, mislaid. Now she reverses
her first experiment by marrying somebody 27 years her
junior. Perhaps Ellen pathetically recognises that old
shoulders are poor support for some of this world’s loads.
The other morning, when Bland Holt drove up to the
stage door of Melbourne Royal, he was surprised by the
apparition of Detective Christie and an assistant seizer.
Christie had made a capture of illicit opium at a Chow
store adjoining the theatre, and, “from information
received,” he was lying in wait for some more of the
smuggled goods to be brought along by a man in a buggy.
An approaching buggy was duly signalled, and as soon as
it was brought to a standstill Christie and his mate
rushed from their hiding place in order to raid the
vehicle. The historic voice of Bland Holt notified them
of their error. “If you’re looking for the rug, you’re too
late,” said Bland. “ Somebody stole it yesterday.”
Ernest Lester, the melodrama-hero actor whom Geacli
has imported for the leading billet in “ Human
Hearts,” has visited Australia in the spirit before
he arrived here in the flesh, on his way to Maori-
land the other week. Lester wrote “ Riding to Win,”
and a revival of that Blandoltinn success will be
happening at Melbourne Royal a week or two hence,
what time its author is starring in “Human Hearts” at
Wellington or thereabouts.

“ G.W.M.” in last Bulletin sums up the
“ Abo.” horse-sense controversy by pointing out
that there are different brands of horses. Also,
there are different brands of whisky. The right
one, D.C.L., will help a man out when mentally
bushed, while other brands may plunge him
deeper into the wilderness of his confusion. Yes,
D.C.L. is the whisky for helping a man to
make the most of his horse sense, intuitional and
otherwise.*
The N.S.W. Deform party seems driven
to fall back on a whole herd of old party hacks to
carry its banner at the approaching election.
Pumpkin Smith and Lonsdale are threatened as
two metropolitan candidates, while someone has
unearthed Varney Parkes for another seat.
It was poor, old, discredited Yes-No, the
Bogey Man of the last Federal elections, that
killed the proposed N.S.W. coalition. Car-
ruthers said, one night, that “the political party
to which he belonged had associated itself with a
party over which Mr. Reid was leader.” Next
day, when the Waddellers met, a majority of
them said that they might swallow Oarruthers
alone, but to swallow Carruthers and Reid at one
gulp was more than even a politician’s stomach
could stand. So by 7 to 4 the proposed coalition
was declared off. and Waddell and a few of his
followers drift across the floor as private rene-
gades.

A MIDNIGHT TRAGEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I do not care, for Care is dead,
We drowned Her late last night—
Yet I am haunted by a dread
The deed may come to light.
And I, grown used to living high,
Shall high and kicking nothing die.
I met some friends—or they met me,
I know not which, or who
Those friends of mine might chance to be,
But they were comrades true ;
And one remarked—a gloomy beast—
“ We’ll drown dull Care to-night, at least! ”
This same remark he often made
In ringing voice and clear,
Each time we boomed the liquor trade
By drinking draughts of beer,
Or any drink on which we fixed—
I and my drinks were somewhat mixed.
Yet Care still seemed with us to flit;
That melancholy chap
Seemed wed to Her—when he did sit,
She sat upon his lap ;
And when the last pub shut we found,
Quoth we : “ This dull Care must be drowned ! ”
We took Her with us to the Quay,
Where, like the slough of sin,
The waters glittered eerily,
And there we plunged Her in.
We caught her roughly by the hair,
And with a splash we drowned dull Care !
I do not know what happened then—
How I got home, or where
I parted with my fellow men ;
But still that splash I hear,
And, mindful of my country’s laws,
I wonder, dumbly, who it was !
N.S.W.

‘You must let me introduce you to my husband.
I’m sure you’d like him.”
“I don't think so. You see, I can never forgive
a man for having a pretty wife.”
Certain Australian bush marriages, in
which the bride’s only dower is her wonderful
rabbit-skinning capacity, make one remember
that beauty is only skinned heap.
Goulburn policemen are not the only-
members of the N.S.W. force who are wearing
out the human thinking engine over rival judicial
instructions as to whether suspected persons, be-
fore being questioned, should or
should not be cautioned that their
words may be used against them.
Justice Cohen says they should;
Justice Simpson says they
shouldn’t; and so on round
the major and minor
Benches. An inspector of
police in a Sydney case, the
other day, told the Court
lie had not cautioned the
prisoner, and that in a
career of many years he
had never cautioned
any prisoner; and a
day or two later
another officer said
he never ques-
tioned a suspect-
ed person with-
out first cau-
tioning him.
It seems to
be all a
matter of
habit.

Everything else can be fought against, but
when a mans minds un-b-bab-battanshed,
then L-L-Lug-Lordhelpem. Letshavadrink!”
Justice Chubb opened the Circuit Court
at Cairns, the liveliest town in North Queensland,
the other day, and this was the start of the list
he read :
A Kanaka, indecent assault.
Billy Martin, colored man, horse-stealing’.
Neils Magnus Neilson, st ealing letters.
“Jack,” aborigine, wilful murder.
A Hindoo, grievous bodily harm.
At that stage his Honor adjourned, with grateful
thoughts, no doubt, concerning the great things
that Philp and his old discolored-labor party had
done for a white Australia.
* * * *
“ L.E.” : How and why does Sydney pro-
duce its painful type of swell young men ? Met a
typical specimen at a King-street fried
fish emporium. He was toying with an
oyster. He wasn’t there to eat, but to
edify. No vulgar chip-chewer he, but
some article of vertu —a street decoration,
an art poster. His coat had a slit up the
back. His collar rose high like a white-
washed rampart. His striped pants turned
up to display blue silk socks with clocks.
His shoes had broad silk laces tied in
fancy bows. Retiring, he dropped a coin
languidly on the counter, not because he
liked the job, but out of graceful con-
sideration for the pleasure of others—
picked up a toothpick and the change,
donned his reindeer gloves, tilted his
chapeau at the correct aesthetic angle,
and then sauntered out of the A 1 Mussel
Saloon with the Willie Kelly crawl
stroke. Harbor Harold quite outclasses
the Melbourne mashers of Puppydog
Corner. Most of him seems to be im-
ported. He is no plain Australian, but
the product of the centuries. He is very
English in parts, with spots of Yankee,
striped with French, mottled with High
German, freckled with Dago—his drab
background of Woolloomooloo relieved
with gaudy patches of the George-street
Orient. Is the elegant creature due to
an ancient schooling in the blessings of
imported goods? Is it the influence of
Joseph Carruthers? Have the streets
affected his cerebellum—those streets so
narrow and crooked ? Is it that he goes
down to the Harbor in ferry boats and
studies the foreign sailors? Is anything
on God’s green earth responsible for him ?
Anyway, he is. There he poses like the
hero of a cigarette-card, and you can tell
the time from his loud-chiming art socks.

Sydney University has had £750,000
in cash, and about the same value in land,
from the State since its foundation ; and
one of the chief results that it forces on
public notice is the labored and childish goatish-
ness of Commemoration Day.

“Snell” : Bank clerks in Melbourne are
lifting up their voices again in beinoanment of
their unhappy lot. They alone have not pro-
gressed with the progress of the State. “Our
unrivalled prosperity” has not enabled the poor
tellers and the forlorn, faded, and prematurely
bald clerks, who wallow in other people’s gold in
the big, financial institutions, to get over their
chronic impecuniosity. The bankers’
myrmidons are now making a sad effort
to better their position by writing
dolorous letters to the daily papers,
and by making an abject appeal to
other people to do something—they
don’t know exactly what. My reply,
as one of
many ad-
dressed,
is: “Hoe
your
own row!
Come
out of
your
sicklya t-
mosphere
of shabby
gentility,
and fight
for your own
hand as other
workers have
done. Don’t think
because you paw tons
of money above-ground
that you’re two storeys
and an attic above the
miner who paws the raw
material down below,
and that the methods
li e employed to assert
himself are not good
enough to raise you
into a position of de-
cent and honor-
able independ-
ence.” Other
workers have
succeeded in
vastly improving
their positions
with the aid of
unions, but the
bank clerks have
despised such me-
thods, or else have
lacked the courage to
employ them. The result is that while the
workers are now proudly parading their banners,
the bank clerks are parading their sore toe.
* * * *
When the Federal Navigation Bill
Commission was sitting, last year, at New-
castle (N.S.W.), it shed such publicity on
the crimping evil that the State Govern-
ment was shamed into a pretence of action.
Certain inefficient regulations are now in force
which tend to restrict the unlawful supplying of
seamen to outward-bound ships, and the imme-
diate result is that the trade of the port is almost
at a standstill, owing to the difficulty vessels have
in obtaining crews. The harbor at Newcastle is
crowded with ships which are ready for sea, but
cannot leave the cranes for want of men, and the
loading of other ships is therefore interfered with.
The ship-masters say that there are as many men
available in Newcastle as usual. The ships
arrived fully manned, and the sailors must be
somewhere. But the boarding-house-masters—
the crimpers—who seem to be quite above the
inefficient State law, refuse to supply men unless
at a very high rate, and the port is held up in
consequence. This mainly applies to British
ships. The action of the crimpers is a protest
against the new regulations, which aim at ending,
or at least limiting, the crimping evil; and these
regulations do not apply to foreign ships, which
sign their crews on before the local consul,
instead of before the shipping-master. So the
non-British vessel gets away promptly. It seems
as if the sailor is just as much in pawn as he used
to be before the State Government took a hand—
a very poor hand—in the matter.

“Kiss the Book” is still the trouble of
Australian courts, and will be till the disease-
breeding old circumstance is suppressed. In
France, no volume is used ; the witness raises a
hand and says, “ Je le jure.” It Spain, there is
no kissing of the Book ; the witness places a hand
on the Gospels and says “ Si juro.” And it is the
same, or thereabouts, in all European countries,
except Britain. This habit of hugging the volume
is an unclean and useless custom taken over from
England—useless because numbers of people who
embrace the tome don’t even know what the tome
is, and numbers of others disregard its authority.
An English magistrate records that 50 per cent,
of the persons who kissed the Book before him
didn’t know what the Book was. And hardly
anyone who knocks his face against the publica-
tion is rendered a whit more reliable thereby. It
is a disgusting habit —this licking of the literature.
Another long trial and another disagree-
ment of the jury leave the Crick-Willis case where
it was. Nearly everybody in N.S. W. appears by
this time to have given up all hope that anything
definite will come out of any Crick-Willis trial,
and the case gets obviously weaker as it toddles
along. Some haggard and perceptibly ageing
witnesses, who were fairly sure of things before,
went more or less to pieces this time on cross-
examination. Dates that looked solid at the pre-
vious trial tottered and disappeared feebly round
the corner on further inspection. Things that
were previously confused either remained so or
got worse. Crick took all the legal points
he did before ; he also found new ones and
took them. The legal profession was so struck
with admiration that there is an idea of
presenting the Art Gallery with a large oil-
painting of Crick taking a legal point, the work
to be done by a competent artist. The prosecu-
tion looked older, and Crick looked younger, and
Willis looked about the same age. The judge
looked like a worried man who was afraid that
Crick knew more law than he did ; he may not
have been that way, but he looked it. Peter Close
looked more unsatisfactory than ever. As for the
case itself, grass and other minor forms of vegeta-
tion already begin to appear on its surface, and it
wears all the appearance of something that is
growing very tired of itself and that wishes it had
never made its own acquaintance.
N.S.W. Justice G. B. Simpson is a master
hand at summing up in a fashion at once chatty
and irrelevant. In the Willis-Crick trial he
discoursed for an hour on the dignity that
doth hedge a Judge, and the necessity for
newspapers to speak respectfully of him, and
for Speakers and Presidents of Houses of Parlia-
ment to guard him against attack. A long
disquisition followed on acting-Justices, inspired
by a complaint from Willis that he had, in a
previous prosecution, been tried before an acting-
justice. Incidentally, his Honor read out his
remarks when first taking his seat on the Bench,
viz. , “ I will administer justice in the fear of God
and without the fear of man.” Then his Honor,
after sparring for wind, got another inspiration,
and dealt with gifts and presents to Judges, which
were improper, but not necessarily corrupt or un-
lawful. Harry Levien, “ the Father of the
House,” had once sent him a wild turkey, and he
tasted it and found it delicious. He subsequently,
when the last turkey bone had been stripped bare,
told Mr. Levien that he must not repeat the
present. A solicitor had surprised him in his
chambers smoking a cigar. In all the long years
of their acquaintance that solicitor hadn’t seen him
combusting tobacco before. Later, he received
half-a-dozen choice Havanas from the solicitor,
but promptly returned them. However, in
Hobart, he bumped against the solicitor, and
accepted one cigar from him, for he was then
“ out of his jurisdiction.” And so forth, and so on,
till the foreman of the jury became afflicted with
earache.
* * * *
In Petone (M.L.), pronounced Pet One
by the uncultured stranger, the soul physician of
a local church was distressed recently to find
himself forestalled in the pulpit by a girl who
had been entrusted by the Deity with a message
for the flock. Remonstrances by the parson were
firmly rejected, and the inspired female was only
removed after a severe struggle with her stern
parent. The maiden shriekingly placed the
responsibility of the undelivered message upon
the preacher, and the reverend gentleman offered
up special prayers for the demented one. She
and her message are now in an asylum—another
sorrowful example of the consequences of much
undigested Scripture on a weak mental stomach.

A Benalla (Vic.) youth who lately pleaded
guilty to an inexplicable act of arson, explained
to the investigating Judge that his outbreak was
due to over-free indulgence in “ Tiger ” —which,
it was explained, is the Victorian name for wine
just out of the press. Judging from its effects,
“tiger” seems as disastrous as the “pinky,”
which has made itself notorious in S. Australia ;
and there is something more appropriate about
the Victorian name, too, “Pinky” isn’t a bit
suggestive of a drink with 97 devils in it.
TWO HYMNS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sundry letters have been received by the Premier’s con-
stituents, dated from Aden, from Mrs. Bleasby on behalf
of Mr. Bent, describing some of his experiences en route
to London. To Mr. Hamilton, mayor of Brighton, Mrs.
Bleasbj'writes “ My father desires me to write to you
that he is feeling the heat very much. He has the prickly
heat very badly.” To Mr. Brownfield she notes : “We
commenced to feel the heat shortly after leaving Fre-
mantle, and it has got hotter, until it is terrible. One
gentleman who was going home to get a title has died
and been buried at sea —a very sad affair.”
It was an elephantine mass
Who filled the seas with wrath
When the hot monsoon came out at noon,
And the shadeless sun was forth.
It was a man of Belial
Who daily raved on deck
When the sea of glass and the sky of brass
Rose up to burn his neck.
For oh ! he was a plenteous man !
(The sun was hot as hell) ;
O’er fat and meat, some cubic feet
Had he of cuticle.
The sun had burned his face cerise,
His chest, geranium pink ;
And there were none to make their moan
Or off .: film a drink.
The prickly heat had made him weak
As any ailing girl,
And tired and spent was Thomas Bent—
His hair was out of Keirl!
Courage ! We’ll soon be through it ! Take a pull at
hymn the second.
A gentleman was goin’ ’Ome,
To take bis title in his ’and,
Where Aden’s lime-rocks tear the foam,
He went to join the ’Eavenly band ;
Tho’ Angels may for this be glad,
We must admit it’s very sad.
The gentleman was goin ’Ome,
For to be crowned in Londing town ;
Alas ! he will not further roam,
He’ll ’ave to ’ave a ’Eavenly crown !
But will not Edward VII. be mad ?
’Tis an affair that’s very sad.
A gentleman was goin’ ’Ome,
For Barrownite or C.M.G.,
He’d ’ave been better not to roam,
But stayed in Brighton a J. P.—
This reachin’ after beads is bad—
And that he missed ’era's very sad.
VIC.
MARTIN LUTHER.


The Hand
The May number of The Lone Hand has come
and gone. Its prompt acceptance by the reading
public was one of the sensations of the book-
selling trade in the Commonwealth.
The time has now come to speak of the June
number. It was said in criticism of the May
number that “ the stories were, on the whole, too
short.” An interesting train of inquiry is sug-
gested by this, as to whether there is a jreal
objection to a story because it is short though
complete, or whether readers were only sur-
prised, expecting the usual “spun-out” story and
getting instead stories in “concentrate” form.
However, the June stories happen to.be longer.
There are seven of them, and the chief authors
are Ethel Turner, Edward Dyson, “G. B.
Lancaster,”and Albert Dorrington; an anonymous
writer opens a sensational series “ The Secrets of
a Prime Minister.”
The special articles of the June number are
particularly interesting. Blamire Young writes
on Fremiet’s “Gorilla and Woman”; “Aqua
Fortis” on Etching (which is illustrated by a re-
markable series of etchings); Sir James Boucaut
on the Arab Horse ; Enid Derham on Lyrics ; and
“ Stargazer ” on “Author and Actor-Manager.”
About the art work and other features we shall
talk next week.

They do throw their money about in S.
Australia. Orroroo, a prosperous northern farm-
ing centre, of considerable size as farming centres
go, threw up its hat with the cockspurs in it when
its local champion, W. 0. Addison, was selected
to go to England with the Australian Bisley rifle-
team. It decided, morever, that it must do the
thing well and put up some of the expenses. So
a “ farewell ” to the champion was arranged, and
it was fixed that the golden wealth of Orroroo
should be poured out there. After it had been
poured out accordingly (and nearly half Orroroo
was there to pour) they counted up the wealth—
£2 12s. 6d.

“The meeting passed off most success-
fully,” says a press correspondent, reporting the
races at Oloncurry (Q.), where the copper-boom
is. And then he mentions, as a passing incident
of one of the waits between races, that “one of
our prominent townswomen publicly horse-
whipped a man for reasons not stated.” That
shows how customs differ on mining fields. Now,
at Broken Hill, where the lead boom is, the lady
would have spent half-a-crown on an advt. calling
upon the male malefactor to make good his words,
or look out for a summons. Perhaps the differ-
ence between fiery copper and dull lead accounts
for it; the patriotic lady suits her temper to her
district’s metal.
The Melbourne Friendly Societies’ Gardens
on the banks of the Yarra, once among the most
popular of pleasure resorts, are now in a steady
process of decay. A Chinese pagoda, tlje
paint peeling off in flakes, rears itself
totteringly in the centre of the
grounds ; and a band stand and
other buildings stagger in various
parts of the premises, all going
the way of the flesh. Looking at
the grounds from the Temple of
the Winds in the Botanical
Gardens, it suggests “Our
Selection ” which Old Dad has
given up as a bad job. Ad-
jacent is a good cricket or foot-
ball ground surrounded by a
banked asphalt cycling track ;
but cracks, inches wide, are in
the asphalt now, and not even
a ghost bikes there on a
whirling cobweb in the moon-
light. Nowadays the ground is
used only by fifth-rate football
teams or occasionally by a ladies’
cricket club, but the cricket club
looks depressed like one batting in a
cemetery, and the fifth-rate footballers play
feebly and curse with a poor, inefficient curse.
A paper that was playing hide-and-seek
got on to a N.S. Wales coroner’s nerves, the other
day, and some people are probably wondering
now whether the game wasn’t kept up too long.
A few weeks back, four men were killed by the
collapse of a tunnel, which they had just started
for the South Clifton colliery. The paper that
played hide-and-seek was the agreement between
the men and the management. Soon after the
accident happened, a detective called into the office
of the eo. and wanted this agreement, but the
office, after going into another room for a while,
said the paper wasn’t there. Next day the office, re-
inforced by one of the proprietors, was quite sure
that it had never been there, so, of course, the office
couldn’t hand it over. The manager at the mine
said he had it once, but didn’t have it any longer.
Later on it transpired that it had been passed on
to a solicitor ; and when it was tracked that far,
dhe solicitor said it didn’t belong to him and he
couldn’t produce it. At about this stage the
coroner remarked that the solicitor had better get
authority from somebody to produce it pretty
quickly, and the solicitor took the hint. As one
of the contracting miners, who didn’t happen to be
hilled, had already declared that the agreement
provided for timber to be supplied at the tunnel
mouth, and as he swore that he had complained
to the manager that the timber wasn’t there, and
as the Chief Inspector of Collieries said that
the fall was due to the insufficiency of timber,
there is going to be trouble.

“ Quandong ” : The Victorian Education
Gazette for April contains a neat piece of the
humor that is departmental, and therefore un-
conscious. Regulation XXIX., re Sloyd Classes
in Country Schools, and Regulation XXX., re
Cookery Classes in Country Schools, are quoted at
full length. Ignorant officialdom, comfortably
salaried and housed artistically, has prescribed in
one regulation : “ If a disused class-room or one of
the rooms of the teacher’s residence is available,
the class may be held therein by permission of the
director,” &c. That refers to Sloyd. In the
other regulation, the same idea is put forward as
a suggested provision for the cookery class. Most
of the Victorian country schools have two 12 x 10
rooms for the residence of the fad-ridden peda-
gogue, and if one is to be given up to cookery and
the other to Sloyd, it will become an even more
difficult problem than ever what to do with the
wife and bairns. Is the teacher to take up his
quarters in the porch, or the dog-kennel, built
through “local effort”? The Victorian peda-
gogue awaits reply from Officer No. 31, Red Tape
Department, in charge of the branch that con-
foundeth confusion.

UNFAIR ONE: “By Jove! Were you like that

After all, the apparent decease of the Col-
lingwood tote seems not to have brought peace
to Melbourne, but a sword. The different fanati-
cal religious packs which bayed and snapped at
John Wren are now on the verge of barking at
one another over the carcase. The Public Morals
League of Collingwood, presided over by the
Baptists of that suburb, annexes the honor; it
alleges that it “found” the enemy, and laid the
other packs on to him. T. 8. B. Woodfull, the
parson who assisted the police (so he says) to
obtain evidence against Wren and Co., claimed
all the power and the glory in the affair as far
back as the Methodist Conference in February.
He was whipper-in of the Methodist pack, it
seems. Willy Judkins, however, sets all the
credit to his own account—witness the ti-tree
crown and the wild whoopings of triumph ever
since. Unfortunately, Thomas Bent is not here
to ponderously assert that his Anti-Gambling Act
did the trick. Another party that has claimed no
glory, so far, is the Salv’army. Strange that it
never came into the fray. Perhaps it grows tired
these times?

“ Flinderino ” writes from a boat:—
Sydney Harbor is not the only place where one or two
boat companies try to run the show. A Melbourne com-
bine fancies that the jetties on the eastern side of Port
Phillip were built for its exclusive use, and its aspect of
shocke I astonishment when another boat comes along,
suggests the wild expression in the eye of Saul at the
rising of Samuel’s ghost. All the jetties belong to the
Government, and are open to any ship that likes to
arrive, but that makes no difference. On a recent Sun-
day two excursion steamers left Melbourne for Schnapper
Point (Mornington). The combine’s boat arrived first,
and filled up the only available berth alongside the jetty,
and landed its passengers. When the second steamer, an
outsider, put in an appearance, it found no room to tie
up, and, by the aid of the steam whistle, requested the
combine boat to move up or come out and make room.
But the combine boat was deaf. The outsider cruised
around blowing its whistle for half-an-hour or so, but
without effect. Then it dropped anchor close in, and
with the flag signals asked the combine boat to kindly
allow it the use of the jetty for ten minutes. The
combine boat had lost the code-book, so couldn’t
read the signals. The result was that a boatload of
disappointed excursionists had a compulsory voyage at
anchor, and returned to Melbourne without having
landed anywhere down the bay. The same thing hap-
pened some weeks previously when the water was some-
what rough and made things unpleasant. The local con-
stable acts as harbor master, and has control of the pier,
but when trouble occurs he happens to be on the other
beat or away collecting statistics.
The method adopted by Sydney companies of buying
up the pleasure resorts and wharves to secure a mono-
poly of the holiday traffic is out of the question in Port
Phillip. True, land round the bay is cheap enough and
could easily be turned into picnic grounds, but then
jetties would have to be built, and as there is no great depth
of water anywhere along the shore they would have to be
pretty long jetties. Anyway, the bay excursion traffic is
a very hungry speculation, and hardly worth fighting
for. Half-a-dozen steamers suffice for the regular sum-
mer traffic, while two can manage through the winter.
Some 12 years ago the Howard Smith people bought a
slice of Point Henry, near Geelong, and turned it into a
pleasure resort which was known as Bellarine Gardens.
The old paddle-boat Bellarine (now dead, broken up and
burnt) made daily trips from Geelong to the Gardens, and
the Edina called there. But the venture proved unprofit-
able, and Bellarine Gardens were deserted until just re-
cently, when the Howard Smith co. turned the one-
time pleasure resort into a poultry-farm. There is really
no “ down the harbor ” in Melbourne.

In one of the latest text books authorised
by the Education Department of N.S.W.
(General Geography by Hugh Robert Mill,
D.Sc. LL.D.) in a chapter dealing with N.S.W.
we read :
Practical shearers can clip 120 sheep per day, but
working at that rate is so cruel to the animals that
Government has limited the daily work of each man to
90 fleeces. (P. 207.)
Bands of convicts, escaping to the remote regions when
the colony was young, became bushrangers or armed rob-
bers, who plundered defenceless sheep stations and shot
the people. A strong force of mounted police, aided by
the unequalled native trackers, succeeded in putting
down bushranging on a large scale. (P. 207.)
The inference, of course, is that the evil still
exists, though mitigated somewhat.
The small towns (of Australia) are very enterprising ;
they all have hotels, a newspaper, a racecourse, a public
park, and verv often a lunatic asylum. (P. 202.)
“ J.G.,” from about the centre of Vic-
toria :
Re “Pterodactyl’s” complaint of the meanness of the
N.S.W. Education Department, and “John Boy-
aragger’s” comment thereon. I would like to say that
the Victorian Education Department is somewhat
meaner, if possible, than that of the Ma State. The Vic.
Department has a warm welcome for the teacher who is
a good cadger, and thus saves the blessed Department a
few quids. One of the Vic. country schools in my neigh-
borhood roosts lonely in the middle of a big plain, where
the sun in the summer time is nearly hot enough to wekl
iron, so that the school becomes a veritable Gehenna ;
and where, in the winter, the wind blows across the
plains like whiffs off the Antarctic. This schoolhouse
had no provision for sheltering the kiddies, if they came
too early, or at meal times, or after school hours, if the
weather happened to be a bit crook. The pedagogue
who earns his bread and fat hereabouts asked the
Department to erect a shelter shed for the,children
at the cost of a few poun s. He received a reply
to the effect that he should go and canvass the residents
of the district and collect enough to build the shed. The
instructor■in-the-middle-of-the-great-plain tried a few
big, wealthy, and religious cookies, and obtained enough
cash off them to pay for one sheet of iron. He then sat
down, and considered deeply, and swore still more deeply.
Then he decided to chuck the cadging business, and got
up some euchre parties at a bob a time. What a howl
went up from the holy inhabitants at this awful, sinful
practice ! But he persisted in his gamble, and eventually
obtained enough cash to build the shelter-shed, which
was a boon to the kiddies. Then the teacher thought that
the dog-box, hitched on to the side of the schoolhouse,
and described in official correspondence as the “Teacher’s
Residence,” would be improved and made a trifle more
pleasant for his family to live in if a verandah were put up
outside the rabbit hutch. He asked the Department
to put a verandah on the kennel, and when it
had finished being staggered—l believe it staggered right
down Collins-street—it bade him go and collect the re-
quired amount off the residents. But he replied that in
the matter of the shelter shed he had already passed the
hat till it was worn out, and after 255. worth of corres-
pondence he got a verandah worth 275. 6d., and was told
that if he gave any more trouble the Department would
feel tired.

Statistics are humorous at times, especially
when they are put together by a person with a
keen sense of duty. The current Whitaker says
that last year there were people of 23 religions in
British gaols, also there were 22 atheists, and 26
people without any views at all, and “ the reli-
gious convictions of one prisoner could not be
ascertained, as he was unfortunately suffering
from delirium tremens .” There is a monstrous
conscientiousness about the publication which,
out of 21,580 prisoners, carefully remanded that
solitary object who was too busy running across
the ceiling of his mind in chase of imaginary
beetles to state his views. Yet it might have just
put him down as “ Snakes, 1.”

It is all very well for Australia to set up a certain
sentimental standard.—Argus, 30/4/W.
Let him who loves the black man go and see, as we have
seen,
The big buck nigger ruffling with the white !
Let him go gently with a gun, or carver, sleeved and keen,
And let him watch his women well at night;
For then there prowls a pestilential travesty of man,
God’s image—black, or white-and-ebon blent,
Who loves the touch of snowy flesh —and touches, if he
can—
Nor minds the sentiment 1
Let him who wants the nigger go and watch him toiling,
slow,
With one black wage for black and white alike ;
And see, as we have seen, spilt scarlet splashed upon the
snow,
Where white men saw foul wrong, nor failed to strike.
Let him once watch the bloodhound-mob go silent through
the town,
And see stacked pine, for one fell purpose meant,
Where grim white men with tears of rage go white-faced
up and down—
And scorn White sentiment 1
Let,him but think on cursing crowds and waves of flame
that break
On such a sight as comes to one in dreams —
Ofhow the hounded nigger gapes all slobbering at the
stake,
And, as he struggles, shouts, and writhes... and
screams.
Let him go ask those silent men with blood upon their
hands
What deed called forth a deed so violent,
There let him ask what dreadful crime such punishment
demands,
Nor prate of sentiment !
Let him go east and west and see the boundless hate of
man
For GOD in mingled clay—and pity GOD !
Lethim go to and fro and know the race beneath its ban—
In black and brown and tan—the outcast clod
With ashen face, with purple lips and yellow, wolfish eyes 1
To one great end our energies are bent:
To keep our children’s blood untouched 1 And Pat, all
wild, decries
Such trashy sentiment 1
Here is the standard of our race—white women for our
men 1
White children all across our farthest lands ;
The white man’s hand and brain to toil, all time, with
plough or pen
And, white men all, with rifles in their hands !
For this Fat’s citadels of greed for ages have been stormed,
And sweat and tears and endless labor spent
To keep Australia’s children free, blood-pure and
undeformed—
A noble sentiment !
vie.
FRANK HENTY

The income figures—and most of the others—are easily
the largest in the history of this great society. In con-
u.ction with many Australian institutions, the present
i tea is that they do very well if they get back to the
figures they showed before “the boom” burst in the
"arly ’nineties. But so continuous has been the progress of
this society even in the worst times that last year, as
shown above, it had a total income of £2,932,755, while
in 1890 the income amounted to but £1,684,661. So there
has been an increase of 75 per cent, upon the income of
even the boom period.
That, of course, refers to the Ordinary Depart-
ment alone, the Industrial being a sprout thrown off
only during the past two years. This Industrial Depart-
ment is being financed by the Ordinary Department,
from which in two years it has drawn no less than
£18,741. The society publishes only a general balance-
sheet ; therefore, it is impossible to tell from its state-
ments for any one year what the new concern has cost.
Tnere is something wrong with that. Policy-holders are
e titled to have kept before them such information as
will enable them to tell the results of experiments like
this. If the Industrial Department issued its own
balance-sheet—as the Industrial Department does in other
concerns running the two—the advances made by the
Ordinary Department would represent capital ; and the
Ordinary Department would show the amounts as invest-
ments ; or, if it was doubtful about the thing turning
out profitably, it could write the amounts off. The sys
tern at present adopted looks too much like smothering
the record of the cost of the new business.
Rejecting the amount charged to the Ordinary
Department to cover the deficit on the Industrial De-
partment’s operations, the expense rate shows up with
that of recent years like this
Expenses Compared with Total Income.

But the year’s experience is really better than that; for
the proportion of new business' gathered in must be
taken into account, and 1906 was a particularly fruitful
year. Charging renewal premiums with 7 per' cent, of
their total, and consideration for annuities with 3 per
cent., we get £121,645 as the cost of attending to those
partsofthe business. That leaves £138,124 as the cost
of the new business; and for that amount £164,250 of
premium income has been got in, so that this new
business only cost 84 T per cent, of the premiums, as
against just under 89 per cent, in the previous
year, and 91 per cent. 10 years ago. When, a couple of
years ago, the management was bombarding the policy-
holders with reasons why it should be allowed to spread
itself out over South Africa and Britain, it was said that
it was so much more difficult and so much more costly to
get business in Australia now than it used to be. Yet 55
per cent, more new business was done last year than 10
years ago, and, in spite of that large increase, the cost
(.reckoning on the basis adopted) is very nearly 7 per cent,
less. The A.M.P. management has very few things to be
less proud of than the “arguments” with which it car-
ried its point over that extension matter. As to that ex-
tension, by the way, the business is still hung up, pend-
ing Gerald R. Campbell’s appeal to the Privy Council.
The interest-earnings naturally show little
change, though the downward movement is persistent:—

Part of the improvement during the past two vears com-
pared with those immediately behind them was due to
severe writing-down of the depreciation reserve, which
stood at £227,155 in 1904, and is now only £114,987.
Meantime, however, though there have been these appa-
rent losses on or shrinkages in securities, considerable
surpluses have been realised on sales of foreclosed proper-
ties, and these have been added to ordinary income, and
about a corresponding amount has been written off fore-
closed properties on the other side. The amounts thus
written off (and shown in the first table above) are apart
from amounts written off the Depreciation reserve. The
matter of interest earnings is, of course, bound up with
the character of the investments. There has been a
steady increase in the proportion of public securities held
during recent years. This table shows the distribution
of the assets:—
