From the pure insurance point of view, the steady reduc-
tion of policy loans is an excellent feature ; and the rapid
growth of public and municipal assets, while it has meant
lower earnings, also means vastly greater security ; for
these assets are backed by the whole wealth of the
country.

The expenses thus for the first year amounted to
<6311 Bs. 7d. per cent, of the total income ; for the past
year £126 18s. Bd. was spent to get every £IOO of pre-
miums in this department. The reduction is large,
but the rate is still appalling ; evidently the existing
organisation, which it was said would be immediately
available for the services of this new' department, has
not counted for much. Quite apart from management,
commissions and agents’ salaries alone have absorbed
£12,917, or over 69 per cent, of the total premiums. If
a radical improvement is not shown within the
next year or so, the question will arise whether,
so far from the existing organisation being a benefit,
it is not an actual handicap; for it is at least
conceivable that the experience gained in and by the
A.M.P. in the ordinary department may actually dis-
qualify it for the entirely dissimilar work of the industrial
department. In any case, the A.M.P. knows now the
difference between working' in a field where its prestige
brought business to it almost automatically, and in a field
where it finds others much better known than itself, and
where practically every line has to be battled for.

“Fluffy” from Perth: “The A.M.P. is a
society for which I have always had something approach-
ing affection. Thirty years ago I reverently conned the
pages of the annual report. One of our ‘ pictures ’ was a
framed advt. of its classic group, indicating the care the
old institution had for its members. The motto, ‘ Amicus
Certus In Re Incerta,’ was my first Latin. Now notice
comes that the annual meeting is to be held, and I am
reminded that, in the interval of 30 years, a tinge of dis-
appointment somehow mingles with my affection. The
A.M.P. is big, of course. But in becoming so big it does
not seem so great. Perhaps I see it with different eyes,
and it is unchanged. Twenty years ago, when I first took
out a policy, the ‘ mutual ’ idea fascinated me.
Nothing seems more fallacious to-day. The policy-
holders have least say of all. The directors—good,
easy men—solemnly present an annual report written
by the officers. The officers are the beginning 1 and end.
The policy-holders come somewhere in the middle ; but
they are entirely surrounded, I almost wrote circum-
vented, by officers. We are invited to vote. Yes ! And
this year two directors are returned unopposed. There
was a crackling of thorns under the pot 18 months ago
when the London office business was afoot. The officers
were aroused. Bigness means more salaries. The officers
were bound to work for bigness, even for bloatedness. I
had a kind message from a resident secretary to come
and discuss the question of my vote with him. I
thought of American methods of insurance, and smiled on
the wrong side of my mouth. Is it too much to suggest
Reform in the A.M.P. ? The officers will not like change,
the directors will be grieved, and I am afraid the policy-
holders, so scattered, so little used to interfering in their
own business, will be difficult to rouse. But is it not a
farce, to offer the policy-holders the choice of Sydney
citizens for the directorate? I have every respect for
Sydney citizens, but individually they are unknown to
me. ‘ The Hon- A. W. Meeks, M.L.0.,’ and ‘James Burns,
Esq.,’ who are to be re-elected unopposed, are only names
to me. The election of directors for my own State
would be a different matter ; but I get no'chance here.
The local directors are appointed by the head office ; that
is, by the officers. I think I would favor the election of
the chief directors by the local directors, if the local
directors were elected by the policy-holders. The pre-
sent system is absurd. Personally, too, I favor the head
office of the Society being shifted from Sydney. I hear
that the largest number of policies is held in Victoria.
If this were so, it might indicate Melbourne as a suitable
place for the head office. But Adelaide would do as well.
Some change of the sort would lead to new ideas being
introduced at the head of affairs. I repeat that I have a
sincere affection for the society, but 1 know that it is
now a bureaucracy instead of a democracy. lam anxious
to see good officers well paid, but not to'have the officers
in complete control. A reform in the method of appoint-
ing the directorate appears to me to be the best method
of saving the A.M.P. for its members.”
* * * *
Kalgoorlie (W.A.), though it jobbed away its
street-tramway rights, kept its lighting in its own
hands, and the results are good enough to probably make
it wonder how it came to let go the other business. Al-
together it only raised £22,500 for lighting, and, of that
amount, £3OOO wasn’t wanted for the purpose, and was
transferred to another municipal account, leaving £19,500
as the net capital in the business. Yet, after writing
down £26,455 out of profits to cover depreciation, the
works still stand at £45,839. In other words, £72,294
has been spent on works, the amount being raised as
follows:
Capital (loans) .. .. £19,500
Profits 49,025
Sundry creditors .. .. 3,769
Total .. £72,294
Sundry creditors total £9095, but there are stocks and
liquid assets amounting to £5326. The profit shown is
after paying interest. Kalgoorlie therefore has the bene-
fits of an electric-light business which has cost on capital
account over £70,000 ; yet, since it owned the business
itself, and therefore put the profits back into the concern,
instead of paying the n away in dividends, it has to pay
interest on only £19,500. As mentioned just now, de-
preciation provided out of profit amounts to £26,455.
This amount, instead of being represented by a cash re-
serve for the replacement of works, is apparently passed
to the general municipal account, where it is represented
by sun try assets—which isn’t fair to the electric-light-
ing enterprise. The object of making a charge on
revenue to cover depreciation is that there may be the
funds in hand to replace the works when they are worn
out; and if, when the works are worn out and hare
to be replaced, the money isn’t there, and can only be
got by drawing on another account—that is,' by
getting in more capital—then the object of the standing
charge for depreciation is destroyed. Kalgoorlie’s sink-
ing funds, though highly respectable (on a total of
£33,000 borrowed they amount to £11,841), are also
lumped together, and the sinking fund is derived from
“loan rates”; and that means that the sinking fund
contribution isn’t a direct charge on the electric-lighting
department’s revenue. Why a municipality, when it
goes in for municipal trading, can’t make each business
stand on its own bottom, so that it retains its own assets
and reserves, and is charged with everything in the way
of expenditure that has to be provided, isn’t clear. Apart
from that, the Kalgoorlie municipal enterprise shows a
fine record.

The dividends are stated approximately. A regular 8 per
cent, is paid—this is called interest, and charged direct
to profit and loss—and the shareholders then further
divide 20 per cent, of the premiums between themselves
and the policyholders. The practice of this office
differs from that of most other Australian fire
insurance offices inasmuch as the latter deduct
the policyholders’ 10 per cent, bonus or rebate,
and show their premiums net. This co. began
originally (1872) with a capital actually paid of £6250.
Gradually, however, by a very careful selection of risks,
and to some extent by moderating the dividends, such
large reserves have been accumulated that the directors
have felt themselves justified in transferring £56,250 to
capital, and another £12,500 is now to be transferred ;
this will raise the paid capital to £75,000, or 15s. per
share, leaving a liability of ss. per share. On the original
paid capital the return is equal to about 147 J per cent.—
remarkable figures, especially in view of the fact that
very good dividends have been distributed all along. The
explanation is that the office pushes the selection of risks
to the limit—a policy which several cos. with a large
initial capital could not simultaneously pursue, simply
because the supply of that class of risk isn’t large enough
to go round. Here are some of the Australian Mutual’s
figures covering 10 years :
Ex- Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses, penses. Rate. Rate.
1897.. £29,335 £4687 £12,345 16’0 p.c. 42-1 p.c.
1900.. 33,888 8131 13,765 24'0 „ 40'7 „
1905.. 36,088 7149 15,009 19’8 „ 41'6 „
1906.. 36,137 8535 15,420 23'6 „ 427 „
1907.. 36,358 7471 15,694 20’6 „ 42'6 „
The loss rate is about 50 p.c. below the Australasian aver-
age. Of course, the expense rate is higher—the office's
policy makes that inevitable. The financial position is
very strong. Investments total £205,489 ; on the average
amount of the investments during the year the return
was at the rate of £4 3s. per cent. The income from this
source (£8369) was almost enough to pay the dividend
and shareholders’ bonus, leaving the under-writing
profit to go to reserves for the security of policy-
holders. The reserves (apart from capitalised reserves)
are equal to 378 per cent, of the year’s premiums ; the
average for 22 leading British offices for 1905 was only
1487 per cent., and that was the best these offices had
shown for many years. Accepting premiums, then, as
the measure of risks, the Australian office offers by way
of reserves much more than twice the security offered by
the average of the leading British offices.

The profits keep at about the mean summer level. There
was nothing in the accounts to explain the bad slump in
the previous half-year’s figures ; and the fact that, if the
West drinks less in winter, its summer thirst is not at all
worn out, must be comforting to shareholders. Besides
the amount shown above as written off, an average of
over £4OOO a year is charged direct to profit and loss for
wear and tear—£2lsß for the past half-year. But while this
amount is written off plant and machinery and furniture,
brewery lands and buildings (£73,292), hotel properties
(£170,594), and a few smaller assets are in at cost. Some
of these properties were bought in the earlier days of Perth,
before the great gold output had tremendously enhanced
values ; but most of them are quite recent purchases.
Compare the two chief lines now and five years ago :
Brewery, land Hotel
and buildings, properties.
March, 1902 .. .. .. £49,740 £49,045
March, 1907 .. ■ .. .. 73,292 170,594
Last five years’ purchases £23,552 £121,549
A few years ago the co. actually wrote up the value of
some of the properties. It is quite possible that there has
been such a large increase in the value of the older
properties that no writing-off for depreciation is called
for ; but unless that increase is so great as to counter-
balance the possible depreciation of recent purchases,
something certainly ought to be written off the latter out
of revenue. It is true that the rents show a return of a
shade over 8 per cent. But that high return doesn’t
necessarily mean that the book values are low : it may
mean that the book values are so uncertain that a
large part of the difference between the 8 per cent,
and the ordinary investment return (say 5 per cent.)
ought to be set aside to provide against a possible
slump. This is the weak point in the co. Prac-
tically the whole capital and reserves are in premises,
plant, and properties; book debts and advances are
counterbalanced by overdraft (£34,126) and other debts ;
and the question is, are the values stable ? If thev are
not, then something ought to come off them, andlhat
would make the profits look smaller.

Hitherto, besides subscribers, there have been permanent
and paid-up members. The permanent capital, 12 months
ago, was £31,250 in £25 shares ; these shares have now
been exchanged for paid-up shares of a like total amount.
Apart from £2517 credited to subscribers, the capital is
£72,857. Deposits total £62,744—a small advance on the
previous year’s figures ; but, in proportion to capital, the
amount is by no means excessive. Under the excellent
Queensland Building Societies Act (this is one of the
very few societies registered according to the provisions
of that Act) the borrowing may go up to, blit not exceed,
three times the amount of the capital, and none of the
borrowed money may be at call. There are sundry credi-
tors for £ll2B and a mortgage of £BOO ; the mortgage has
been reduced at the rate of £BOO a year for the past four
years, so that presumably it will now disappear alto-
gether. So far (though that is a good movement that was
just mentioned) the figures don’t show any very striking
change for the better ; yet there is a change; Five years
ago the advances and deposits at interest returned £s lss.
per cent, gross ; now, partly no doubt on account of the
amount written off nearly three years ago, the return is
£6 3s. 4d. per cent. For several years the advances fell
away ; now the tide has turned, though last year’s total
went only from £120,537 to £122,958. And no doubt,
though Brisbane, from all appearances, won’t have to do
any house-building for a good many years to come, having
years ago pretty heavily discounted the future in that
respect, the outlook from the point of view of such a
society as this is much improved. That also seems to be
the local opinion ; for this society’s shares have lately
stood higher in the market than at any previous time
since 1893.

Aron Hirsch and Son’s copper statistics for 1906
are published ; the Hirsehes and their associates are the
biggest metal buyers in the world, and their statistics are
about the most elaborate. The matter, on their figures,
may be put this way :
Production. Consumption.
1903 .. 574,740 tons .. 605,686 tons
1904 .. 640,935 „ .. 685,378 „
1905 .. 697,845 ~ .. 752 617
1906 .. 736,711 „ .. 788,008 ”
Every year, therefore, there has been a large excess of
consumption over production. When the public is told
that last year (other years are not mentioned) the pro-
duction of the metal fell short of demands by nearly
50,000 tons, it jumps at once to the conclusion that that
sort of thing can’t go on, but that meantime prices must
boom. The fact that probably 75 per cent, of the copper
that is used isn’t wasted, but is put into articles which
when they are worn out, are still of value as copper and’
as copper go back to the potto be used over again-that
important fact is overlooked. When copper commands
£IOO or thereabouts a ton, the inducement to turn the
old copper-pot into money is pretty severe. And that
has to be taken into account when the boomster men-
supplies the eonsumption of c °PPer is outstripping the

Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, continues to open up satis-
factorily, and looks as if it still had a long life ahead of it.
On December 31 the ore reserves gave a supply of 51
months, assaying 545. 5d., and since then the extraction
has been 455. 3d. per ton. The ore reserves do not include
the two bottom levels, which are looking well, but are
not much developed yet'. Manag'er Nicolson appears to
have got a good substitute for underground manager
MacDermott, who has gone to the Sons of Gwalia ; any-
way, things continue in the same style at the Ivanhoe
* • * »
North Boulder, Kalgoorlie, recently recon-
structed with an assessment of 2s. per share, has taken
over the Oroya Extended, long known as the Kalgoorlie
Mint and Iron King. The price was 80,000 fully-paid
10s. shares. The co. is in 400,000 shares, of which 87,000
were in reserve, so that no increase of nominal capital is
needed. Up to the end of 1899 the co. treated nearly
30,000 tons for a yield of 40,0000 z., and paid divs. of
£27,500, from the Friday lode, on the extreme w estern
boundary of the lease. This lode then passed into the
Associated, and has lain idle for eight years, although
numerous offers have been made to successive Associated
managers to work the ground on tribute. The same lode
was worked by an open cut in the Oroya by Capt.
Parkes in 1897-1899, and yielded 21,0000 z. from 49,500
tons, resulting in one div. of £9491. Recently,
owing to the persistence of would-be tributors, a
prospecting shaft was started near the east boun.
dary in the Associated, and the lode proved at
100 ft. to be 3ft. wide worth 250 e. per ton, and at 200 ft.
it is equally rich, where tested, and carries telluride.
Whether the lode continues in the Associated, or passes
into the old Kalgoorlie Mint and Iron King further south,
is not yet proved. Gerald Browne, late attorney for the
North Boulder, held the opinion that the Mint gets it ;
hence the present amalgamation. Australian share-
holders have three months from March 27 to come into the
new co.
• • * *
Kalgurli (W.A.), so far as one can learn, is all
right below, the fall in profit being attributed to re-
duced tonnage treated, and the consequent higher costs.
The fact of the matter is things have been run at too
high pressure, with the inevitable result that machinery
could not stand the strain. The mill engine has been
overhauled and auxiliary engines used meanwhile ; and,
until a thorough overhaul is completed, the tonnage is
likely to remain low. Still, on the last two months’re-
duced profits, the co. is earning £54,000 against quarterly
divs. of 7s. 6d., or £45,000, so it can continue the present
distribution. The mine is a very difficult one to sample,
as the ore lenses run up to 200 ft. in length, and, in
places, up to nearly half that in width, and values vary
greatly. Ore reserves, on December 31, were sufficient
for five years’ supply. The value was then given as 60s.
Since then the extraction has been January 595.,
February 605., and March 595. ; while costs have been
21s. Id., 225. 4d., and 225. Bd., repairs of breakages
accounting for the increase.
* * * *
Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, now thrown back on
the hands of the original owners, is to be vigorously
developed, and equipped with a 10-head mill. The main
shaft is to be sunk from 300 ft. to 450 ft., and anew level
will be put in at the latter depth. There is a lot of ore of
payable grade in the oxidised zone, and this will keep a
mill running for a considerable time. Of course, the
mine will not be worked on the big scale that would have
been adopted had 8., M. and Op. taken it in hand, but still
it will be handled vigorously. The price asked was
£45,000 cash, and one-fifteenth interest, while the adjoin-
ing owners demanded another £13,000 cash. This meant
£58,000 cash down, and as much more would have been
required to open up and equip the property—a pretty
tall order. The owners reckon that the property
will never be offered again at so low a figure, and are not
worrying at the failure of the deal.
* * * *
Queensland Menzies(W. A. )isbeing straightened
up by Manager Duncan. With the idea of economising,
late manager Ferguson shifted everything from the old
mine to the new (the Goodenough), and, as much of the
material was bad, the result was unsatisfactory. Duncan
has appointed an entirely new staff, and is erecting new
head-gear, and trying to put everything in order. But
unless the Brisbane directors give him a freer hand than
they gave his predecessor, Duncan will be too hampered
to g'et good results. Whether the mine will ever become
payable it is impossible to say. The original owners
rooted out a lot of rich patches, but “ Boulder ” Hamil-
ton rejected the mine after sampling. Nevertheless, too
little work has been done to utterly condemn it.
Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), which has been
rather under a cloud for some time past, is looking up
again. The ore reserves, which, a year ago, were esti-
mated at 16,000 tons, worth 50s. a ton, are now stated to
be 25,000 tons of about the same value. Monthly costs on
this mine are very high, running up to nearly 40s. a ton,
and are not published regularly. The scrip is very little
dealt in, but shares are nominally about 3s. The co.
paid its last div. in April, 1905, and since then it has been
struggling, owing to the impoverishment of the reef in
the sulphide zone.
* * * *
Tindalls, Cue (W.A.), about four miles north of
Great Fingall, is pushing on development, but very' little
information can be gleaned about the results. When the
co. was recently' floated, the lode at 550 ft. was given as
20ft. wide, and worth 20dwt. Since then the level has
been opened up to an extent of about 500 ft., but pub-
lished reports say nothing of width or'value. One bore-
hole on the south end of the property has located
nothing, and a second is down 200 ft.," also without
encountering values. The spirits of Bull holders are
kept up by' cables to the effect that important develop-
ments are expected shortly ; but nothing eventuates.
The property may be a good one, but is fully valued at
£450,000 in £1 shares. There does not seem to be any
market for the scrip, and there is not likely' to be till
more precise information is available.
* * * *
Whim Well, Pilbarra (W.A.), has just sold its
first consignment of copper ore in Liverpool—the first,
that is, under present control. The parcel consisted of
99 tons, assaying 26‘94 per cent, copper ; and it realised
£2536, or £25 12s. 4d. a ton. The co. holds 260 acres
about 50 miles east of Roeburne, and 15 miles from the
coast. There is a hill of copper ore on the property, and
it is said that it will not be necessary'to do any mining
for years, as enough ore can be quarried out, even if the
property is worked on a large scale. Since 1891 ore has
been sent aw'ay' at intervals to smelters, but very'little
since 1902. In all, 5006 tons have been disposed of for
£99,901. The mine is now held by a London co. with a
capital of £150,000 in £1 shares, and it is said that 300 tons
of ore will be despatched monthly. If it realises as much
as the first lot, the monthly' output will be over £7500.
The average value of the ore shipped previously was £2O
a ton ; but that was when prices were much lower. The
mine was formerly' held by' the Bank of New Zealand,
which accepted £IOO,OOO in fully'-paid shares for the
property in 1900.

An old and experienced correspondent puts for-
ward a better view of Barrambie than this paper has been
inclined to entertain Barrambie Ranges, Murchison
(VV.A.), promises better than most places in that fickle
goldfield. The gold occurs in rich dabs along the line of
reef, already' proved for a length of 1000 ft., ana the
cupidity' of men employed has already led to several
prosecutions and convictions. Sly grog selling in that
part of the country is rampant, and the morals of a cer-
tain push are sulphurous. One lady', who was recently
fined £3O for using the corkscrew surreptitiously',
elected to take it out, and is the heroine of the
camp w'hich she infested. A jamboree to celebrate
her entrance into captivity ended in her shanty being
burned down, but nearly enough crude gold was
forthcoming next day to build a full-fledged pub;
and yet mines are not numerous thereabouts.-
Mannger Aldridge, now on the .Barrambie mine, has
taken vigorous action to stop gola-stealing there, and
police from Nannine have already chased some of the
undesirables out of the district. Aldridge was one of the
first managers on the Murchison ; he bossed the Island
Lake Austin for a Melbourne crowd, 15 years ago. He
has control of the Mainland Consols as well as of the
Barrambie. The former is a show abandoned by Kaufman
after an expenditure of something like £IOO,OOO. It used
to be wonderfully rich—one of the leases, the Last
Chance, dollied out at the rate of 3000 oz. to the ton. G.


A new improved drill maker and sharpener is
superseding the somewhat expensive method of sharpen-
ing; drills by hand. The machine will be welcomed by all
mining coy's, because the men are able to sharpen up to
about 140 drills an hour without trouble, consequently
increasing the output and reducing the cost which secures
to the mines substantial benefit. The makers are Messrs.
Word Bros., of San Francisco, who have supplied their
machines to all leading mines throughout North and South
America, and in other countries. W. & J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne and Sydney, have been appointed the sole Aus-
tralian agents. The machine has the distinction of being
a drill maker as well as drill sharpener.*

Two patent portable traction scoop engines
are being built to the order of Messrs. McLelland and
Cooper for cutting the main irrigation channels in the
Waranga Basin District of Victoria. The engines will be
placed on opposite sides of the channels and will pull and
tip the scoops in their own direction, and so make the
channel and form the embankment. There are two
engines of the vertical compound type, and, along with
winding drums, friction clutches, worm gearing, and
other appliances, are mounted on a mild steel framework
carriage which projects above and forward over a portion
of the locomotive boiler. The boiler is mounted on
driving and steering wheels. The main haulage engine
is 160 I.H.P. and the smaller one 35 I.H.P. The Austral
Otis Engineering Company Ltd., South Melbourne, are
the manufacturers.* [d.]

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discriDtion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Engineer Frew, who built the Chillagoe line, is
bustling on the Almaden-Georgetown railway, and the
Einasleigh people are hoping that the end of the year will
see the rails well out towards their mine. But with so
many railway proposals on hand at the same time in
Queensland, it does not appear as if any construction
records will be broken. Commissioner Thallon feels
cocksure that Cloncurry will be visited by an engine by'
the end of December, and he may be right; but he is a
bit optimistic. Labor and sleepers will be joint troubles
in Queensland for some time to come.
When that engine shrieks itself into Cloncurry
at Christmas time (if it does), those who have bought
into freeholds, and wild cats, and, lastly, good copper
shows, will learn how sadly they have miscalculated the
position. Even if there was a half-hourly service to
Townsville, a vast percentage of the shows in this dis-
trict of vast distances would still find that “ all they want
is rail connection.” Asa matter of fact, when they do
get to work it will not be long before they will also
realise that they' want sulphide ore, and unless the pubi c
takes a commonsense view of tne position, Cloncurry will
be unmentionable to the investor. The railway to Clon-
curry will, like the smelter on an undeveloped mine,
show for the time the emptiness of things. And the void
in many’ cases will be a painful one, for the Australian
speculator swallowed Cloncurry stocks at high prices ; and
now the London promoters are waiting for him to cough
the undigested morsel up. Doubtless in the course of a
few months London will have something new in the way
of North Queensland floats to dose Australia with.
Stated on good authority that Bewick-Moreing
are thirsting for Etheridge (N.Q.) propositions, and it
looks as if this much-discussed and neglected field will
get a little bit more than its railway this time. On paper,
the railway project, if carried out by the Chillagoe Cos.
with as much of its own money as possible, looks a very
good thing for that co. The old Einasleigh Fr eh old Cos.
narrowdy escaped the right to construct the Hue from
Almaden, through Einasleigh, out to Georgetown ; but a
purchase of the line by the Government was not then
thought of. Now the Chillagoe Cos. has purchased the
Einasleigh Co.’s survey, and that will help on Engineer
Frew’s work.
One thing certain about the Etheridge is that
there is no dearth of sulphide. Primarily, it is a gold-
field—a field of refractory ores—but, to those who know
it well, the gold is only one phase. Silver-lead, Copper,
tin and wolfram are the industrial metals which make
the Etheridge railway an interesting proposition ; but it
will be w’ell, before the public is presently dragged into
an Etheridge boom, to consider who are promoting the
business. Thar. Cloncurry deserves a railway need not be
doubted; that it also deserves a certificate as an over-
boomed place will later on be fully realised. That the
Etheridge line is justified by possibilities likewise cannot
be doubted ; and it now remains for the public to see
that it does not allow itself to be caught in an Etheridge
boom.
Not much is heard, in all the North Queensland
boom talk, of the Einasleigh. Refloated in London recently,
an Australasian office has been opened in Adelaide. Probably
no mine has been so much reported on—from Richard
Daintree, Government Geologist in the ’sixties, who ac-
quired the property, down to G. H. Blakemore of Oobar,
and Stewart and Hill of London. Certainly the Einasleigh
people need not make much of a song—with the railway
through to the mine, the lode, if it is as large and high-
grade as good men say it is, should do all the singing that
is required.
Another Einasleigh Cos. is shortly to come into ex-
istence. Two blocks, both of which are supposed to con-
tain the big Einasleigh lode, have fallen into the hands of
syndicates headed by R. S. Archer, Robt. Philp, G. C.
Willcocks, W. H. Woodhead, Wm. Maxwell and James
Stewart. Dr. Jack has been sent up to report upon the
properties.
• • * *
Dr. Jack’s report on Mt. Cannindah (Q.) is
not altogether the scrappy, inconclusive thing that some
of his recent reports have been, no doubt because this co.
could afford to pay him to take II days over the examina-
tion. Yet the report is a long way from being conclusive.
He values the metal in the mine down to No. 2 level at
£634,565. Also he mentions that the mining, milling,
and smelting costs were £23 2s. per ton, according to the
last half-yearly report. But that was only for copper in
an average-grade matte. What of the costs of convert-
ing, refining, and realising? There are about sdwt.
gold and lOoz. silver per ton of 50 p.c. matte ; and
that will go some way, but not all the way, towards
paying refining charges. Freight of matte to the
coast alone at present means about £lO per ton of copper.
Converting would probably cost £8 more. (In the half-
year to September 30, 1906, in which the O.K. started to
handle its own matte, it produced 352 tons of blister
copper, and the converting costs were £3816, or nearly
£ll per ton.) In view of the gold and silver contents,
add£6 more for refining and freight; total £24, or more
than the costs on the mine. To arrive at the £634,565
mentioned, copper is taken by Dr. Jack at £95 per ton.
He estimates 6627 tons of metal. With the market at £95
and costs at £47, there would he a profit of £4B per ton,
or in all £318,096. Put it in another way :
Value of Probable Per
Copper. Profit. Share.
£95 £318,096 17/8
85 1 251,826 13/11
75 185,556 10/2
65 .. .. .. 119,286 6/6
But out of that must come part at least of the cost of
equipment ; for present plant is insignificant. Beyond
the No. 2 level Dr. Jack doesn’t go. But the possibilities
beyond that may be very well used as a set-off against
the inevitable losses in concentration and also the
possibility of the cost-figures given above being too low.
The cost of mining' is officially given as 6s. 4d. per ton of
ore, or £l6 4s. per ton of copper. That is remarkably
low for a low-grade (2£ per cent.) ore. At Wallaroo and
Moonta it cost £54 10s. per ton of copper last year ; at
Mt. Lyell, for the half-year to September last, it cost
6s. OJd. per ton of ore—at Lyell, with its great open-cuts.
But, making allowances of this sort, Cannindah, on Logan
Jack’s word, is a bigger thing than it had been reckoned
by most people. *
Position with regard to Mt. Hector (Q.) is un-
certain. It is alleged that, while G. P. Doolette would
not take Glassford Creek without Hector and preferred
them together, he is willing to take on Hector on a six
months’ option. The chances are that, though the
shareholders threw Doolette’s amalgamated proposal out
with so much scorn, they will accept the new offer. And
they had better grab it with both hands. They profess
to believe that they have no end of a mine; but they
don’t show any eagerness to push Dr. Jack’s report before
the public. And the smelting returns are very small
potatoes up to now. The proposal is to give them £1 per
share in cash and four shares in anew 300,000 share co.
Doolette has left for England, where he will place this, if
he handles it at all.
* * * *
According to Home Secretary Airey, the Kid-
ston Government has made up its mind to supply one
custom smelter at Cloncurry and another at Townsville.
The Bulletin wishes Queensland better luck than every
other State-smelting enterprise has had in Australia. An
assured and regular ore-supply is the first essential of a
smelter, and in practice the only way to get a regular
supply at a remunerative price is to own or control a
mine. If the State wants to make a success of smelting,
it will either be obliged to have its own mines to turn to,
or in issuing leases it will have to reserve certain rights
to an ore supply.

tell how sick Charters Towers keeps

The shrinkage in calls is almost as unsatisfactory as the
shrinkage in dividends; for in many cases it means,
not that success, but that something like despair, has
come. Carrington United (which is going to put up
reverbs, to deal with its copper), Pfeiffers (the most
profitable plaything of the share-dealer lately), and
Brilliant Central are the brighter patches in »
gloomy situation. Mention of share-dealing recalls
that to the end of April this year the local Ex-
change recorded transactions in 157,021 shares, as
against 375,256 for the first four months of last year.
Mills United for the six months to the middle of April
had a revenue of £94,863, and a profit of £46,041; the 3s.
divs. with tax absorbed £47,250, so they were not wholly
earned. The stone treated was 24,724.t0n5. Known ore
reserves are said to be “probably the same as at the
commencement of the term, viz., 120,000 tons; but the
average value is slightly less.” The market says the
shares are worth 17s. There have been times when on
those figures the market could have pVoved to its own
complete satisfaction that it was throwing the shares
away at 275.

Ambrose Silver-Copper Mines, Ltd., privately
floated in a co. of 120,000 shares of ss. each, takes over a
show situated on Gowrie Creek, 90 miles north of Charters
Towers. A shaft is down 130 ft. on the Spray lode, which
carries high-grade silver-copper ore. From this shaft 46 tons
of ore netted £1992. The bottom shows 3250 z. silver and
20 per cent, copper, worth £52 12s. 6d. per ton of ore. A
shaft is down 110 ft. on the Lass o’ Gowrie lode, the sink
bulking lloz. silver and 28 per cent, copper. Eight tons
netted £220 16s. The Silver Spray lode is said to be
equally rich, but has not yet been tested with a shipment.
The Spray lode has been opened by shafts over a length of
lode of 1900 ft.; and in width it runs to about 2ft. 6in.
Reduction plant is to be installed immediately. The
show was opened by Herbert Ambrose, who gets £3OOO
casli and 12,000 paid shares.
* * * •
Mungana (Q.) should be making money out of
ore sent to Chillagoe smelters. A month’s run gave 32
tons copper and 55500 z. silver from the copper furnace,
and 156 tons lead bullion containing 11,6500 z. silver from
the lead furnace —roughly about £BOOO worth of copper,
lead, and silver for the month. There is no knowing
what the expenses were. Of lead 976 tons were smelted,
and the return pans out about 15 per cent, lead and 12oz.
silver, with some copper in matte. Mungana lead ore
thus yielded about £6 per ton, and the copper ore nearly
twice as much ; but the profit depends on the cost of
mining and smelting—and Chillagoe does the smelting ;
and Chillagoe wants money. One shilling a share means
£25,000 to Mungana Cos. ; so the profit on £BOOO worth of
metals produced in a month won’t go far per share. Still
the co. had a handy credit balance at the last clean-up of
accounts, and a profit of £2OOO odd a month would make
a Is. dividend in 12 months —which is a little in a long
while. It is probably the look of the ledger that has
prompted English booming of these shares; but, con-
sidered as an investment, profits need to be more rapid.
* * * *
Manager Palmer, of Broken Hill Junction
North, in his zeal to provide plant to treat the co.’s ore,
and ore to be treated, seems to have swept through the
co.’s funds like a tornado, and would have been
going still had not the Board cried a temporary
halt. Development work that might have waited
awhile was carried out with great vigor, a tremend-
ous amount of surface construction work was put
in hand, and the mill was overhauled and altered and
added to until it hardly knew itself. When the mill
settles down to sober work there is certainly plenty of
ore at the three bottom levels to feed it on. Palmer’s
policy has at least assured that. The 1137 ft. is almost
ready to go into at any'moment, and the completion of
the central mullocking system is all that is required to
allow of a large ore production.
* » « *
B.H. Block 14’s Mother Goose is the carbonate
zone, which has produced some thousands of tons during
the past half-year, and there is now more of that ore in
sight than there was six months ago. In the sulphide
levels, however, there is often a good deal of scraping
done to keep the mill going, and nowadays the rapacious
monster rarely has a real good feed. The grade of ore
broken, too, is not improving.

North Broken Hill mine is proving a nice body
of ore at the 500 ft. level, where the upper portion of the
lode now being worked at the lower levels is being
developed. There are rumors afloat that the Junction
North ore bodies, which were supposed to be dipping
into the North mine at depth, have declined to show such
disloyalty, and instead turn turtle (as Barrier lodes often
do) and skip back into the Junction North. Still, the
North mine has large bodies of ore of its own. The
Victoria Cross shaft is now about ready to allow of
development work being started there, and this will prove
whether the North ore body pitching towards the
Victoria shaft exists in depth in that property. Some
people think that it has been deflected to the westward.
It is the same problem which has so far baffled pros-
pectors at the other end of the line of lode, towards the
White Leads.
* * * *
That Lobb’s Hole mine (N.S.W.), which a
Sydney co., just formed, is to take over, has some extra-
ordinary features. Though it has been proved payable
for 540 ft., with good ore showing in one face, the vein is
on an average rather less than Ift. wide ; but about 6in.
of that has been unaltered ore averaging 28 per cent,
copper, which is equal at present price of copper to about
7oz. gold. Without being wholly worked out, down to
120 ft. this vein, taking copper at £BO per ton, has yielded
£41,000 worth of metal; and underfoot it is the same
value. Mine-engineers Horton and Freeman, who report
on the show, say that it is a true fissure, and practically
certain to continue to great depths. If it does, and the
value continues, there should be £56,000 worth of copper
in every 100 ft. of depth, taking copper at £BO. The most
striking thing about the vein is that right from the cap-
ping it has been unaltered ore, due to the fact that over-
lying it is an impervious breccia. The original
owners have worked it for 10 years under tremendous
difficulties; they have had to send their ore away
on pack-horses, which have had to climb 3000 ft. in 2J
miles. All the second-grade ore has therefore been left
on the surface ; and second-grade ore under such circum-
stances has been 6 per cent, stuff—worth over £6 per ton
at present prices. Tracks have, however, been cut, and
transport facilities are now being generally improved.
The mine shows the size of the difficulties that the
pioneer miner still has to overcome even in N.S. Wales.
The Mt. Lyell Cos. will not go on with the Blue
Tier tin racket—a hint in this direction was given in The
Bulletin a month back. Blue Tier, had the Lyell folk
been satisfied, was to have been melted into a 250,000
share co., with a working capital of £150,000. The area
to be controlled was about 1600 acres near Lottah, and
included the Australian tin mine, as well as the Blue Tier
leases. One thing can be done now by the owners—that
is to work the ground. Tin is there, but it needs to be
won, and small cos. can do the best work. Or it is not
too late for them to come to terms with Lewis, of the
Anchor, and see if he could not make a better do of his
own ground with the aid of ore from one or two of the
Blue Tier leases.
* « * *
Manager Thomas, who has had the Cybele
(Tas ) in hand, has hit that boom tin-show hard with two
telegrams. He has had the mine for months, but his
discovery is mud« only after nearly £20,000 has been
spent on plant, and after one miserable clean-up has been
obtained. Over 150 bores were put down on the
around prior to flotation, and Thomas him-
self seems, after his. appointment, to have had some
boring done. On the original boring Grayson and others
brought out the prospectus, in which tin estimates of lib.
per cub. yard were given. Thomas says the show is a
“ r otter ” and Grayson, full up with doubt, wants an
independent report. The first thing to do is to have the
bores checked, and to ascertain whether the supervision
was close and how and by whom the samples were washed
up. On this everything depends, for what gave the show
support was the apparent care taken to test the
ground in advance of flotation. If the boring was not
negligent, the show stands as before. If it was
negligent, responsibility wants to be sheeted home.
While this is going on investors can dwell on the Tas-
manian tin records that have been put up. Gladstone
and Ringarooma are pretty well water-logged; Briseis
Extended, Weldbrook and Wyniford are in much the
same way: and even the Briseis is far below original
estimates. The tally is a sorry one. Still, seeing how
the South Mt. Cameron pulled itself out of the mud,
there may be a chance for the Cybele. Someone will
have to be heroic. The co. wants a manager. Let Gray-
son take it in hand and justify himself.

T. J. Dyson, who came down from the Northern
Territory three months ago to have a loolj around on
behalf of the Northern Territory Mines, Ltd.—which has
some money it doesn’t know what to do with and some
machinery that it knows less how to find a use for—has
been rummaging Tasmania. He has picked up the old
Adelaide mine at Dundas, whose former owners have
always been beaten by water. Some of the plant now in
the N.T. will be shifted down.
“ E. 8.,” from Reedy Creek (Vic.): “ Our field
a ‘ noted call-paying-district,’ and our reefs ‘ little better
than stringers ’ 1 Listen to me. The Langridge turned
out, it is estimated, four tons of gold ; in three years,
previous to 1881, after it was considered worked-out, it
gave £23,000 in dividends. The Crown Cos. paid £30,000
in dividends. You can’t call such mines stringers. The
Golden Dyke contained antimony and gold.; and it is
only of late that they have got appliances for treating
such mineral. They are at present on the tailings, which
are giving- good results. The Empress of India and
Balmers United are only down 200 ft. The former is
estimated to have yielded 16,0000 z. to that depth ; and the
latter gave good results when worked by a co-operative
party, and is at present being developed at the 200 ft. level.
This has been a neglected goldfield for the past 25 yearsj
but in its day it was one of the richest in the State.”
That’s what’s the matter with a lot of fields. “In their
day ” they were very good ; but their day was yesterday.
Even a good goldfield can’t dig out its gold and keep it too.
But that is the last thing the old goldfield realises.
* • * *
When the directors of the Union mine, Dia-
mond Creek(Vic.), unwatered that veteran, and found that
the holes in the pump had in olden times been stuffed with
candles, they took it as a happy augury. In the Hartz
mountains the miner would have said that it was the work
of the “ little men ” —the gnomes who never do anything
but mischief. But Australians jumped to the conclusion
that this meant rich stone left in the old days—that some-
body or other had made up his mind to get the property
after the then owners had been flooded out. The Union
has, so far, however, only added another tribute to the
good workmanship of the old-timer. The good stone
was skinned as clean as a Board table usually is of the
directors’ fees that are thrown upon it. What there
was left of the reef hasn’t set the Thames afire either :
as although the bottom (500 ft.) level carries 12 inches of
stone, the gold is as poor as an average bank director’s
charity to his clerks. There is some 7dwt. stone between
Nos. 7 and 8 levels, and this ought to pay expenses and
allow the other funds to be applied towards prospecting
along the different levels. The Union deserves better
luck, for it has been kept off the share market; it has
enthusiastic backers, and its success would favorably
affect other mines in the locality.
* » * *
The Daylesford-Ajax (Vic.) flutter looks like
proving barren ground to most of the investors. Fully 25
call-makers can be counted up in the list for May. The
drain on the public for the month in calls is put down at
£BOOO ; but it isn’t anything like that, because investors
did not bite so well as promoters hoped they would.
Ballarat took up a lot of scrip, certainly, but it was on
the pool system ; and the Stock Exchange of Melbourne, by
refusing to quote promoters’ shares, hit the men who
wanted to make a market on the reputation of the Ajax
between wind and water. Flop went rates, and they have
not finished falling yet. The next stage will be that of
forfeiture, and the next, if new finds are not reported,
will be liquidation. Two mines only stand out as profit
earners at Daylesford. One of them, the Victorian
Cornish, seems to have run its profit-earning course for
the present; and the other, the Ajax, does not show its
old strength. The big battery may be telling tales, and,
if so, the next two or three crushings will be interesting.
As it. is, the mine is a fluke, for the make of stone now
being worked was not the one the management expected.
Still that leaves the co. with a second string to its bow, on
which, should the first fail, it may be able to play. The
main feature about the market in the co.’s shares is the
plenitude of sellers and the scarcity of buyers.
Someone has been playing a game with the Sir John
Franklin (Vic.), and the Argus, in its attempt to vie with
Age mining spread-eagleism, has sent out a column of
flam. Sir John Franklin is a little show up Wood’s Point
way that is trying to make both ends meet by working
quartz veins in a dyke formation. This isn’t very pro-
fitable when depth is reached, as all local records show,
though near the surface Wood’s Point mines were some of
the richest bonanzas of Victoria. Shares for months
have moved as languidly as a loafer away from a free-
lunch table, a shilling being about their top rate. Then
someone, whether in a spirit of mischief or as a fraud,
wrote a letter in an illiterate way, suggesting that it
came from a couple of hands in the mine, telling of a
hidden-up strike of stone. The letter was dropped where
the claquers of the Stock Exchange would be bound to
get it. But it was too obviously a fake. If there had
been anything in the alleged find, shares would not have
dwelt at under a shilling. Above all, miners would not
have written in the strain adopted. The whole thing was
so clumsy that even the Argus ought to have seen
through * * *
Britannia United, Ballarat, is in the death
throes, owing to expensive legal and liquidation proceed-
ings. Some months ago compensation of about £2COO
was paid to a Methodist church for undermining the
ecclesiastical property, and the fight to a finish with the
Last Chance Cos. entailed a lawyer’s bill of nearly as
much. The mine was then taken over for the “ benefit ”
of the creditors, who, however, like the shareholders,
seem to be left out in the wet. Presently the plant and
everything that is not a legal document will be auctioned
off, and one of the most promising shows in Ballarat East
will be an object lesson to mining cos. not to go fooling
with the Law.

“Ralph Tinderwood” writes from Maoriland : —
“ The attempted resurrection of Mt. Deddick (Vic.) recalls
memories. I was a mining reporter in Mt. Deddick’s
previous days, and was turned on to those parts several
times ; also I worked there underground. The Deddick
country was the scene of ‘Mackay’s Nugget,’on which
numerous yarns have been built. In the early 'forties
two drovers, O’Loughlin and Mackay, were travelling
through that part, when Mackay picked up a shining
yellow lump and handed it to his mate. O’Loughlin,
saying it was brass, threw it away. Years later Mackay
saw gold nuggets, and then he remembered his nugget.
The long search, which ended up in Mackay’s mind
becoming unbalanced, was unsuccessful. Fourteen years
ago I was leading one of the Government prospecting
parties, and well searched the Mackay country, and I am
satisfied that the yellow metal he found was native
copper : I found some of it near the New Country River,
in a dolomite formation.
“ The Deddick group was found by the Smith
family, though the James brothers claim the predit—and
that isn’t much. On the office maps the lode looked
splendid: it went through Central Mount Deddick,
United Mount Deddick, and had a look in at White Hills,
half a mile off its course. The North Mount Deddick
lode, on the opposite side of the river, was about 9in.
wide by 30ft. in length in the syenitic and porphyritic
granite, the country rock through which it intruded.
On the southern side of the river was the Black Hill,
supposed to be a continuation. Shafts, costeens and
tunnels failed to discover the lode’s whereabouts. The
Central Mount Deddick had four imaginary lodes, and
tunnels were driven to strike them at 400 ft. below the
surface ; but unfortunately the lode materialised-only
on the office plans. The United had the only genuine
formation on the whole group. The lode was from 2ft. to
4ft. wide, of solid galena, about 100 ft. in length, before
the syenitic country rock cut off its glorious career. It
was alleged that it assayed 90oz. of silver to the ton ; but
I could not find one-fourth of that. This was about the
only genuine silver-lead lode on the field. The Mount
Deddick had a patch 30ft. long and 2ft. wide, proved down
about 30ft. by a shaft from which a level was driven 20ft.
north and south ; but, as usual, the syenitic rock chopped
it off short.”

Wallaroo and Moonta Cos. has 40,000 £2 shares
in reserve, and 160,000 issued ; and for some reason not
very clear the reserved shares are to be let out in a month
or two. The announcement isn’t much use to the
market, which hasn’t been told any of the conditions,
except that the shares are to be offered pro rata. This
would mean one new share for every four old ones. The
co. can’t want to get in money (it had a liquid surplus of
£201,396 at the end of December), so presumably there
will be no call on the shareholders. A 10s. dividend (25
per cent.) would pay for the new issue. Last year the co.
made £160,122 before allowing for depreciation, with
standard copper averaging £B7 Bs. sd. per ton ; and as it
has stood at £IOO all this year, aiid as Wallaroo and
Moonta’s output is about 3850 tons of copper per half-
year, a 25 per cent, dividend, to' be paid in the shape of
a share bonus, isn’t at all impossible. But the official
announcement says that “legal steps will be taken to
carry out the directors’ decision, after the declaration of
the dividend in June.” That doesn’t necessarily conflict
with the idea of a 25 per cent. (10s.) dividend, to be
absorbed by a 10s. call to pay for the new shares ; but it
suggests that the two things are to be distinct. Any way,
at is.a windfall. Shares (£2 paid) are now worth £5 15s.
At the end of 1905—seventeen months ago—they were
worth £ll2s.
Craig and Cos., Paisley, Scotland, report on
Taranaki crude petroleum :—“ Oil of exceptional
quality... most superior we have examined... products
all of excellent quality, color and smell... high propor-
tion of wax of high-melting point... very small propor-
tions of unsaturated hydrocarbons... resembles in
character and purity Pennsylvanian petroleum." The
results of first distillation were Crude spirit, 21'53
p.c. ; crude petroleum, 25'74 p.c. ; heavy oil, 47T5 p.c.
Refined products Spirit, 14'3l p.c. ; kerosene (Ist
quality), 32'00 p.c.,; kerosene (2nd quality), 6'25 p.c.;
gas oil, 14'81 p.c. ; spindle oil, 8'75 p.c.; refined wax,
1070 p.c. ; residue oil from wax, I'Bo p.c. The
report reads well;; . but definite opinions had better
be left over till Craig and Cos. have supplied quota-
tions for the necessary refining plant. Expert George
Adams, of Galicia (Austria), Java, Sumatra, Borneo
and the world, estimated that the cost would reach
£200,000 ; if so, it takes some of the gilt off the glowing
remarks. However, the pressure at the Birthday bore is
as high as ever, and steady progress is being made at
other bores. But the non-arrival of an expected expert
from Canada is delaying matters.

B.H. Prop, has generally been ready to adopt
progressive ideas—slime sintering, Huntingdon-Heberlein
converters, sulphuric acid manufacture, and a long tail
of smallerimprovements, including lately the steam shovel.
The latest item at the Port Pirie smelters is a gas pro-
ducer, which is to replace the fire boxes at the roasting
furnaces. Producing gas in a furnace by the suction of
air and steam through a mass of very hot fuel is a process
we aredikely to hear more about. There is a continuous
line of progression in such things when inventive talent
is fostered ; but that is only the case when we manu-
facture for ourselves.

“Hassan”: Be par (25/4/’O7) concerning
the trade in lyre-birds’ tails and portions of
other deceased Australian “protected” fauna.
Victoria is not the only State where the
furriers openly display these latter ends.
The trouble is that if any inquiries are
made by the police of Victoria the shopkeeper
suavely informs the zealous John that the tails in
question came from the wronged and bleeding
New South Wales. Conversely, the Sydney
bobby is told that the articles were separated
from the ends of Victorian dicky-birds. The
only remedy is, of course, a Federal Act, but
when I consider the possibility of such a “ wrong ”
to New South Wails —well, excuse me while I go
out and kill a Victorian.

Curious conduct on the part of a cow is
reported from Narracan Creek, Gippsland. There
had been a heavy downpour and much thunder,
and the cow did not turn up next day. After a
two days’ search Mrs. Moo was discovered stuck
fast in a large hollow log. She had evidently
worked her way into the log seeking shelter.
There was room for her only in a kneeling posi-
tion, and she had broken both horns off in her
struggles to get out. The log had to be split to
liberate the source of Victoria’s greatness—fide
Bent.
A fox was, the other day, seen as far
north as droughty Hergott (S.A.), well up
towards the heart of the continent. When this
recently-imported curse began to show his sharp
nose, S. Australians, anxious to make the best of a
bad bargain, said that, anyhow, he couldn’t
spread beyond the comparatively mild south and
south-east. And already he is stalking the lamb
in the Far North. The fact had better be remem-
bered when Dr. Danysz says that the beastie he
wants to liberate to wipe out the rabbit is too
slow to catch other animals. Under Australian
conditions there is no telling what the imported
beastie won’t do in time.
The only newspaper the boundary-rider
at Dumbletown threw into the hut-lceepeffs open
window was an occasional copy of the Sydney
Morning Herald. It thus came to pass that
Jerry, the hutkeeper, imbibed a whole cataract
of foolishness about the despotic interference of
Socialism in the affairs of life, and, visiting Syd-
ney at Show time, fell an easy victim. “Hi,”
said an austere man in blue serge, “ where are you
going?” “To look about me, after seven years
on Dumbletown,” answered Jerry. “Where’s
your permit?” asked the austere man. “Me
what?” “Your permit. Did you lodge your
money ? No ? See here, are you mad ? Do you
want to be thrown into gaol for five years? Don’t
you know you should have reported yourself at
the Central, lodged your money, and only drawn
each day what you required?” “I knew there
were some new-fangled ideas, but I didn’t think
they were so bad as that. What does the law require
me to do?” “To lodge your money and get a
permit, of course. You’re liable to five years for
going about as you’re doing. Come this way.
How much money have you on you?” “Four
poun’ ten.” “ Well, give it here. This is the
Central. I’ll get you your permit.” Half-an-hour
later a policeman asked Jerry what he was
lounging about for, and, being told, laughed so
loudly that the sergeant sent a man to move the
disturber. Jerry now says that it was all the
fault of that fool of a Herald, which had really
persuaded him that the liberty of the subject was
quite obliterated.

“ Gip ” : Read somewhere, the other day,
that very little w;is known of the habits of the
butcher-bird in captivity. For the benefit of
those who may be tempted to make a pet of a
butcher-bird, I send in this : When learning to
shoot I one day aimed at the white trunk of an
old-man gum, and shot a butcher-bird in the wing
some 25yds. the other side of said trunk. No one
was more surprised than myself. Captured the
bird in my skirt and brought him home. From
the first he ate bread and meat thrown to him.
Later, he fed from my hand, and after that I
allowed him to run about the yard. On the fourth
day of his freedom, he ruthlessly murdered four
just-hatched downy ducklings. I remonstrated
with him tearfully, but reframed from punishing
him because he dodged me round a briar, and I
couldn’t catch him. Next day I kept a watchful
eye on him, but he found an opportunity to
murder the rest of the ducklings. All went well
after that until a proud hen brought forth thirteen
prize Orpington chicks. The butcher-bird had
shown himself very friendly and affectionate
since his last big offence, climbing to my
shoulder and burying his beak in my hair,
and I had grown to trust him. A disturb-
ance in the poultry yard caused me to run
out in haste. The hen was flying wildly at the
butcher bird, and nine prize chicks lay dead on
the ground. When I had spent my anger on the
cat, his nibs peeped cautiously round the door
making soft overtures, but refused to let me catch
him. That night an angry man put an end to
him, but not before he had killed a much-loved
canary, let out for his daily fly round the dining
room. Apart from this, I don’t know anything
about the habits of the butcher bird in captivity,
or New South Wales, or anywhere else.

“Fortunately,” said the S.M. Herald
the other day, talking of the threatened shortage
of water at Broken Hill and the possibility of
mines having to close down—“fortunately, we
are just now on the verge of the winter season,
and this particular difficulty will probably be
removed when the usual rains come.” There are
a lot of things the coastal man still doesn’t know
about the innards of his country. Back in the
mulga, where Broken Hill is, there are no “ usual
rains.” Purnamoota, just outside Broken Hill —
a settlement older than Broken Hill—has the
hoariest rain records for that district. In 1894
December was the wettest month ; in 1895, Janu-
ary ; 1896, November; 1897, January; 1898,
June; 1899, June ; 1909, January ; 1901, August;
1902, December; 1903, December; 1904, July;
1905, June;_ 1906, June. Over a long stretch of
years November, March, May and July have
given the least moisture, if all the moisture was
stood on end; and if even that happened, and G.
Reid’s dog were thrown into it, it wouldn’t be
enough to wet him all over. Most of the rains
seem to be purely accidental, and the accident is
liable to happen at any time, though, as the
average for 13 years past is only 7.7 in., the acci-
dent is far more liable not to happen at all.

“ Paul Cupid ” : I notice that many parts
of N.S.W., are complaining of a grasshopper
plague. There appear to be (as is the case with
humans) two classes of ’hopper—a local, stationary
class, and a floating or rather flying population.
These latter always fly towards one particular
point of the compass, not delaying long en route
even for the most tempting vegetation, though
they sample it all as they proceed. Their flights
seem to take place at one particular time of the
year, generally in March. These two conclusions
point to the supposition that the travellers are
troubled with a cargo of eggs, and are hurrying to
a suitable breeding ground. For the benefit of
embryo naturalists, it may be explained that the
male grasshopper doesn’t lay eggs, but he hurries
along with his female relatives just the same, to be
present when the business is done. The stationary
’hoppers lay their eggs in March or April in their
own locality. They select a bare patch of ground,
which, when they have finished laying, looks as
if it had been broken up with some pronged
instrument. The process of depositing the eggs is
interesting to observe. The female who is doing
all the work is in the centre of a ring of males,
who look just as ecstatic and silly as a man talking
to his first squalling infant. Each female lays
about half a hundred eggs. When ’hopper junior
comes out of the egg, he has to shed his skin, which
he does with the ease of a hardened politician,
before he can fly.
Did you ever see a petroleum well ? The
workers at these wells never have consumption or
any form of lung trouble. If they catch cold they
take liberal doses of crude petroleum, and it never
fails to cure them. Angier’s Emulsion is specially
purified crude petroleum combined with the
hypophosphites. There is nothing equal to it for
coughs and lung affections, and it is pleasant to
take. In three sizes, of all chemists.*
“ Jostler”: I hereby enter the Central
Queensland frog for the prize for voracity. Frogs
and grasshoppers are as plentiful up here as the
want of money in Philp’s deficit. I tried an ex-
periment at feeding a frog last night. Froggy
ate no less than 11 grasshoppers within an hour
and a half, the smallest hopper being an inch and
a quarter long, and the largest two inches.
Froggy thus ate over 16 inches of grasshopper,
and as I see him looking well at his accustomed
camp near the water-bag, it is evident his snack
did him no harm. He is just now consuming a
large cockroach by way of counter-lunch.

“Catbird”: Be “F.B.’s” inquiry (B.
14/3/’O7) as to how a person dies from snake-bite.
My experience is that different snake-bites pro-
duce different effects on different animals. . A
nigger I knew in Queensland was bitten by a
death-adder. He caught the adder and tore it in
two pieces, and then gave up the ghost forthwith
without making a remark. A woman, bitten by
a black snake, took 24 hours to die. Then I once
saw a horse bitten by a tiger-snake about six in
the evening. At 12 o’clock next day the victim
started to bleed from ears, eyes, nose, and mouth.
This lasted till evening, when it gave three leaps
in the air and perished. A calf, bitten by a
tiger-snake, bellowed, and ran amok wildly for
two hours, and then dropped, never to run again,
amok or otherwise. Have seen a dog bitten
inside a minute by a brown snake and a black
snake, and it died in about 15 minutes.
# ► * *
“Broncho George”: Of the vitality of
the bovine many instances have been recorded.
Here is one more. Some six years ago a mob of
cattle were purchased at Newcastle Waters,
Northern Territory, and driven to Annandale
station, Queensland, a distance of over 900 miles.
Amongst the animals was a lively young steer
that attracted much attention by its great
objection to associate with the other cattle.
It was frivolous, but an instinctive her-
mit. After some four years, during which
time the beast successfully eluded all efforts to
make him communicate with man, he. was shot for
beef purposes. On dressing the carcase Mr.
Gaffney, the manager of Annandale,' was surprised
to discover in the paunch the head of a native
spear, made of metal, tempered at one end, and
about 11 Jin. long by 2£in. wide. No such weapon
is used in Annandale district, but it is well known
among the Northern Territory natives, so the
animal must have carried it from Newcastle
Waters, and lived with the implement in its
paunch for over six years. Examination of the
hide revealed what appeared to be an old cut, so
presumably the frisky animal hadn’t swallowed
the spear-head, but had been attacked from
outside.
S. Australia, cursed with imported spar-
rows, and starlings, and foxes, not to mention
numerous things with roots, has learned its
lesson. Following on a wave of paragraphs that
swept lately through Australian newspaper
offices concerning “ African rabbit-eating ants,”
a consignment of ants’ eggs from Russia arrived at
Pt. Adelaide; but before they could be landed
the practical Premier Price had rushed through a
Gazette notice declaring ants, in or out of the
shell, prohibited immigrants. He didn’t have
any faith in the capacity of any ant —African or
Russian—to distinguish lamb from rabbit.
“ Draillibor ” : I was once war correspon-
dent to an uncommon campaign. It was on
Rawdon Vale station (N.S.W.), and the row was
between sugar ants and green-head ants. The
fight lasted three days. Just before sundown each
day active operations would cease, and each side
would spend some considerable time in carrying
its dead about four yards back from the fighting-
line. The third day ended the war. At about
noon delegates from the two armies met in con-
sultation, and, after much debate, peace was
signed. I think the sugar ants paid an indemnity
to the green-heads. Certainly, che sugar ants
were the weaker force, and had the heavier losses,
and it was they who first held up the leg of amity,
and suggested peace.
* * * *
“ Agricola ” wants to know :
The eaglehawk does not display any great amount of
brain power in his chase after rabbits. The other day I
saw one skimming along after a rabbit, which was shift-
ing grass in its hurry to reach a hollow log about 75yds.
away. Just as things began to get interesting, bunny
side-stepped to the extent of about 6ft., and the hawk,
unable to turn so close to the ground, whizzed by. Before
it could get under way again it had to rise 20ft. in the
air, and all the while bunny was putting in big licks for
the coveted log. The bird came swooping down again,
and, as before, bunny side-stepped just as its claws were
almost parting his fur. Three times this happened before
bunny got within dashing distance, and then he dived
into the hollow with neatness and despatch. The eagle
whirled round a bit, and then roosted on the log
and invited bunny to come out walking again ; but that
cautious animal, with the tip of his nose just showing
at the entrance, remarked that the weather was too
warm for such violent exercise. I have seen eagles
several times putting rabbits through their sprints in
like manner, but never with any more satisfactory re-
sult. Now, I want to know how on earth do they catch
them, for they may often be seen flying away with hapless
rabbits in their talons.
A common explanation is that on sunny days the
fleeing rabbit knows when to dodge by watching
the bird’s shadow, and that on dull days, when
the bird casts no perceptible shadow, the rabbit
is a lost bunny. At least a bushman of long
standing once propounded that view of the case
in The Bulletin.

THE BLAOKBUTT.
[For Thu Bulletin.]
I know a tree that grows within a valley,
Yet rears his lordly height
Above the hills, and soars majestically
Into the starry night.
He sways with grand complaisance to the breezes,
Acknowledging their presence, an he pleases.
His huge dark butt is gnarled and buttressed greatly;
His bole is round and white ;
A hundred feet of bulk it tapers straightly
Into the upper light,
Before a massive limb goes upward, sharing
The lofty crown of foliage he is bearing.
Great claws of roots, with twists and turns and bosses,
Clutch firm the nether mould,
With sheltered coigns where clinging ferns and mosses
Of ancient right take hold ;
And deeper down he binds the rocks together
To serve as ballast in the windy weather.
Up there, high overhead, he holds communion,
In rustling, dizzy heights,
With wing’d wayfarers, making gay reunion
After prodigious flights.
He gives them hostel—aye, and right good housing;
And glories in their garrulous carousing.
So grand a tree, whose roots are in a valley,
Yet in the clouds his head ;
Who bears his greatness most majestically,
With huge high branches spread,
I know—and stand in awe before the giant,
So high and strong, so mighty, yet so pliant.
VI0 , C. G. A. Oolles.

“ Atlang’s ” narrative : —
Yes, I was a young fellow of 25 then. It was down a
Tilpaninnie, in S. Australia. I had a billet in the flour
mill, and I was badly mashed on Brown’s daughter.
Brown had a farm, and was well off. The girl seemed
willing, too, but I hadn’t been able to collect pluck
enough to pop the question. I was shy with women.
Well, one evening I was sitting with her when I noticed
two blaekfellows’ waddies which ornamented the mantel-
piece, and they set me talking. “ I was reading, the
other day,” I said, “ of a marriage custom among the
Australian niggers. It seems that when a young man
decides to marry he gives chase to the girl, and, when he
catches her, he knocks her down with his waddy. Thence-
forward she is his woman.”
Say, do you believe that a person’s thoughts can be so
clearly shown by the eyes as to be easily read by another?
I did—then. I’m not so sure now ! The queer laugh
that greeted my account of native marriages caused me
to look in her face, and in her eyes, as they glanced first
at me and then at the waddies, I read a challenge: She
gave me no chance to doubt, for, repeating her queer
little laugh, she darted through the open doorway and
across the verandah into the garden. It was full moon,
and it needed not her backward glance to make me enter
into the spirit of her mood. Collaring one of the
waddies, I sprang after her just as she reached the gate
leading into the orchard. The chase didn’t last long ;
perhaps she didn’t want it to. When I was close behind
her, she suddenly crouched, in pretended fear of the
blow. Now, I never could hit a woman hard enough to
hurt her —even in fun—so I completed my proposal of
marriage by giving her a slight tap with the waddy on
the side of the head. Then I laughed, and stooped to
lift her up, but she was too quick for me. She sprang to
her feet, and faced round, and waved me off. With eyes
that flashed scornfully she passed by, and I was too
astonished to move. Then, when she had put a short
distance between us, she turned and said: “Oh, you
coward 1 you ninny l I knew you would be afraid to hit
me hard.”
And then she went in and banged the door—hard.

“ Janor ” : What about the road-side
shanties as the “ biggest curse out back” ? Many
a man has been outed by one drink at these dens,
and he who is thus doped or hocussed (“locust ”
the busbies mostly call it) is never again as good
a man, physically or mentally, as he was before
the dose. ’Tis to be regretted that so few workers
realise the true inwardness of this drink biz. The
other fellow—the employer—does all the time. A
few years ago, Woolenough, hastily called to Fog-
land, left the management of the station to his
son. One of the first matters he had to deal with
was the leasing of a piece of land near the home-
stead as a site for a projected hotel. He was a
decent chap in spite of his Tory upbringing, and
he turned the proposal down, much to the disgust
of the would-be Bung, who had almost completed
the business with Woolenough. In due course
the squatter returned, and the first thing he
wanted to know was why the devil the hotel was
not up. Son explained. Father blew up. “Why,
you dashed fool,” said he, “don’t you see that
when we have this pub within a mile of the station
we’ll always have plenty of men about, and we’ll
get ’em at our own price ?” The pub went up.

AGIN THE LAW.
ITINERANT HERBALIST: “I kin read the cause of all yer troubles in yer face —an a shillin'
box of my magic pills will banish it for ever.”
TENANT COCKY: “ Hivins, man, d'yez want me ter pison me landlord?”
The man who alleges this encloses his own,
or someone else’s, card as evidence of good faith: —
Some 16 years ago, on the Tasmanian east coast, the
Seamander bridge was washed away in a big flood, and
the Government fixed up a temporary punt. The custom
was to drive the four horses and the coach on to the punt
and pull at a wire rope. A good fresh was running when
I was there, and the rope parted and left us drifting
towards the ocean some hundred yards away. For-
tunately the punt grounded on a sand spit. The driver,
one Avery, told of a previous experience of the kind, but
on this occasion he went out some miles to sea, and was
there picked up by a coastal steamer without losing a
horse. The mate was on the bridge at the time, and
when he sighted the punt he sent a sailor down to the
captain to report a coach and four horses on the port
bow. Said sailor was immediately ordered into irons for
being drunk and disrespectful and a dangerous lunatic.
It being a bright moonlight night the captain presently
saw how things were, and took everything on board in-
eluding the driver, who had stuck to the reins right
through.

for so many years spoken, written, and lied

bidgee whaler is practically extinct, and only a
very rare speci-
men whales for-
lornly on the
ground where
the abundant

be. This unfor-
tunate state of
affairs is due, the
writer is dis-
creditably in-

bits. In olden
days the whaler
presented his
empty wallet
with the assur-

would be filled,
but in these
times the old
chap is dead
certain to be in-
vited “to do a
bit of trappin’
for tucker and
what you can
make, traps
thrown in.” Now
the swaggie who
is physically in-
capable of trapping the rabbit is also beyond
bluey-humping, so he has no middle course,
and must either starve or trap. The thorn in
the flesh with our modern whaler is that there
were no bunnies to be trapped in the palmy
days of his ancestors. Unless the pestilent
animal is obliterated, whalin’ will be reckoned
with the industries of the past.

“ Backblox ” : lie “Jimmie Pannikin”
(4/4/’O7), on Outback coach-travelling. Have
often travelled on Tibooburra read, from dusty
Broken Hill. Had an extra lively team in once,
and the leaders thought it a great joke to kick
over the traces. Tilpa Bill (the driver) calmly
requested me to “ j Ist git down an’ see if y’ can
fix ’er, will y’ ? ” Often I had j ust settled down in
the boot for a doze when, “ Gate ! Are y’ ’wake,
mate?” I opined that I was there to work my
passage, so made no objection. Another time,
met a team of camels on the road. They kept in
the middle of the track, and Bill’s talk seemed to
make the atmosphere quite hazy and sulphurous
for a while. “I dunno w’y they ever let them
flamin’ cows come inter Orstralia fer,” observed
he. “If I’d’a’ ’ad my way, I’d ’a’ blanky well
settled the blanky cusses.” Pulled up at a shanty,
and inquired for a meal. Two lumps of goat-
steak, a piece of stale bread and a mug of tea
were bumped down in front of us in response, for
which we paid the modest sum of half-a-crown.
Was sorry to part with Bill and his conversational
gifts at sleepy Euriowie.

Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy has been
declared free from injurious substances by
Government chemists. For sale everywhere,
Is. 6d.*
One Hair, a selector from Wagga (N.S. W.),
has just finished wrestling with a problem that
started with 3d. and ended with disaster. A crowd
of applicants applied for a block of land near
Wagga. Hair’s application was sifted out as the
most deserving, and he got the land. Whereupon
a dissatisfied rival moved the law to eject Hair,
on the ground that Hair, when posting his appli-
cation to the State lands agent, did not register
his letter, but sent it by ordinary post. The
objector" quoted regulation 356 to the Law,
and waited results. Regulation 356 sets
out that all applications for land must be
lodged in person or by an accredited agent, or
they “ may be sent by registered post.” Hair took it
that “ may ” meant that the blessed thing was
optional, and that he might send by any old post,
and save the price of a long beer. The learned
judges of the Supreme Court, who finally heard
the appeal, were unanimous in saying, with costs,
that “may” meant “shall,” and added that the
fool individual who drew up 356 would have saved
a mint of trouble by writing “shall” and not
“ may.” So Hair has lost his land. Many are
the pitfalls awaiting the man who tries to go on
the dust in N.S.W.

“ Edyson ” : David Griddle had an excel-
lent 300 acres at Hill End, a fine paddock under
crop, a promising orchard, seven good cows, and
the prospect of doing excellently with pigs. He
resolved to marry, as a cheap means of getting
assistance, and that being settled in his mind he
looked about for a suitable girl to fall in love
with, found her, and fell in love with business-
like promptness. Then he went to speak to old
“Cockatoo” Carter about it. He said: “I’m
pretty well fixed, I’m in love with Hettie, and
Hettie’s in love with me. I want to marry her.”
“ Yeh can’t,” answered Carter emphatically.
“ Can’t!” cried Davy, “and why the devil can’t
I?” “Because it ain’t Hettie’s turn,” said Carter;
“it’s Martha’s turn.” “But I don’t want Martha,”
said Griddle. “ It’s Hettie I’ve been courting.”
“That don’t matter,” answered Cockatoo
Carter stubbornly. “ Martha will overlook that.”
“But I don’t want Martha, I want Hettie,”
cried the young man. “Then you won’t get
Hettie. Hettie ain’t goin’ t’ marry out of her
turn. I’m a just man, I am,” responded the stern
parent, “and I believe in fair dealin’. ’Tain’t
fair dealin’ for Hettie t’ marry afore Martha.
Martha comes first. If you come to me askin’
for one o’ my daughters, you’ve got to take ’em in
order.” “I’ll see you hanged before I’ll marry
Martha,” snorted Griddle. Cockatoo Carter
arose and shook his forefinger impressively. “I
warn you, young man,” he said, “I warn you.
You come here askin’ fer the ’and 6’ my daughter,
’n’ you ain’t goin’t’ get out of it so easy just cause
you can’t pick’ll’ choose t’please yourself.” “Go to
the devil,” thundered Dave, and stumbled out. A
week later Griddle was up at the township, and
there learned the latest news. Instigated by her
Draconian parent, Martha Carter had commenced
proceedings against him for breach of promise.
She lost the case, and Cockatoo Carter quotes it
to this day as a shocking miscarriage of justice.
