No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
On June 3rd, Melbourne’s Police Magis-
trate Joseph Anderson Panton will enter his 77th
year. Then he is to retire. He came to Aus-
tralia in 1850, and in 1852 was appointed
Commissioner of Crown Lands and Gold Fields
Commissioner at Bendigo. His first police
magistracy was at Heidelberg; then he was at
Geelong, and finally at Melbourne.
Bishop Langley, of Bendigo, well-known
in Sydney as Archdeacon Langley, once
MR. C. H. HANNELL,
the father of the New-
cattle Jockey Club.
Mr. O. H. Hannell, who has
held the position of judge to
the Newcastle Jockey Club
since 1877, has tendered his
resignation on the ground of
Jailing eyesight.
negotiated an emin-
ently successful
financial transac-
tion. Probably
his early train-
ing among the
fleshpots of the
Bank of Aus-
tralasia was
partly re-
sponsible.
Bishop Bar-
ker, of Syd-
ney, was in
pecuniary
doldrums
about thirty
years ago,
and consulted
his secretary,
Mr. Langley,
on the matter.
And Langley
went forth
like a ravening
lion, and raised
a church build-
ings fund of
£10,000. This
was lent out in
large or small
sums to needy
churches, on strict
business principles
and with a due re-
gard to the security.
The fund is still in
existence, but it has
been lent and re-lent
and recovered until
the total issued
amounts now to over
£60,000.
The Melbourne
Law Students’ So-
ciety has elected Ah
Xett as its president for the ensuing year.
Ah is the youthful Mongolian lawyer or China-
man One who smokes big cigars in Collins-
street after lunch, and hangs out in Selborne
Chambers, where are also Woolf and Jacobs and
Agg and Box, and scores of other limbs of the law.
That rather stormy Presbyterian parson,
the Rev. W. Frackelton, of Queensland, has had
his certificate of sky-pilotage suspended by the
Marine Board of the Church —that is, the General
Assembly—for six months. What worries The
Bulletin is that it can’t find out whether the
gentleman had steered badly and run his church
aground, or whether he had overloaded his
tabernacle so that the Plimsoll mark was
submerged.
Heart disease removed W. M. Bolt, a
Maoriland M.L.C., the other day. A curious
thing about this particular democrat was that,
although he was ear-deep in learning, he was con-
tent to be storeman for a Christchurch firm for 30
years. His useful fad was co-operative settle-
ment and co-operation in labor affairs generally.
He wrote extensively on labor matters, and his
speeches in the Uppah Chamber showed that he
had been wrapt in thought. His real usefulness
as an apostle of co-operation came to an end in
1891, the year Seddon spiked his gun with the
Council bauble, and put him where the old men
pay no heed to the voice of remonstrance.
ThelateR. W. Holmes,
a Gent One, who left £26,500
to Ballarat charities, worked
his way out to Australia on
the ship Bella in 1855.
In 1857 he was stone-
breaking in Ballarat,
and, later on, became a
road-contractor. After
he commenced practice
as a lawyer, he married
a barmaid ; and, as
the Gentleman One
industry wasn’t re-
munerative just then,
the little woman
waded in bravely in
the cow and chookey
industry, and help-
ed to keep the pot
at a temperature
of 212 degrees.
Years ago, Mr.
Holmes founded
the legal firm of
Holmes and Salter,
and, when he died, he
was the oldest practising
solicitor in Ballarat.
When Harry Levien first
entered N.S.W. Parliament the
Legislative Assembly consisted of
103 members. That was in December, 1880. Of
that 103 only two members hold seats in the
Assembly to-day, Levien and J. H. Young, 101
having gone or been sent or taken elsewhere.
Levien, though Father of the House, is still com-
paratively young—not yet 56.
Premier Price is working and worrying
too much in his fight against the S.A. Council
majority (thirteen of them rule the country—the
unlucky number again !), and he is looking fagged
and ill. Yet he still throws himself into the tight
with all the enthusiasm of a zealot, and there is
the Brisbane Premiers’ Conference and a heavy
Parliamentary session just in front of him.
Alfred Deakin, as was expected, refused
to take even the title of Privy Councillor. A
Privy Councillor nowadays doesn’t give coun-
sel privily or otherwise; the two letters are
mere frill, devised to enable the wearer to
secure precedence and be called “ flight Hon,”
and Deakin despises such tricks. For such an
affable man, Deakin is quite firm to the titles
hawker who comes round with a barrow of
“ distinctions.”
Justice Simpson (N.S.W.) complains of the
limited number of J udges and their overwork. The
remedy does not consist in appointing additional
J udges, but in retiring aged ones who are past
active service, and appointing younger men. Of
the seven N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges, three
are over 70. One is 77, another 73, and a third,
whose age is not ascertainable, was admitted to
the Bar in 1858, which should bring him over the
allotted span.
Audit Commissioner Cooke, who has just
died in Adelaide after 25 years’ service, was the
most ponderous official person in the State. He
was an able, painstaking and conscientious man,
but few ever understood either what he said or
what he wrote. He was diffuse and involved
always, and had a perfect genius for complicating
an explanation. He was a most valuable financial
watchdog, but ordinary people had to look up his
bark in a dictionary before they could get the
hang of it.
Nugent Robertson, the “ Prudent Federa-
tionist,” who was about the bottom of the N.S.W.
Federal Convention poll in the long ago, still
hurries after his grievance against the Balmain
New Ferry Co. It was something about a penny,
and a turnstile that didn’t revolve with sufficient
suddenness, and an angry and detained barrister.
The enraged legal gentleman, and the penny, and
the grievance have already been ail through the
N.S.W. Courts, and are now going to the Privy
Council in London. A wiser man would have
broken the company’s window or blackened the
eye of a director, and let it pass at that.
Alan Gr. Pendleton, S. Australia’s sole
Railway Commissioner, has his 70th birthday this
month; and the S. Australian Law Department
is turning up its books to see whether the
Septuagenarian Act applies to him. If it does,
he can only remain where he is from year to year
by special resolution of the Government. Just at
present the Government would pass that resolu-
tion promptly ; for it isn’t often that a S.A.
Railway Commissioner has helped a S.A. Govern-
ment to make a surplus of a quarter of a million,
as this year’s will be.
“F.C.”: Melbourne Age is responsible
for the statement that the Peerewur Estate,
which once ran from Bacchus Marsh to Bungaree,
and the last portion of which was lately cut up,
was “owned by the late Mr. Bacchus, who gave
his name to Bacchus Marsh.” I always thought
that explorer Sturt named the physical features
of that locality after the tamer of Bucephalus and
his belongings—Mount Alexander ; Macedo’n, his
kingdom; Campaspe, his mistress; Bacchus, his
god; etc. Was there really a Mister Bacchus?
if so, who was he ?
A most respectable Victorian lawyer who
died recently left among his effects quite a multi-
tude of photos, of nude girls—all stout girls. He
never showed them to anyone. He never told
nude stories, and he never rioted with the boys,
and he was never known to pursue the barmaid or
Tottie Fewduds of the chorus. In fact he was a
cold, silent, buttoned-up man whose apparent
morality was something terrible.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers, the other
day, cited a few of the graduates of Sydney
University:—
The first Prime Minister of the Commonwealth was a
Sydney University graduate. So were the former Pre-
mier of Queensland, and the present Chief Justice of that
State. Two of the University professors in South Aus-
tralia were from N.S.W., and in three of the States the
men who drafted the laws for the consideration of the
Parliaments were Sydney graduates. One was professor
of anatomy at Cairo, another professor of Psychics in
India, and a third lecturer in the Northampton Institute
Technical College, London. The engineer-in-charge of
the underground electric railway in London—no small
concern—was a graduate, and the son of a leading official
in this State. In Korea, a very outlandish place, the son
of his old chief at the Sydney Grammar School was in
charge of some very important mines. When the
Government of Siam chose an officer to take a leading
position in its railway service, it was a Sydney graduate
who got the job.

A man who has played an extraordinary
number of parts is having a probably final look-in
at Australia. R. A. Tarlton started life in a
London bank, and then thought he had a “ call ”
to convert the heathen of Tasmania. But Tas-
mania apparently wasn’t as heathen as he thought,
so after a few years he passed on to S. Australia
and '.started money-grubbing—got in with the
Wills firm, had a finger in promoting the Bank of
Adelaide, and dabbled in squatting. Then the
energetic little red man —more like Zeal in
manner and appearance than any other Australian
public person—plumped for the Commercial Bank
of S. Aus. That was a pious institution, and an
ex-Rev., who had become M.L.0., did well for a
chairman. Then came the smash, and R. A. T.
crawled out to S’Africa, where J ohannesburg was
just being first mentioned. He made heaps of
money again, and got into so good a position that
at the start of the Transvaal trouble he was chair-
man of the “Australian Committee.” Now, at
about 78, he looks in again at Adelaide, where
he won’t find much of the smell of the old Com-
mercial left.
Dr. J. H. Zillmann, once a man of some
tonnage in the Anglican Church, but of recent
times a “ marrying parson” in Sydney, was some
months ago dropped by the Registrar-General off
the list of parsons authorised to perform the
wedding ceremony, and the Supreme Court has
refused to order his re-instatement. The idea of
N.S.W. law is that a parson should show at least
a certain proportion of preach to so much mar-
riage. If there is more than the specified amount
of preach it is pleased, but there musn’t be less
than the minimum of preach. The Rev. Zillmann
married 283 couples during the latter half of 1906,
and the Registrar-General held that there wasn’t
sufficient expounding to cover all this. He set
down Dr. Zillmann as a plain, ordinary, “marry-
ing parson,” and knocked him out. The reverend
is said to have been in the middle of a marriage
ceremony (he promptly broke it off) on January
31, when he received the belated news that he had
been abolished in that capacity at the end of the
previous month, and if that yarn is true the
belatedness of the news is very remarkable and
somewhat embarrassing. The State would save
itself much worry if it made marriage a civil con-
tract which must, in every case, be performed be-
fore a State official, and left the parties to have
whatever religious ceremony they pleased after-
wards. Then this question about an intolerable
amount of marriage to a mere ha’porth of preach
need not come up, and the marriage shop could be
let as a green-grocery.
It is pretty hard to escape from the Aus-
tralian and Maorilander. Malcolm M’Daren, for
some years mining geologist to the Government
of India, and himself a Maorilander, exploring
on the Irrawaddi River, found a party from
Otago running a dredge right upon the Burmese
frontier, within 20 miles of the hostile Kackin
country; and he heard of another lot of Maori-
landers who had been working in the northern
Shan States, in a place so remote that to get
their machinery there they had
to cut and make a 40-
mile road.

Five mendicant missionaries, whose Asiatic
tongues can’t get round the education test, were
held up in Fremantle last week. Here is the list
of them : Our old friend Goliath Joseph, G. EliaS,
G. Nicholas, Deacon Jacob, Deacon Simon. They
have knocked at the door and it has not been
opened, so they are sailing again. One paper
describes Goliath, whom the Foreign Trade
press tried to elevate into a Six Hatters or Petriana
myth:—
Joseph Goliath is a very different man from the neat
and cleanly-garbed foreigner who passed through the
port some four weeks ago. Unshaven, shivering, and
with a thick coat pressed up against his ears, and an un-
clerical looking cap on his head, he looks misery personi-
fied.
Apparently, Goliath left off washing when he was
refused admission.

The fact that he couldn’t be in two places
at once did W. P. Crick a good turn last week,
when he was haled before a magistrate on a charge
of discharging firearms in a public street. The
late charge of conspiracy took over a month to
decide, and Crick naturally couldn’t get away to
be harassed at the Police Court. When he did
appear, over a month had elapsed since the date
of the offence, and the magistrate dismissed the
information as being out of date.
About a deceased veteran :
Captain William Jackson Barry was the most picturesque
liar in Australasia, but he saw stirring times, for all that.
On goldfields in Australia, California, and M.L., running
horses to India, whaling, in shipwrecks, amongst bush-
rangers, in the navy (first Chow-British war), sheep-
farming, selling saveloys in the streets, and in other
occupations he lived up to the theory of the strenuous
life. Barry was once Mayor of Cromwell (M.L.), and
during his term he paid a visit to Dunedin. His visit was
prolonged, to the detriment of his municipal duties, and
also to the great red-painting of Dunedin. Cromwell
Council met and passed a vote of censure. The outrage
brought Barry back in haste, and next meeting of the
local body was sudden. The Captain arrived late and
placed his back to the closed door. “Now !” he roared.
“ Who was the blankety blank who proposed that resolu-
tion ?” The motion was rescinded.
By a man who worked with him :
Henry Hudson, N.S.W. Clyde Engineering Co.’s general
manager, did a lot in the way of pushing along Australian
industry before he died at Sydney, the other day, aged
71. He was born in London, and came to Maorilandwith
his parents in 1839. A few years later the family arrived
in Sydney, and Henry became an apprentice to the
joinery trade. His first jobs were on the erec-
tion ‘of the first part of Sydney University,
and at St. Andrew’s Cathedral, where his father
was in charge under the late Colonial Archi-
tect Blackett. The foundation of the Clyde Co.
was unobtrusively laid down in 1860, when the Hudsons
started small joinery works at Redfern. In 1879 they got
their first Government contract for railway rolling stock.
In 1881 they turned out the first “ American ” suburban
cars used on N.S.W. railways. They built Sydney
Coast Hospital, and did some other odd jobs. In
the drought of ’B4, when the shrinkage of Botany
swamps threatened a water famine, Henry was
the man who said “ lec there be wet”—and there
was wet. He connected Potts Hill reservoir
with the cement canals on the catchment area of
Cataract waterworks - then very incomplete with
an open, half-round iron channel, and thence
brought something like 4,000,000 gallons of water daily
to Botany watershed through 20in. pipes. About
that time the joinery concern was floated into a co.,
capital £300,000, with Henry as general manager. His
big ambition was to build locomotives, and he got his
wish. He saw the first of the engines made under the
Government contract of last year placed on the rails, but
he never saw it under steam, for he became ill the same
night, and died a couple of days later from heart failure.
ONE RESULT OF THE ALLIANCE.
The British Lord Chamberlain has prohibited perform-
ances of Gilbert and Sullivan’s “Mikado” for fear of
offending Japan.— News Item.
MOTHER BULL (to little Johnny): “Hey! You
mustn't annoy your stepbrother .”
Henry Hudson, contractor, who died at
Darling Point, Sydney, last week, aged 71 years,
commenced in the joinery business in a very
small way, in Botany-street, Redfern, about 40
years ago. The immense luck of the firm in
getting Government contracts raised the sus-
picion that it had more influence behind it than
most small joiners possess. The late “Anxious”
Cameron, Chairman of Committees, once told the
writer that he believed John Sutherland
“ Honest John ” —was a member of the firm, and
Honest John was for some years Minister for
Public Works. Henry Hudson offered himself
once for a seat in Parliament, but was defeated by
a two to one vote. He never tried again.
About an inventor :
Brennan, the torpedo man, is to lecture before the
London Royal Society this month (May) on his new mono-
railway. It is a crazy-looking affair to describe, but
Brennan knows things, and can’t be dismissed as a mere
crank. He was a humble Melbourne tradesman in the
employ of Mason and Firth, the printers, and such-like
firms, when he devised the torpedo which brought him
£lOO,OOO. Many will recall the laughter evoked in Mel-
bourne by his idea of driving a torpedo with wire reins.
He exhibited it in a forlorn way at Williamstown, and
people still laughed, yet it proved a success. Brennan
has been displaying to Sydenham Clarke (our quondam
gubernatorial friend “Chinny”) and other experts the
working model of his mono-railroad, and all agree that it
runs well. The idea seized him while travelling with his
wife, and she was half afraid he had become deranged
when she found him in a room with an immense number
of tops spinning, he having apparently, bought up
all the most expensive tops in the town. He
had been impressed with a German invention for
applying the gyroscope to stop the rolling of ships.
The gyroscope is that kind of top with tr.o rings.
Going round quickly at right angles to each other, they
keep the toy upright. Recent cablegrams mention bow
the German invention of a vast gyroscope on shipboard
keeps the vessel perpendicular, and it only pitches.
Brennan applies the idea to his mono-railroad. The
question of traction is not involved. Each carriage will
contain a gyroscope, of proper size, enclosed in a box,
and this whizzy thing, set revolving, will keep the car-
riage upright on the single rail. There are other mono-
railways, but these have the rail overhead. The wheels
travel on top of the rail, and the carriage is hung under-
neath. Brennan claims that with electricity his train,
balanced on one wheel, can scuttle along at 200 miles an
hour, and he reckons that with his method Australia s
two transcontinental railways could be built and worked
at less than the cost of one under the ordinary system.

An excellent machine for Blacksmiths and all repair
shops. The gearing is thrown out when doing light work,
and when running back off finished screws, making quick
return. It holds work, both straight and crooked.
Any crooked or odd-shaped pieces can be threaded
without straightening. The spindle is hollow, and bolts of
any length can be cut. For bolts fin. and under are
rapidly done with the gears out, by the handle on the
balance wheel. For larger work the gearing multiplies the
direct force of the crank at the side of the machine between
six and sevenfold, and the arrangement is such that the Die
being run sufficiently far on the work, the gears may
instantly be thrown out and the Die whirled swiftly back
off the thread by means of the balance wheel, making
quick return.
Extra steel dogs are attached to the vice to secure large
smooth pipe which might sometimes slip into the jaws.
These are both made to cut exact Whitworth threads.

EST. 1818.
To suit the children of Israel, who might
object to vote on their Sabbath, the Vic. Govern-
ment has altered the date of the Leg. Council
election from June 1 to June 4. Now, to suit
the children of Australia, the Vic. Government
might alter the qualifications for electors and
candidates. That would matter; the other
doesn’t. For all anything matters till the quali-
fications are altered, the Government might
almost as well take the election for granted, or
postpone it till June 31st.
This is Gr. H. Reid to the daily press, a
few days ago, concerning Prime Minister
Deakin’s Preferential Trade proposals at the
Imperial Conference :—
Mr. Ramsay Macdonald, the Labor member in the
House of Commons, was perfectly correct when he said
that at the last Federal elections the talk was nearly all
about increasing Protective duties, and seldom about
Preference.
And this is G. H. Reid to the House of Reps., on
the same subject, 21st February, 1907 : —
We candidly admit that there was one feature
in the Government policy which stood out unmistakably.
There is no doubt that that issue was put clearly before
the electors, and that upon it we were defeated.
So that in any adjustment of the tariff which may be a t-
tempted we labor under no sense of treachery, no feeling
that the people have not authorised such change. The situ-
ation in regard to Preferential Trade has been cleared
considerably by the decision of the people at the last
general election. Given a Protective tariff, the Free-
trade objections, wherever t hey existed, to a system of
Preferential trade, pretty well disappear. So far as we
in Australia are concerned, we can have no possible
objection to the relaxation of fiscal bonds in favor of the
Mother country.
Just the same old Yes-No.
A youngster with a wide future before
him transpired at South Melbourne the other day.
He was a lone orphan, living with his uncle, and,
though he admitted that both uncle and aunt
treated him kindly, he averred they were heathens,
and sternly discouraged his attendance at the
Sunday-school he loved ; wherefore he had left
their abode and wandered around an unclaimed,
derelict infant, until the police took an interest in
him. The quaint honorariums swallowed this tale,
and sent him to a religious orphanage, where he
will grow up to be the founder of a new religion,
or a successful seller of “spiffs,” or an importer,
or a shining light in some other calling which
needs a well-developed inventive faculty and an
abnormal capacity for misrepresenting facts.
“ Stinking Fish ” writes :—
I wish to call attention to some Federal extravagance.
At the Emmaville (N.S.W.) post-office there is a notice
calling for tenders for firewood by weight, and I see an
odd 81b. which I consider could very well he done with-
out. I hope Carruthers will notice this with his eagle
eye, and that Haynes will > immediately call ft seeesb meet-
ing. The situation is beco mug intolerable. If this 81b.
of wood is allowed to go unchallenged, we are doomed,
and deservedly so.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 14, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
A multitude of lace with the musical name of
Argentella will be loaned to the Mayoress of Syd-
ney by Miss Rose Scott for the Exhibition of
Women’s Work. Miss Scott has a lot of lace of
divers kinds that was in her family a century ago,
and which is identifiable in books of reference on
lace just as if it were Westminster Abbey. Some
of the specimens are infants’ caps—the quaintest
things, with hand-made lace of so fine a quality that
the single thread is invisible except to good sight.
Yet the spindrifty material has endured the wash
and wear of long time. She has also her mother’s
wedding dress (the silk it is made of has long
ceased publication), and her uncle’s flowered
waistcoats—brilliant things in green, scarlet and
black brocade. It would be a good idea to have
a section at the Ex. for wedding dresses and
veils of more than fifty years ago. Amongst
Miss Scott’s collection is a wedding veil that
was truly the veil through which he could
not see the bride, and the wrong girl might
have been palmed off on him. This is very dif-
ferent from the transparent films of to-day’s
brides, when there is simply a spider web in
front of a girl.
An Australienne has come in for the bulk of
the late Cecil Rhodes’ fortune. He left it to his
eldest brother (there were seven Rhodes brothers),
who died shortly after his bigger though younger
relative. Then the huge fortune went to Captain
Ernest Rhodes, who had, in 1887, married Miss
Helen Jeffray, a Melbourne girl. He died a few
weeks ago, hence the Melbournian and her four
children take up the rich person’s burden. The
lady’s father, R. J. Jeffray, was here quite re-
cently as Commissioner for British Trade.
That particular millionaire Smith, who died re-
cently in Japan during his honeymoon, had a
near Smith relative in Sydney, a few years ago,
and this near Smith relative provided considerable
amusements and sovereigns. He used to scatter
coins amongst waiters, and would insist on paying
! the horse when he took a hansom, because it was
the horse that did the work. But he didn’t pay
the horse in oats, so the animal was not really any
“ forrarder.” Also, when he went to the theatre
ihe reserved two seats—one for himself and one
for his hat. Uncle Smith, the founder of the
ifamily, was a miser who died at a London club
on 15s. a day, leaving between five and six mil-
lions to Smiths and other relatives. (One of these
days I am going to write a learned essay on the
■origin, habits and customs of Smiths, the cause
■of Smiths, the purpose which' Smiths serve in the
■scheme of things, and the decline and fall of
Smiths.)
The baronet who ran away with the naval
lieutenant’s wife, and who now lives on a yacht
swinging at anchor on the unruffled deep in one
of our sunlit coves of peace, - has, as his family
motto, “Contented with his lot.” (The trouble
is that some people require such a lot before they
are contented.) No doubt he used to read it as
“A contented mind is a continual beast.” The
lady—she gets on and off at one of the Mosman
jetties—has wonderful frocks, which, with her
burning eyes and slender figure, are of great
interest to the ferried crowd. And it’s astonishing
how soon the serried, ferried crowd drops to a
Frisker’s identity. Upon a dav they knew her
not; cometh a cable and—“ That’s she !”
Where is the dimpled elbow of which the
novelist —especially the male novelist—is so
fond ? The fashionable sleeve publishes the
elbow in daytime in the street, as well as by
electric light in ballrooms. And for one
dimpled or soft-cushioned elbow there are
five hundred bony and “corny” ones. When
the arms are bent the dry wrinkled patch, like
the knees of a goat, is not so noticeable, but when
the arm hangs by the side, the onlooker from the
back hurts his eyes. An American writer says
that “ hard, rough elbows mean that a woman is
nervous.” Almost in the same breath she asserts
that they are made “ corny ” because they are
used as props for the head. At luncheon it is the
fashion to put your elbows on the table while you
smoke your cigarette, or just to show off your
rings. The reform treatment for elbows is to
sandpaper them, rub in cold cream, and cease.,
abusing them by making props of them.
This from a friend who is in a private hospi-
tal recovering from pneumonia : “ The matron is
about forty-five, gentle, and tired-looking. I
asked her : 4 Have you seen many persons die ? ’
: She thought for a minute, and replied : 4 Between
.300 and 400—in the general hospitals, of course,
where I was employed.’ 4 How did they take
iit? I mean did they die hard, or regretfully, or
lhow? I’m interested in the question of what
.■someone calls “holy dying.’” 4 Only two per-
sons,’said the matron, 4 both men, stand out ’in
my recollection as dying hard—not wanting to go.
All the others, whether dying consciously or
unconsciously, did not show any terror. Women
especially die very coolly.’ ‘ The exceptions,’l
asked eagerly, 4 what of them ? ’ 4 One was a youth
of twenty,’ she answered. ‘When he was told
there was no chance of his recovery he gave way to
fear and horror. For three days and nights,
without intermission, he was entreating God, the
doctors and nurses, to save him. He howled and
raved his entreaties, always asserting that he only
wanted to live long enough to right some wrong
of which he had been guilty.’ 4 Did he say what
it was?’ 4 No. And as the end approached—he
was quite conscious—he became blasphemous.
He jeeringly told the Almighty that He wasn’t
able to cure him. The lad’s mother came to see
him, and he included her in the general curse.
He died mad, with the horror of having to die.
The mother looked a rigidly-respeetable, hard-
featured woman, and she showed no emotion
whatever. Most of the nurses were unnerved by
that time.’ 4 And the other one, matron ? What
of him?’ ‘I won’t tell you of him.’ ‘Well, did
these hard-diers profess any religion?’ ‘Yes;
they both belonged to the same religion, but I
won’t tell you which.’ ‘ And she left me wonder-
ing what is the faith in which people die so hard.”
Captain Max de Bathe, well-known to Sydney-
siders as one of the most amusing, and withal
rapid, young Britishers that have struck these
parts in recent years, has married Lady Miller,
who relinquished an opulent, if not happy, home
for his sake, some twelve months ago.
Young Kelso Henderson, who was with the
Repertoire Co., is about to take himself and a
very pleasant baritone to the States. He is
fortified with letters from J.C.W., and on Friday
had a successful farewell concert.
Lady Stanley, who, as Miss Dorothy Tennant,
married explorer Stanley, has just taken a
second mate a well-known surgeon named
Curtis. The lady came to Australia in her bridal
days, and those who saw her then tell of her fine
complexion—rather cabbage-rosed as to cheeks.
Miss Winifred Purnell, who flashes an artistic
pair of hands over the ivory keyboard, gave an
attractive recital last week under Mr. Arthur
Mason’s direction, previous to her flight to Ger-
many for more study.
I found this remarkable wedding notice in the
S.M. Herald of May 11:
CONGREVE-GURNETT—UPTON.— April 28,1880, at
St. David’s, Surry Hills, by the Rev. John Douse
Langley (now Bishop of Bendigo), Captain Sir
William Augustus Congreve, Baronet (known in
the colonies as Captain Gurnett), to Lucy, eldest
daughter of Thomas Upton, of Penrith, N.S. Wales.
In this connection “Debrett’s Baronetage” for
1887 has the following :
Major-General Sir William Congreve, 2nd Baronet, the
inventor of the “ Congreve Rockets,” died in 1828, leaving
two sons (1) Sir William Augustus, 3rd Baronet, born in
1826, who was last heard of in 1860, at which time he was
in Sydney (N.S.W.), and proposed going to the Fiji
Islands. (2) Frederick William, horn 1827, formerly an
officer in the R.N., who was last heard o/at Liverpool in
1862, when he proposed going to Halifax, New Brunswick.
Daughter living of the 2nd Baronet, Isabel Christine,
horn 1828, married 1863 Sir William Comer Petheram,
Chief Justice High Court Bengal.
The Congreves seem to have been a disappearing
family. At that time one brother had been mis-
laid for 27 and the other for 25 years, and when last
heard from they both proposed going somewhere.
Mrs. Carreno, the famous pianiste, figures on
the passenger list of the Oruba as Tagliapietra.
But the other name is better for week-day use.
And the lady won’t play On Sundays.
Miss Beatrice Grimshaw, who is by way of
being another Flora Shaw, plus the creative
writer, is again in Sydney. Her book, “ Fiji and
the Cannibal Islands,” is a sparkling and infor-
mative piece of writing. Information so rarely
sparkles.
The unspeakable pea rifle was nigh adding
another tragedy to its list, last week, when it ex-
ploded and wounded Miss Daisy Windeyer, who
lives Hunter River way. The charge missed the
heart by a line or so, and for a few days the
patient’s recovery was doubtful. Mrs. Ralston,
an aunt of Miss Windeyer’s, lost a sou, some two
years ago, by the untimely discharge of a rifle.
When Miss Eva Champion (the Maoriland con-
tralto) gave a concert in Adelaide last w'eek she
had the same manager as had Ada Crossley when
en route to England for the first time. That con-
cert realised £5O. Little Eva’s brought £94 clear.
But Adelaide, and for that matter the whole
Commonwealth, has gone far in things musical
since the fair Ada left Australia in the early
’nineties. Besides, Adelaide, according to
“ Rosna,” has got a Soul, and she adds that "you
don’t know how pleasant it is to have a Soul till
you try.” How did Adelaide get its Soul? By
inheritance, in a Tatfcersall’s Sweep, or by
“trying”? Also, are Souls smuggled through
Adelaide’s Custom house along with Spirits ?
But to return to that concert. Mrs. Quesnel
went over specially to play little Eva’s accom-
paniments, and most of the women whose breath
makes the society wheel revolve blew hot upon
the enterprise. Therefore was it a success. Miss
Champion, who is getting her musical education
from the Girls’ Realm Guild, will give a sample of
her bird-like melody in Sydney before leaving for
the foreign aviary.
“ The Red Cravat,” a book, has come to keep
all other Red-named books company—from
“Under the Red Robe” to the “Red Rat’s
Daughter.” Talking of books. Mrs. Gertrude
Atherton never writes “d—n” in her novels.
She calls that form of compromise cowardice, so
she writes the whole bad bit of language. “ She
grew up in the rich-blue atmosphere of California,
and might not be so easily shocked as many.”
Australia’s atmosphere is as “rich” and “blue”
as that of California. Some of thp Australian
children whose pinnies are trimmed with real lace
swear like teamsters. One Silken Hair—a he—of
five declined to mend his language, so his adoring
mother told him he would have to leave home.
He packed a miniature swag, and went off through
the bush for a mile or so. (Pa is a country doctor.)
Mother followed, unseen. Presently a horseman,
pricking across the plain, called out: “ Little bo.y,
is this the road to Bugleywallah ? ” “ Ah,”
murmured the mother, “now I shall see my
little Reggie at his best; he is so courteous
to strangers.” She didn’t, for Silken Hair said
nothing. The horseman repeated the query, but
the small linguist strode moodily silent. At the
third time of asking the child turned on the
questioner with : “ You go to blazes. I’ve
troubles enough of my own.” And the poor
mother sat down in the “rich blue” atmosphere
to weep. Then she took Silken Hair home, and
tried to arrange that he should at least only swear
out of doors.
Hon. Gerard Collier, second son of Baron
Monkswell, is still staying in Sydney, golfing
and taking large bites of this quite perfect
blue-and-gold winter weather, and lecturing on
History at the Uni. _ Famous artist John Collier
is an uncle of our visitor. He—the artist—had the
courage to marry, as his second wife, the sister of
his first (deceased) wife, who was a daughter of the
also famous Prof. Huxley. Persons with remnant-
counter departments in their intellects like this
sort of guffermation. It does not rise to the
height of information.
An Australienne sends some theatrical notes
from London :
The death of Henry Irving left Londoners wondering
which of the leading actors might be looked upon as his
successor. It is now pretty generally accepted that the
dead actor’s mantle has descended to his son, H. 8., who
is capable of fine things. One to whom it undoubtedly
did not descend (though I expect he thinks it did) is
Beerbohm Tree—a master of make-up beyond question,
but as an actor great in.spots and commonplace in large
patches. Moreover, nowadays he lets his daughter, a
young lady of very ordinary qualifications, playiead in
parts she is quite incapable of grasping. There are points
in being the manager’s daughter !
George Alexander is a disappointment to an Australian.
Here he is the man in the sort of parts we know through
Titheradge and the late Robert Brough, though of course
they, ana Brough particularly, covered a field in versa-
tility undreamed of by Alexander. Even in his own
single line, he is not in it with either of them. To com-
pare him with them is to place a fair actor {fair, with a
patronising accent, please) alongside two artists. His
position is not unassailed, even here. Heard someone re-
mark : “ Don’t miss ‘ His House in Order ’—Pinero at his
best, and well-played. Even Alexander is good.”
Australian Oscar Asche is way up at the top of things
as an actor-manager.
There used to be a long-legged youth, named Cyril
Keightlev, who played background parts with William-
son’s dramatic cos. I fancy he figured in “ Prisoner of
Zenda ” —lengthily as to figure, but skimpily as to part.
He is coming on well here, where it is pretty difficult for
a new man to get his nose in.
People here picture us Australians as sighing in the
wilderness for a sight of their idols ; but sing “ Pooh ” to
them. They haven’t a company here to compare with
the good old Brough cos., nor the Royal Comic Opera in
the days when it numbered in its ranks Nellie Stewart,
Vi Varley, Flora Graupner, Florence Young, Elsie
Cameron, Bracy, Syd Deane, Lauri, Vernon, &c„, all at
once. There is not a musical show in London with such
a cast. As to choruses, Australians are leagues ahead.
The J. 0. Williamsons and their children
depart by the Mongolia on a visit to England,
France and America. They will be absent some
six or eight months. On Sunday night, Mr.
Williamson’s Sydney managers Goodman, Co-
ventry and Knight, Minister for Education and
Mrs. Hogue, and a few of the departing J.C.W.’s
stars and others, attended a till-we-meet-again
dinner at “Tudor.” An ex-Beaucaire and an
ex-Lady Mary Carlyle were amongst the “others ”
—Mr. Osborne and his wife. The latter is that
one-time idol, Miss Maud Jeffries. The Beaucaire
of the present, Mr. Julius Knight, and the Lady
Mary of the hour. Miss Elbert-Orton, were also
there.
Talking of Miss Maud Jeffries Osborne: She
left by the Aorangi yesterday, amidst a floral rain
from Sydney society. Her mother is in bad health,
and the visit has been a long-promised one. She
will return as soon as she may to her home on the
sheep station, and resume the hoeing of the young
pea and the boiling of hubby’s breakfast egg in
her halo.
Mrs. Arthur MacArthur, the handsome Maori-
landress who makes Dreams (of frocks) for Fair
Women, is going hence to Europe by the Mara-
thon, to put her sons to a school in the
only country where the Oxford bleat grows and
the Eton tone blossoms in the springtime.
Miss Edith Vaughan (of an Irish Vaughan clan)
came out by the Mongolia, and is staying with
Miss Rawson at “ Cranbrook’’—State Govern-
ment House.
The death of ex-Sydneysider Labertouche in
London has set the old identities talking of his
two daughters. One of them married a son of the
then Governor Loftus—that Augustus of the
Hens, who reigned at Sydney in the dark ages
between ’79 and ’B5, just before Carrington
arrived and started to take injured footballers
home in his carriage, and to help shunt trucks at
Bourke. There was so little “smartness” in Syd-
ney in those days that if a woman so much as
brightened her hair and whitened her face she did
it at great risk to her good name. The two ladies
had a swagger bonnet emporium in Bond-street—
London Bond-street, of course. The Sydney
place of that name is only a split between two
rows of grimy offices.
Everyone who calls herself Cultured is agi-
tating the grey matter of her brain cells over the
coming of Dr. David Starr Jordan, one of the
biggest authorities on education, and a man who
knows more about fishes than any other person
living. lam told that it is quite impossible to
sell him tinned sprats for sardines, as people
always do to me, and what he doesn’t know about
haddock isn’t worth naming. Marvellous are the
monographs on fishes that man has written ! He
was the force selected for the colossal work of
organising the Leland Stanford University, which
has an endowment of £4,000,000. While in Aus-
tralia “ he will lecture on methods of education
in the States.” But not on fishes. And lam so
interested in the problem why the breakfast fish is
so dear, and so often has the dull eye of suspicion
in its head.
Two ancient “horn and hide ” families were
united in marriage on May 1 at St. Philip’s,
Church Hill, by Rev. T. Langley—Harrie Bad-
gery to Nina, youngest daughter of E. E. N. Strick-
land, of Forbes. The Badgerys date over a cen-
tury back, and the Stricklands were at Bunda-
burra, near Forbes, long before gold was thought
of in New South Wales.
By the India went Mr. E. Trelawny, of the
P. and O. Co. on a trip abroad; Mr. Gordon
Wesche will fill the temporary gap made by his
absence.
My Brisbane correspondent writes :
The vice-regal party is a-wandering in the wilds of the
lovely northern passages, calling at the various towns
and disturbing the crocodiles on the beaches as it goes.
Miss Scott and the little Thesigers alone inhabit Govern-
ment House, and Arthur Morgan fills the bill of Lieut.-
Governorpj'o tem. I believe he is the first of the native
horn to occupy that office.
Great is the rejoicing among the Commercial Rowing
Club men at the success of their hop on Thursday even-
ing. Thtre was, it is true, no vice-regal light to shine,
for his Excellency is now serving as a lighthouse in Whit-
sunday Passage, or thereabouts—yet things went with a
swing to the tune of a big financial and social success. Nine
young things crept forth into the cold world in the usual
regalia. The most successful fiutterers were Miss Rita
Murphy, third chick of the Hon. P., and your own Miss
Backhouse, a graceful girleen, who came under the black
and white wing of Mrs. Chas. O’Reilly.
Brisbane is excited over the probable partnership of a
gallant capting and the well-groomed daughter of an
ex-Downs squatter. Talking" of engagements brings
me to theatricals, and reminds me that little Aileen
Thompson, the very young daughter of Ear, Eye and
Throat Robert Thompson, was commandeered, at a late
hour on Saturday, to lead the White Ball ballet in
“ The Orchid,” the youthful mummeress who should have
filled the part having a pain in the lower blouse. No re-
hearsal was possible, but Aileen romped through as if
to the manner born, and brought down the house.
Oh, the charity of these Christians ! During the week
the Presbyterian Assembly suspended, for six months,
Dr. Fracltelton, a kindly old minister in charge of the
Ann-street kirk. The alleged reason was “evil beha-
vior” (not defined). The old man was unpopular among
other expounders of the same clan, who found their audi-
ences and stipends thinning, so many of their flocks pre-
ferring the doctor’s rugged harangues to the other fellow’s
“culchered” eloquence.- With the aid of a money-lender
and a few malcontents, thq virile, kindly old doctor has at
length been shot out into the street, a’nd the way those
dear, kind folk gloated over the white-haired man’s dis-
missal, at the annual river picnic of the Presbytery last
Saturday, was something too depressing for words.
There was not one grain of pity for the two old people,
who have now to turn out and begin all over again.
If there is one family in this city which can rest satisfied
in the knowledge that it has fulfilled its duty to the State,
it is the Marks clan, which has founded a little colony on
Wickham Terrace. First, there is Doctor F. 0.; next door
are stepson Doctor Espie Dods and his wife, who have
builded for themselves (with the aid of architect brother
Robin) a weird early-Ann dove-cotlet, and flourish there
with baby. Further up, next door but one, Dr. Alex.
Marks has settled down with his newly-arrived Irish
bride, and I hear that more Marks are expected.
The Queensland Turf Club comes out in a new light;
it is going to lend its energies and ground to succor the
Lady Bow'en Lying-In Hospital by means of a benefit race
meeting.
Yours Affectionately,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, Mat 13, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Operations are growing into something like an
epidemic, and the vice-regal folk are well in the
fashion. Dame Madden, of the sweet pale face,
has had something done to her arm with a lethal
weapon, and daughter Leslie, of the tiger-striped
hair, has been pampered in a private hospital
over one of the long list of mysteriously-named
modern operations.
Mrs. Ross-Soden, of the imposing “Mandeville
Hall,” gave something of a party on Tuesday
afternoon. It was quite above the average “At
Home” for cheerfulness, and the decorations made
one glad. In the music room (which carried all
the faintest tones of mauve and pink), Miss
Violet Mount sang, with piano and flute accom-
paniments; clever little Adrian Amadio was
the flautist—he also played soli, and at times
Miss Coultar, of Ballarat, told funny stories.
The frocking was a thing of beauty. Mrs. Ross-
Soden affected faint pastel shades that were just
the right tone for her flower-decked room—a pale
fainting tint of blue-green in the cloth of her
gown, and a pensive autumn-grey hat drooping
with pale blue plumes. Mrs. Larking displayed
dark green chiffon velvet. One Mackinnon
maiden wore almond-green velvet, and the sister
a rich, deep brown. These warm tones make a
fine effect for autumn dressing, likewise do the
wine and plum shades that are being rushed. Mrs.
Wrixon’s garment was a royal purple, and Mrs.
Vallange’s rig was in so warm a wine color that it
only saved itself from Bacchanalian effect by
being tailor-made. Pretty Ruby Madden sailed
about in suede-color cloth, with a fascinatingly
illogical hat crowned with cherries and cherry
blossom.
Doris Madden, the rather cold-shouldered cousin
of the “Cloyne” folk, is making one more fare-
well for still another trip to Vienna, where she
intends to finish her protracted piano study. The
concert was dated for Tuesday of this week.
Rupert Clarke returns with his literary
daughter, Phyllis, as well as a bunch of mum-
mers. He’s developed much parental pride
since his lass wrote a booklet. All his chums
were presented with a volume, aqd were made
the recipients of the authoress’ picture.
The twentieth century has evolved a new type
of girl for the special edification of twentieth
century men. She wouldn’t appeal to any other
male generation—she’s the typewriter girl. She’s
neat, but not gaudy. In the way of outside
coverings she runs to serge skirts, a shirt blouse,
a collar and tie, and a Dana Gibson arrangement
of what hair she possesses. (She’s not the sort of
girl who’d stoop to purchase tresses, be her own
ever so scanty.) Her face is wholesome —solid,
keen, and often comely, but she lacks to a great
extent all the suggestion of yielding, unreliant,
man-adoring femininity one likes to see peeping
through the eyes of one’s second-best girl. (To
diverge : A man never sees anything tangible in
his very best girl. He just imagines things about
her until they’re married.) To return to the lady
of the Smith-Premier. Her voice has borrowed
some of the snap and rattle of her clattering
machine ; her movements are as brusque as a
business letter. She talks in a condensed, ellipti-
cal fashion, caught from her daily task of taking
hasty notes. And she has a way of disposing of
the most sociable folk with quite commercial
callousness. She’s anomalous, but she’s nice in a
hard, healthy way, is the typewriter girl. Com-
monplace and unique at once, she can occasionally
combine the nicest qualities of one’s best and
second-best girls. That’s what makes her a vast
success, and, now and again, a co-respondent.
Mrs. Tittell Brune, the com motional actress,
also threatens to write a book. As usual it will
be about her travels—their joys, and their dis-
comforts. The world isn’t gladly expectant.
Somehow, a high degree of theatrical success
seems to distort the outlook and dislocate the
sense of proportion in most mummers’ minds.
And the mental deformity is never more obvious
than when they take up their pens and spoil
paper. Actors have instinct, not intellect; but
while there’s a willing publisher beckoning you’ll
never get them to believe it.
The Anti-Sosh Leagues, the Cits. Deform,
and the Cats’ Tea and Political Club have been
enjoying a unique and lengthy entertainment.
For seven days the dames who run the wimmen’s
association have haunted the Banco Court in
their best furs; the court fleas have found what
sable feels like for the first time. Janet Mrs.
Clarke and her ewe lambs, Mrs. Staughton and
hers, Mrs. Hughes, and a score of irate matrons,
turned up with the same regularity as judge and
counsel. And they managed to convert the
legal pomps and circumstances into a burlesque
worthy of Gilbert. Grace Watson, spinster and
defendant, gave a finished performance in the
witness-box, dramatic, but tinged occasionally
with an inappropriate suggestion of low comedy.
She showed surprising self-confidence under the
circumstances, emphasising each hit with a
glance at Janet Missus. Also, she managed to
interpolate some incoherent discourses upon the
purity of the ’ome and woman’s duty to Throne
and Empire, which had no bearing on the business
in hand, but elicited loud applause. During the
hearing of plaintiff Chomley’s cause, ordinary
procedure was suspended. The ladies indulged
in loud laughter, hissed anathema at the immobile
Chomley, chortled praise of their Grace in the
ears of his womenkind, and when one of his wit-
nesses had a heart-seizure in the box, vented
hysterical shrieks, and then audibly disbelieved
in the genuineness of the poor man’s agonies.
Plaintiff 0. sat by and heard nis probity attacked
with some grace (not of the Watson sort). He
showed no animus, and shrouded his sense of
humor in placid dignity. And his sisters and
his cousins and his aunts followed his tactful
example.
C.-J. Madden had a hard time maintaining his
judicial dignity during the scrap. He knows so
many of Miss Grace’s allies and so few of them
know anything of court etiquette. Often a rust-
ling dame would become quite excited on recog-
nising his Honor in horsehair and silk. Then
would commence Mr. Madden’s martyrdom. An
elaborate coiffure all agog, shaking jewelled ears
at him, strained this way and that, seeking his
cold, grey eye. The blank orb moving round the
court, slowly and unseeingly, seemed to pass over
the eager head. The lady who knew his Honor
would bow gushingly, and then subside into
offended quietude. His Honor, in the later days
of the hearing, never dared to look down at the
body of the court, and went home in a cab. And
half-a-dozen frocky acquaintances allege that he’s
“ just horrid.”
It was a pleasing thing, the other day, to see
pretty little Gracie Graham at one end of a
chain, and a big red dog at the other end, sprinting
-down the sedate and doctor end of Collins-street.
Gracie, who is a dog enthusiast, had made love
to this Irish setter, and was taking it along to.be
photographed. Quite a prim maiden in white,
and quite a dignified auburn dog, until a familar
motor shot by ; then a whirr of excited puppyism,
a swirl of fluffy white, and a whirlwind mixing of
honeyeolored locks and blonde dog.
The Glaciarium is being rushed, now the
winter has set in. Something very special has
arrived in the way of lady-professors froqi Lon-
don, and from the Palais de Glace in Parig.
Mrs. (or should it be Miss ?) Carreno, who is
one of the most-travelled of musical artists, has
never visited her mother’s native country—Spain.
That’s where her blue-blooded pride comes in—or,
rather, doesn't come in, because she never goes
there. The mother was a Castilian marcliesa in
her own right, and her haughty poor relations
threw up their hands in horror when she married
Carreno, a mere Republican Minister of Vene-
zuela. It was in South America that Teresa
Carreno was born, the young mother dying shortly
after the event.
There was a swirl of motors and carriages to
Spencer-street, a fluffy gushing whirl on the plat-
form, and much jovial, broad “ Ach,” also a per-
fect tornado of flowers and good-byes, and the
special express toted off the German Opera Com-
pany to its short season in Adelaide. That was
on Saturday afternoon, and quite a lonesome
crowd felt a big blank in its head for want of the
usual Saturday First Night. The opera had
become quite a habit, and the long entr’actes, with
coffeeing in foyer and Winter Gardens as a
distraction, had become a function.
Manager Williamson is bringing out an in-
teresting Edwardes Company. It opens here
somewhere in June, and will play “Blue Moon,”
“ Kitty Grey,” “ The Three Little Maids,” arid
other such frivolities. The list of the co. includes
Miles Clifton and Victor Gouries, whose names
echo faintly from London Gaiety, and tenor
Arthur Groves, known to a few people in Aus-
tralia as one of the beauty men of picture post-
cards.
Some hundreds of adoring students welcomed
Prof. Marshall Hall back to Melbourne in a
special outburst at the Conservatorium. An in-
tensely serious young man named Crowe made a
speech. The Prof, himself absolutely refused to
be drawn intoanythingsoconventional; he laughed
in his best Mephistophelian manner, and thanked
his students, and told them he would give them a
lecture instead in a week or two, when he’d had
time to simmer down. The Studenten felt that
they wanted to present the master with something
in the fulness of their joy, so, instead of a set of
egg-spoons, or an illuminated address horribly
framed, or a laurel wreath, they gave him a roll
of new red baize carpeting for the wide staircase
of the “ Con.” ! It was a glaringly original pre-
sent, and so long as the principle isn’t carried
further, and a returning hero given an acre of
asphalt for his back yard, all is well.
The hearty Prof, is as glad to be back as his
friends are to see him. The Bearded Boy—your
true artist remains a child to the end —has had
wonderful good fortune in his perfect recovery
from an illness which threatened his eyesight.
Jack Dane, of rollicking tendencies when he’s
playing, and of mining themes when he’s serious,
has struck it rich in the Bendigolden City, or
thereabouts. His particular nugget is a sweetly
pretty girl, the younger of the two very
young Hunter girls, who own an indulgent and
wealthy father, and a strikingly handsome grey-
haired Ma. The engagement, though not formally
announced, is murmured about by sweet girl
chums.
Some funny things happened during the seven
o’clock beginning of the “Walkiire” week.
Everybody had managed to be op to time for the
7.30 and 7.40 commencements of previous operas,
but the struggle for the 7 o’clock of the first night
was appalling. Motorswhizzed round the Princess’
corner, and fluffy womenkind did a Cinderella
sprint up the marble staircase. The door dosed
on the stroke of seven, and not all the cajolings
or threats of late-comers could open it one crack.
The guardian of the dress-circle entrance was a
hero. A bevy of men, with a dozen or so of
smiling, coaxing girls, used all their persuasive
arts to be allowed to just “slip in and stand at the
back ” until it was time to find their seats ; but the
janissary was inexorable. Then two very pompous
papas got angry, and explained just how much
money they had put into shares for the opera,
and spluttered about motoring 30 miles—to be
shut out like late school children. Still the door-
keeper refused quietly, and the attempt of the
infuriated late-comers to hurl their huge German
selves through the door and carry the d.c. by
assault, failed.
( JThe opera company was farewelled last week in
the scattered moments that it could spare from
“Die Walkiire.” The strain of such a big per-
formance has been intense. Miss Sara Anderson
was troubled with influenza, though no one would
guess from her wonderful performances that she
was anything but very fit. Mr. Greder, the
energetic basso and stage manager, collapsed al-
together on Wednesday, and was unable to take
his part. On Thursday and Friday he was work-
ing as enthusiastically as ever, and on the last
night gave a passionate farewell speech when
called forcibly before the curtain.
Mr. Riinger, the leading baritone of the opera
company, is the happiest of stout gentlemen just
now. His bride has just arrived from Germany
(they were married only a short time before he
left for Australia). She is a really beautiful
woman, and the members of the German clan in
Melbourne regret that she is leaving them before
they have an opportunity of getting on smiling
terms with her.
A German atmosphere added itself to the usual
crowded Tittell Brune house for the Saturday
performance of “ Leah Klesolina.” Ruby Madden
wore something very white and fluffy, with a lilac
cloth kimono cape that wound up at the peaks
with cunning silk tassels. Mr. and Mrs. Ernest
Brookes and Colonel Campbell (also quite
earnest) took care of Miss Madden. Mrs.
Brookes looked like a handsome forest nymph
in Robin Hood green, _ with snaky-green velvet
coils in her auburn hair. Lady Adeline Strick-
land turned up her aristocratic nose in the front
row, and wore pale blue and a coronet-looking
comb. Mrs. Ernest Raleigh (of the dresses) was
in the front stalls with her nice, big husband, and
wore her very prettiest chiffon velvet. Lots of
folk, who have been trying hard to live up to
grand opera, settled themselves with a satisfied
rustle at the nice, “usual” first-night feeling.
People who had not cultivated the Wagner soul,
yet hankered after the trail of the Smart Set, had
a cruel time while op£ra lasted.
Gaston Mervale must have got huffy over some
detail of the performance. After a big scene with
Leah, he refused to come on to bow acknowledg-
ment to the plaudits. Miss Brunedid not improve
matters by stepping before the curtain at the
third “rise” and declaring, in a chirpy voice,
that “ owing to a misunderstanding ” Mr. Mervale
refused to accept his share in the affable uproar.
You could almost hear Mervale grinding his teeth
behind the stage at the clumsiness.
Gaston Mervale, no longer the saturnine person-
age beloved of matinee girls, is nowadays the
usual escort of a brace of motherly dames, who see
that he doesn’t drink his tea too strong.
Harry Sweeney, the bright young man of the
Brune co., has, after all, been snatched from the
prospective terrors of a deep-sea journey in search
of opportunity abroad. He takes the place of
Pickford of the Profile in the Brough-Flemming
co., and will play lover-like youths till further
notice.
Hal Cohen, the large, soft, promising tenor-
baritone, who took his well-provided way to Hing-
land recently, sends pleasing records of his pro-
gress. The extreme cold, or the example of
sundry successful chums, induced the lengthy one
to put aside his letters of introduction and settle
down to hard study. Bouhy, his teacher, is proud
of the result. At the same time, gracious Melba
smiles on the aspirant, makes much of him, calls
him “Faust,” and extends her capable hand to
help him along the hilly road. Which all bodes
well for the Ballarat boy.
“ Rosna ” writes from Adelaide :
Adelaide has been suffering from a Cup week. All the
squattocracy left its lambs browsing on a thousand
plains, jammed its best clothes into the tonneau, and
motored madly to town. The frock-builders had a wild
and hairy time, and most of them had to provide “ young
persons ” to pin their frenzied clients into the unfinished
splendor at the last moment. The first day was unex-
citing and disappointing, partly because there was a
boat-race raging elsewhere, and partly because most oi
the dames and demoiselles were nursing their charms for
Cup Day. It requires moral courage to wear the same
garment to two successive race meetings, and in this
inild, economical State it is considered a sign of unre-
generacy to possess anything beyond the conventional
“ best frock.” Charming Grace Palotta, in a confection of
fawn voile, criss-crossed with dark brown velvet, paraded
the expanse of herbage on Cup Day ; and another visitor
of attractive frocking, who wandered on top of the sheep-
food, was Mrs. Hope Osborne from _N.S. W. She sported a
gorgeous robe of emerald green voile, with cream passe-
menterie embellishments. Governor Le Hunte was back,
and pervaded the landscape with Mr. Baillie Hamilton
of the many names, taking off his distinguished plug hat
(was it a plug?) to the fair frockers. Notable frills were
worn by Mrs. Charles Warren (a gorgeous lavender silk),
Mrs. Herbert Rymill (pale blue), Mrs. Adolf Gebhardt
(copper-colored gown with pompadour passementerie,
and copper-colored plumes waving on her blue silk mil-
linery), Mrs. Arthur Rymill (lovely green pinafore rai-
ment over Limerick lace vest, sleeves hooped with green
velvet, and white plumed hat), Mrs. Albion Tolley (a
cream and pink effect), Mrs. Green (handsome brown silk
with insertings of floral silk, latticed with narrow velvet),
Mrs. H. W. Varley (navy cloth, white satin and velvet
trimmings), Mrs. Bleechmore, Mrs. d’Arenberg, Mrs.
Deeley, and others.
The Palotta-Ward-Willoughby Company has departed,
after filling the theatre nightly. And some time in the
middle of last week the populace began treading each
other under foot in the wild stampede for grand opera
seats. At time of writing the rush still continues, and
long streams of patient male adjuncts are sacrificing
their few remaining hairs on the altar of duty. I know
one mate who plunged into the crowd 15 times. Four-
teen times he rebounded empty-handed, but finally he
succeeded in getting the seats he particularly didn’t
want, somewhere wheie you can’t see anything, and
everybody treads on your feet. He was looking jaded
when he came out, and his coat was rent at the seams.
Perhaps one in a huudred of the audience will be able to
appreciate good music and understand German. The
other 99 don’t know anything about music, and-have
merely seen a German sausage in a shop window.
Miss Hannah Cohen, accompanied by her father, fled
on the 14th to Westralia, where she will be married in a
gorgeous blue travelling frock. The popular damsel’s
trousseau is a thing of joy, and the verdant neighborhood
of Green bushes, which she is shortly to adorn, will enjoy
revelations.
Died in Adelaide last week, Mr. E eneeer Cooke, Com-
missioner of Audit for some 22 years. Deceased was a
clever amateur musician, an authority on art, an enthu-
siastic collector of pictures, and a skilful wielder of the
brush. He leaves a widow (who is related to the Fishers,
Herbert Phillipses, and various other well-known Adelaide
folk), and a grown-up family, several of whom are
graduates of our ’Varsity.
Our chief institution for the advancement of melody
arose and had its first concert of the season in the Elder
Hall last week. Governor Le Hunte gave the powerful
encouragement of his countenance from the front row,
and the Salt of the Earth was present. The Conserva-
torium hasmuch youthful genius in its ranks just now,
and not a few of its ornaments have gathered honors in
the other States, against all comers. Soprano Clytie
Hine and pianist Carlien Jurs took the largest iaurel
wreath away with them.
Tortoise Town is suffering from an epidemic of private
masque balis, whereat Edwin dances with the wrong
Angelina, and plain Miss Clodd has the time of her life.
What time Adelaide is not frivolling in this fashion it is
giving girls’ fancy-dress carousals, a form of dissipation
which, in spite of masculine scoffing, has survived from
last season. The male element is strictly excluded. It
is confidently expected that by the middle of the season
said male element will appear at the carousal door on all
fours, and beg for admittance to the revels once more, and
state humbly that it is willing that its best tall hat should
be used as a slop-basin at refreshment time.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
Despite his much-advertised provincialism,
Premier Carruthers’ policy speech at Kogarah
was largely stolen from Yic. Premier Bent; and,
in its essentials, amounts to little more than a
replica of Bent’s own programme. The biggest
point of all, the decision to resume the Cooma-
Bombala lands before the railway is constructed,
is simply Bent’s Western District scheme over
again. The abandonment of the Arbitration
Court in favor of Wages Boards is another Yic.
idea. The only thing about the programme that
isn’t taken from Vic., or some other Australian
State, is the proposal to remit the income-tax and
the stamp duties.

MUDDY.
There ought to be far more good men offering
their services to their country to-day. When you
ask men why they will not come forward, their
reply is that they refuse to enter an arena
where a man’s equanimity is so much disturbed,
his character assailed and besmirched ; they re-
fuse to enter the turmoil of public life with all its
disadvantages.— Cockyruthers.
And Joe speaks from experience.
One of the Howard Smith Co., at the in-
spection of the new coaster, Cooma:—
Broadly speaking, the Australian coastal trade was
better catered for, and at lower prices, than the coastal
trade of any other country in the world.
But he said nothing of a country so excellent in
ship-services being capable of building its own
ships.
