POLITICAL
POINTS
Some people would like to know if
Jokearruthers, when he is censoring the papers
that are to be bought for free distribution in
London, bans those which contain his abuse of
the Australian Government.
CURIOUS FORMATION OF STORM-CLOUD
OVER AUSTRALIA.
I am quite aware that Australia will fight hard against
giving away any territory or fostering our aims in any
way, but Australia has to choose between a black popula-
tion and a Jewish white one. Australia is vvaking up to
her folly and her possible future collapse. The Jews
could possibly safeguard her future as a white territory.
Thus we are in a position to dictate our own terms.—
Zanowii.l, the Jew, on Australia.
An unspeakably dead thing is the Upper
House in which only rich property-holders are
qualified to sit, and for which only moderately
rich property-holders are qualified to vote. Of
the recent Westmoreland (Tas.) election it is re-
ported :
The polling was small in all districts, and the returning
officer declared that the rolls are shockingly inaccurate.
They have not been used for thirty years.
All that time William Dodery and his pal were
returned unopposed, because it wasn’t worth while
to put up another Tory just like themselves to run
against them.
A few doses of Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy will cure your cold, and perhaps save a
doctor’s bill later on. It always cures.*
Carruthers announces that he proposes to
go to the country as an insect buzzing on the
end of the Dry Dog’s tail: —
The political party to which I belong has associated
itself with a party over which Mr. Reid is leader, and
people should he entitled to express their opinion at the
(State) elections as to the conduct of Federal affairs.
There is no particular reason, either, why people
shouldn’t express their opinion on the conduct of
Federal affairs at the election of aldermen, or in
connection with the appointment of a municipal
dog-killer; also there isn’t any particular reason
why the election of churchwardens shouldn’t turn
on the conduct of Carruthers. In fact, there are
churchwarden candidates who would rather the
election turned on anything except their fitness
for churchwarding, and that is what is the matter
with J. Carruthers. The State elections turn on
State politics, and J. Carruthers has been in
charge of State politics for three years, and he
seems desperately anxious to change the subject.
Eastern papers mentionedlately, asanother
instance of how large estates are being cut up for
closer settlement without the
assistance of any iniquitous land-
tax, that the Westrahan Midland
Railway Co. had just held an-
other sale, and had permitted
the public to acquire no less that
73,781 acres for £46,382. It was
a very good yarn as it stood;
but since then the auctioneer’s
“ property report ” has been pub-
lished, and it transpires from said
report that Emanuel Bros, and
S. J. Phillips in partnership, or
Phillips alone, brought 25,364
acres out of the 73,781, and
Francis Pearse bought 8860 acres,
and the Harlots 6395 acres, and
the Holmes Bros. 6526 acres in
one line; and the Emanuels,
and Pearses, and Darlots and
Holmeses were already spread
over vast areas before they raked
in these further little lots. So
instead of closer settlement start-
ing, the big estate disease has
been aggravated.
Why was it cabled out to Australia that
Edward VII. had a conversation lasting 15
minutes with President Fallieres, of France ? It
was only about long enough for the genial monarch
to say “Can you keep one down this morning,
Fallieres ? ” and for the ever courteous Frenchman
to remark that it was the first he had had that
day. Seems that a poor King can’t have a wet
without a reporter spying upon him.
It is solemnly proposed that a referendum
shall be taken in N.S.W. at the coming State
election to decide whether Dalgety shall, or shall
not, be the capital of all Australia. Not a
referendum of the people of Australia about the
Australian capital, but a referendum of the people
of N.S.W. about the Australian capital.
Carruthers hasn’t heard of Australia yet,
so he doesn’t see the humor of the situation.
And the chances are that Joseph will die
without discovering Australia. He is getting
too old to go on exploring expeditions.
“ Hassan ” : The Commerce Act is all very
well. It stops cotton passing the Customs in the
masquerade of tweed. It stops “ Oby’s ”
“silk merveilleux” (B. 25/4/’07)
unless it sheds its silken pretence.
It treats Brum jewellery as
severely as the Excise ought to
treat Stinking Fish. But what
good does that do ? As soon as
the entries are passed, off comes
; the honest label. Do we see
“artificial or imitation silk”
stamped conspicuously upon the
“silk merv.” of the retail shop?
Does nobody ever buy Brum as 18
carat ? Please explain, somebody,
how the Commerce Act protects
the general public, which doesn’t
deal direct through the Customs,
but with the mostly dishonest per-
son who takes the tell-tale labels off
as soon as they have served their
turn. What is wanted is an Act
which shall make misdescription of
goods by the retailer a criminal
offence. If there are any honest re-
tailers, they won’t object; but I im-
agine that the howl of apoplectic indig-
nation at such a proposed “restriction
of trade ” would once and for all convince
the very simplest soul that Diogenes
wouldn’t find much difference between
Athens and one of our cities. What
difference he might find would probably
be in favor of Athens.
The soulless, old private enterprise con-
tractor’s theory that anything, or almost anything,
is good enough for a soldier dies hard. Witness
this, from a Hebald account of a visit to the
Cooervvull Woollen Mills, where material for
Commonwealth uniforms is manufactured :—
On being shown a sample of kharki, our representative
remarked that it seemed almost too good for soldiers’
clothes. “ You are right,” replied the manager. “ It is
too good for men who have to sleep on the ground and per-
form military duty."
To anyone but shoddy-loving newspapers, it
would naturally occur that men who perforce
have to sleep on the ground are the men
above all others who require the very best
clothing. At all events, even at the risk of
another “Federal extravagance ” yelp, the Federal
authorities should intimate, by a system of rigid
inspection and ready condemnation, that clothing
too good for the men who may have to sleep on
the ground in doing their country’s work can’t be
made.
Tired in the morning? There are times
when the night’s rest doesn’t fit you for the day’s
work. Those are the times for a stimulant that
will brace you up and stay by you. The best of
all tonics is Wolfe’s Schnapps.*
Our friend the Jap continues to squeeze
out the foreigner. Comparing his shipping re-
turns for the first months of this year and last,
his own tonnage trebled, while foreign tonnage in
Jap ports fell 15 per cent. The foreigner is ever
so welcome in the Monkey cage, provided he can ■
live on air and veiled insults.
THE EDUCATION OF LEO.
The Standard relies on the educative influence of the
colonial statesmen to induce the motherland to give
reciprocal preference. It observes that even in the disas-
trous month of January, 1906, when the general election
took place, nearly one-half the nation was prepared to
accompany them “the whole road.”
Deakin : “ Jump, you beggar /”
Tasmanian Governor Strickland leapt into
the arena of politics to plead for the right of the
State Frights crowd to get representation at the
Imperial Conference. He effectively damned his
cause with this advocacy :
Moreover, by reason of the constitution of their Upper
Chambers, the State Governments represent the voice of
the middle classes and of vested interests, together with
a certain continuity of policy, in a manner which cannot
be represented by a Commonwealth Government, having
two Chambers elected on one and the same universal
suffrage.
That represents practically the main reason why
the Australian democracy is resolute to have
more Federalism.
The ancient phrases “extorsively re-
ceiving” and “by color of his office,” tossed
about lately in Sydney Central Criminal Court,
prompted Justice Simpson to advise the Govern-
ment to construct an up-to-date code. Chief Jus-
tice Higinbotham did that for Victoria, and did it
well; also Chief Justice Griffith did something
very good in the same line for Queensland. The
N.S.W. Government spent some £16,000 in con-
solidating statutes a few years ago, but so shame-
ful was the waste of money in feeing junior bar-
risters that the work was broken off short. Judge
Heydon, now on the District Court Bench, had
undertaken to do the business without pay, but
he was permitted to employ help.. The result was
that an army of young men was furnished with
scissors and paste'pots, and turned loose on the
statutes. They cut them to pieces, and gummed
them together again with an immensity of labor,
and to the great detriment of their clothes through
stickphast. But there was no visible result, and
at long last the Australian Star called the
attention of Parliament to the fearful amount of
paid “help” that was at work, and Parliament
sacked the earnest young men and threw the
gum out of the window.
A politician writes :
Premier Oarruthers of N.S.W., finding that buzzing-in-
a-bottle like an irritated beetle isn’t heard outside to
any appreciable extent, threatens to deal with the Federal
capital site question after the manner of the confidence
man. He proposes at next election to put the question to
a vote of the N.S.W. electorate: “Will they have the
capital at Dalgety or will they not?” Now supposing,
just to humor the Insect in the Bottle, that such a
referendum will settle the question, and assuming
that the right of the electors of Australia to
the choice of their capital does not exist, and
taking it for granted that'a majority of the N.S.W. people
are pig-headed enough to try to dictate to the
Commonwealth as to where the capital is to be, the way
in which Oarruthers proposes to solve the difficulty is
worthy of a political magsman. Apart from that section of
the community (and The Bulletin thinks it is the largest
section) that believes Dalgety to be ab ut the best avail-
able site, and the other section that honestly has its
doubts, there are all the barrackers for every other spot
that has a possible or impossible show of being chosen.
All these will, of course, howl Dalgety down, just as they
would poke borak at the Jasper Sea, if that hallowed site
werr put forward when they wanted a capital at the abat-
toirs, and just as vehemently as they would howl at each
other. Lyndhurst, Yass, Tumut, Sawyer’s Flat, Dead-
man’s Hollow, Biddulph’sßackyard, and every other place •
that thinks itself in the running will deliberately
combine to vote Dalgety down, and they would do the
same to each other, if submitted one by one. If the In-
sect must play with a referendum, let him play fair, and
put up every available site at once. Then the place with
the most lively lot of barrackers will come on top, and
though that fact won’t affect matters in the slightest,
Oarruthers can strike an attitude, and say that he was
honest. The leagues, associations, and cliques now be-
hind Oarruthers openly say that they support him
because they consider him the bad best of the bunch at
present. If a referendum were to be taken on the
bare question : “ Oarruthers —Yes or No ” —Joseph would
get a ten times worse bump than the Dalgety site will
ever get.
Help the weak if you are strong,
Respect the old if you are young,
Own your faults when you are wrong,
And when you’re angry hold your tongue,
Pay your debts before you bet,
Back not a bill for rich or poor,
And when a cough or chill you get,
Take some Woods’Great Peppermint Cure.
“ 0.K.” : A recent Plain English reference
to the sins of the “Reputable Employer” in
Victoria jsa little out in its statement as regards
the starch trade. Starch manufacturers present a
cleaner front to their critics than the following
figures—quoted from The Bulletin article—
would indicate. You say :
There are 131 employees in the trade, of whom 73 are
adults ; 42 are females and 31 males, and the average
adult wage of £1 Os. lid. stuck in the Wages Board’s
throat. So the matter is to go to the Court, which will
practically have to decide whether there is such a person
as a reputable employer in the whole boiling of them.
The correct number of adults employed in starch,
making is 94, and their average wage is £llls. 7d.
This, though poor enough, is 60 per cent, higher
than £1 Os. lid. The terms offered by the em-
ployers are: 365. per week to all adults of two
years’ experience ; 335. to those who have served
12 months in the trade; and 30s. for the first-year
workers. Presumably there is something to learn
before a starch-maker acquires proficiency. He
or she learns the whole of that something in two
years, and is then valued at 365. per week. The
inducement is not much for “reputable employers”
to blow about, but it looks considerably better
than the 30s. lid. wage they are accused of. That
is the average for all employees, boys, girls
and men.
At last a real live man (he signs himself
“ S. Beaumont ”) has arisen and written to Syd-
ney Telegraph on the Australian capital question.
This is his letter :
With no authoritative interpretation of Clause 125, it
seems to be a wilful and useless waste of time and money
to go a-hunting after sites for the Federal, and, relyingin
the good faith of the people, decide that the charge for
admission shall be not less than one shilling each, to be
collected during the interval the public, relying on the
good faith of the promoters, fill the hall, the interval
takes place, the collector starts with his box, Is. 6d. it
offered (50 per cent, more than the sum set down in the
bill), the offer is rejected, and one pound demanded by
the promoters and claimed on the capital. The Federal
Parliament will fool this State point of view. Suppose
Mr. Reid engaged to deliver an address in the Melbourne
Town Hall, say, on equity of good government, the pro-
moters confident of success, in Melbourne all the time.
How does their preposterous claim to an unlimited area
and any distance they choose from Sydney' look from an
everyday business to the top of its bent, and keep the
seat of government ground of that sum being not less
than a shilling.
The Flinders (S.A.) election on Saturday,
May 18, ought to lead to the death of somebody.
Warren stands for the Price Government and
franchise reform, while ex-Methodist parson
Foster, who was Works Minister under Butler
and Jenkins, is the chosen of the National Ass.
Foster once posed as an advanced Radical, but
now he is Tory and National Ass pure and simple.
Three Ministers are in the district this week
fighting for Warren, and pretty nearly the whole
drove of Ass is also there fighting for Foster.
There is scarcely enough hotel accommodation in
the district for all the Ministers and all the Ass.
Premier Price went forth in person, and made
hair-raising statements about Foster’s record
when he was in office, and the ex-preacher replied
in print:—
I have just perused the characteristic slanderous state-
ments of Mr. Price at Gumeracha. They are absolutely
false, and he knows it. I wish I could in open court
command the presence of Public Works officers and the
production of official documents. I would then abun-
dantly satisfy the public as to what manner of man this-
malicious, vindictive, designing, coarse, vulgar, blatant
slander er, who is Minister of Education and Premier of
South Australia, is. “ These be thy gods, oh Israel 1"
Then Price repeated his statements, and quoted’
official documents in support of them, and the
battle had become a shockingly violent row when
this issue went to press.
THE SECESSIONIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The builders built, in a mood sublime,
A House for All;
But a rat has come in the early time
To work its fall.
He paws his muzzle and gnaws his bone,
His hide is sleek ;
He threatens the steadfast walls of stone
With an angry squeak.
The rat is king in his mouldy den,
His cellar dim,
And the light that visits the souls of men
Is not for him.
The paltry goal and the petty gain
Are all his dream,
And the mean, small spites of a little brain
He holds supreme.
Where the slimy streams of treason flow,
He lies and laves ;
The rat is ever a rat, and so
As a rat behaves.
His teeth are keen, and he squeaks his power,
His petty pride ;
If the rat bide not in the topmost tower—
Then woe betide!
In the House for All we are friend and friend,
And may not swerve
From the common cause, and the common end,
We speed and serve.
Our strength is little enough ; 'twere less —
A thing for mirth—
If a chair were given to Bitterness
Around our hearth.
Clear reason tells us that, once and all,
Our only fate
Is this: To stand as one, or fall,
A ruined State.
For never a wall was built so stout
That it could win
Sure triumph over foes without
And rats within.

SPORTING
NOTION
A healthy sign of the times is that the Aus-
tralian who believes in home-made goods is getting into
an aggressive frame of mind. He is starting out to prove
that his country’s products are not only equal to, but, in
many cases, better than, the imported ones. In this con-
nection, the trans-continental bike ride of Messrs, Lennie
and Warren takes on a new and national aspect. It arose
out of an argument as to the relative reliability
of Australian-made and imported bicycles. Lennie,
who is a carpenter, stoutly maintained that the home-
made (Australia is home) wheels were the best, and when
someone suggested that he should try one on a journey to
Sydney, he offered to do it on a path racing machine.
Hitherto heavy roadsters, which were said to be the
“ best imported,” had been used ; but Lennie and Warren
swore to do the trip on Australian path racers just to
drive the argument home. They left Perth on February
25th, mounted on racers built by the Davies-Franklin
Go., of Ballarat (Vic.), and using light road-racing
tyres made in Australia by the Dunlop Co. Lennie
weighed lOst. 41b. and Warren list. 131 b. when they
arrived in Sydney, and in addition to this they carried
between 301 b. and 401 b. of luggage on machines that
weighed 221 b. and 231 b. respectively. Friends and rivals
prophesied disaster, especially if they struck bad weather
and heavy tracks. And they did. They had 10 days of
rain before getting clear of Westraiia, and the tracks
became too awful for words, but the filagree machines
came through it all without a crack, three small tyre
punctures, which were easily repaired with the pocket
outfit, representing the total casualties. From Melbourne
to Sydney the pair used road-racing tyres of slightly
heavier make, and the machines are now, at time of
writing, on view in the window of Grffiths’ tea
emporium in George-street, Sydney. As an ad-
vertisement for Australian-made bikes and tyres
this will take a lot of beating. The locally-made
bike is fast supplanting the imported article, but
after this tremendous demonstration of its toughness and
reliability, Australian riders have no excuse for buying
imported machines, and every inducement to stick to
those made in the land in which they live. Hitherto
the wildest Freetrader has always based his preference
for imported goods on the false premises that the local
article is inferior; but when 451 b. of Australian-made
machine and tyre carry 391 lb. of man and luggage, or
Sit), lloz. for every lib. of machine and tyre, the gag
about “ superiority ” becomes a very discredited
chestnut.
The languishing industry of path-cycling (it
cannot fairly be described as a sport) threatens to revive
somewhat next season. Major Taylor is announced as
a certain visitor, and Lawson has offered to display his
hind legs for £lO per night, while MacFarland asks only a
modest £6 ss. Taylor, it is understood, will accept the
same fee as Lawson. The negotiations are being con-
ducted with Victorian sports promoters—which is
another injustice to the Ma State ; but, no doubt, the
undutiful daughter will allow the poor old mother to look
at the imported legs if the parent will part up sufficiently.
Said that many of the cyclists who raced under the
auspices of the Cyclists’ Ass. are realising that the Ass.
is a foundered brayer, and that the present state of
affairs is far from satisfactory. The racing cyclist has a
tender pocket, and the League is still almighty to hit him
there, as its disqualifications are recognised all over the
world.
* * * *
The average Yankee paper can give even the
Sydney dailies long starts in the lying stakes and then
w»in easily. The latest lovely gag is that Australian pro-
moters have offered Major Taylor £2OOO for 12 races. Had
the papers stuck to its own beloved dollars, the
amount would still have been far in excess of what any
Australian promoter could afford to offer Ixion if he rode
his wheel round both Sydney Cricket Ground and Ex-
hibition Oval the same night.
•V * * *
Sydney saw some wild-cat tussles on the Rugby
fields, on Saturday last, when a clear, crisp atmosphere
filled the impatient ’bailers with a fierce desire to wrestle
with bulls or tratncars just for the fun of breaking them.
No bulls being available, and the men in the tramcars
keeping their charges at a safe distance, this incan-
descent mass of fiery energy was let loose on the opposite
side, and, as they say in the classics, the results were
great.
The match of the day was that at University
Oval, where Newtown and Glebe—adjacent electorates,
always keen rivals, and dead-level in the competition—
met to decide which of them should go down a step. It
was a strenuous struggle. Fifteen thousand people
looked on and perspired with excitement and
the making of noise. On the Oval, the players
made the game faster and faster, each side vainly striving
to utterly exhaust the other, and sometimes the gasping
teams took a firm grip of each other, and swayed blindly
about the field. Newtown had the better of the first
spell, hurling Glebe dow r n on its line again and again,
and crossing it twice. Towards the end of the half Glebe
came again with a series of rushes, but, failing to get
across, the first half ended with Newtown leading—six to
nil. In the second half the Glebe men pulled themselves
together. There had been too much individual play
before this, the forwards i rying to do everything. Now
they concentrated their energies on getting possession of
the ball from the scrum and feeding the backs. The
effect was striking. Newtown was put on the defensive
at once, and Glebe, like Napoleon at Waterloo, let loose a
whole series of charges on the enemy’s line. None of
them got across owing to the strenuous defence, but a
penalty and a field goal placed Glebe in the front, and,
before Newtown could equalise things, time ran out,
leaving the Butchers winners by one point.
When St. George met Eastern Suburbs it found
an indifferent pack mightily sustained by a combination
of first-class backs. D’Alpuget was like a rock whom
there was no shifting, and Messenger was backed
by three other demons like unto himself. The
names of these devils were Corbett, Stuntz, and
Rosenfeld. The first half closed with Eastern Suburbs
six to nil. The second half saw St. George
improved a lot, and the Eastern backs played a
defensive game that was good to watch. St. George
played with desperate eagerness, and for a time it looked
as though the Eastern men would be overcome. St.
George had notched six points, and a chance goal
might ruin everything. Eastern team changed its forma-
tion, playing seven men in the pack and letting loose a
winger. But it was a failure, and East was thrown back
on its line with St. George struggling to get a chance to
pot a goal. The whis le blew at a most interesting stage,
and left Eastern Suburbs victorious with nine to six. At
Sydney Cricket Ground, Balmain was hopelessly over-
whelmed by South Sydney, which won by 13 to three. At
the Sports Ground, Sydney District and Western Suburbs
drew with six points each ; and at far-away Manly the
local team was trampled under foot by University—a case
of 22 to nil.
* * * *
In the competition, Glebe and University have
now established a substantial lead with six competition
points each. Eastern Suburbs, Newtown, South and
North Sydney are all level in the second line with four
points each, and behind them come Sydney and Western
Suburbs with three each. St. George has two, and Manly
and Balmain none.
* * m *
Maoriland Rugby Union is taking no chances.
Association football is gaining ground somewhat, and the
Rugby Union, determined to do something to check this
growth, has started operations by voting £2OO to each
branch for the encouragement of the Rugger game among
school-boys. As everybody knows, the Association is the
old, original form of the game, and at the present time
is also gaining ground in New South Wales and Wes-
tralia, although in the latter State it has the disadvantage
of having to play without the stimulus of gate money.
* *• * *
N.S. W.'RugbyUnion, which hasnotbeenmaking
money in the prolific manner of the Metropolitan Union,
is casting around for ideas whereby expenses may be re-
duced. It proposes to begin a self-reliant policy by
asking the Railway Commissioners to reduce fares on
football teams, and will approach Mr. Johnson on the
matter. What the S.M. Herald’s attitude is has not, so
far, been allowed to transpire. Anyhow, it is to be hoped
that the new Railway Commissioner keeps a specially
sharp axe on the premises for the benefit of deputations
of the sort
* * * *
On the cards that a N.S.W. Rugby team will
tour Westralia some time this season. The N.S.W. Union
has offered to:finance the jaunt on the understanding
that, after ground charges have been paid, expenses are
to come out of the balance. If there is still a margin, it
is to go to the Westralian Union, to be used in “ fostering
the game,” for the Westralian Rugbyites regard the pro-
gress of the rival branches of ’balling with considerable
apprehension.
* * * *
In the Sydney “Soccer” competition Glebe
and Balmain hold pride of place, having won both
matches played up to date, with four points each.
Ultimo is secorid on the list with three, and Pyrmont and
Rozelle have two each. Petersham, the newly-formed
club, has one, and his Majesty’s navy flies the sign of the
hen-deposit. The trouble with the Navy was that the
men were, in comparison with the others, quite out of
condition. Whether it is that the Soccer men practise
all the year round, or are naturally harder than any
other kind of footballer, writer knoweth not, but, some-
how, they seem to start their seasons in better condition,
and play their game in a manner much superior to that
of the other two brands of footballers.
» * * *
Sydney League footballers (Australian rules)
are sadly hampered by the want of proper enclosed
grounds on which to play. At present the League games
are conducted in open parks, and the excited barrackers
encroach on and hamper the players. Sometimes they
even rush in among them when the ball gets close to the
touch-line, with advice as to the best possible means of
disembowelling the opposition men. This sort of thing
is now quite general every Saturday afternoon, and it is a
beastly nuisance that will have to be got over somehow
before the game can go ahead as it ought to. It will
mean a lot of expense to the League, and at present that
body is hardly in a position to stand the strain.
Cannot some League official design some sort Of temporary
barrier than can be erected before the mill, and taken
down when the ambulance is removing the wounded and
the dead ?
* * * *
The surprise of Saturday’s play was the defeat
of Newtown, last year’s premier club, by Redfern. The
figures were fairly decisive, too: 46 points to 23! The
unexpected result was due to the preponderance of sure
kickers in the Redfern team. Newtown men held their
opponents too cheaply at the beginning, and by the time
they realised the fact it was too late for the discovery to
do them much good. During the last half the crowd
mingled freely with the players, and it was hard to keep
the ball in motion. The crushing defeat was that of Pad-
dington by Balmain. Paddington made a most miserable
display. North Sydney downed East Sydney in an easy,
comfortable way, by 59 to 31 points, and Sydney scored
32 points while the good young men of the Y.M.C.A.
were piling up 23. This last was the best-contested
match of the day, and play on both sides was distinctly
good. The result of the scramble was to leave Sydney
and Redfern at the top of the competition list, with four
points each. North Shore, Newtown, Balmain, and
Y.M.C.A. are dead level, with two each, and East Sydney
and Paddington are still in the slough of despond, repre-
sented on paper by a round dry nought.
* » * *
In the Victorian League games St. Kilda con-
tinued its victorious career by walloping Essendon, thus
constituting itself the only undefeated team this season.
Its barrackers already prattle prematurely about winning
the premiership. Seeing that the club never occupied
anything higher than third place, and that was a genera-
tion ago—in 74, namely—it is about up to it to get a
move on. The game against Essendon was fast and
strenuous but wonderfully unscientific, the outstanding
points being the adhesiveness of the seasiders’ hands
when the ball came near them, and the wonderful way
they lasted. Essendon is notoriously fast, and endeavored
to play the others to a standstill; a rushing, impetuous
third quarter left it two points ahead of Saints, yet the
last spell saw the latter pile on 5 goals 4 behinds
while the tired ’Dons could score but a pair of scraggy
points. It was a hard and well-won victory, and St. K.
deserves the shekels that will accrue therefrom pre-
sently. Carlton met Melbourne and, remembering St.
Kilda, took no chances with a weak team. The resuit
was a comfortable victory by 86 points to 47. Colling-
wood met “ Skinner’s hirelings ” on the South
ground, and seemed to have contracted paralysis
on the way down. At quarter time the score was, South
34 points, ’Wood ONE point. The Magpies woke up a
little thereafter and finished with 38 points to South’s
66 ; which was a disgraceful licking. ’Wood seems to
have forgotten that its system won’t keep in working
order unless the machinery is kept constantly greased
and free from rust, so to speak ; also that other teams
have adopted and improved on the same system, to the
originators’ manifest disadvantage. Geelong—the sup-
posed hopeless, disorganised Geelong—came up to Fitzroy
and, to the horror of the local barrackers. walloped last
year’s runners-up on their own ground by 77 points to 64
—and ’Roy hasn’t got over the shock yet. In the
Association the money at the back of Essendon (A.) tells
its tale ; the six captured League players placing it head
and shoulders over all rivals. Richmond was the victim
this time, by 78 points to 61. West Melbourne made an
awful show of Brunswick—by 14 goals 14 behinds to 1
goal 5 behinds ; while Prahran bestowed a similar favor on
Preston—by 121 points to 25. The other games don’t
matter. One Richmond player dislocated his shoulder
while taking a high mark, and will duly appear on the
balance-sheet as medical comforts or boots or something
for a month or two in consequence. Or he may be a hot
sea bath.
tt m ■* *
It looks as though the Victorian Football
League had bitten off more than it could chew in the
case of W. Monagle. This man with the curious name
was employed by the Melbourne Football Club, which
seems to be the winter edition of the Melbourne Cricket
Club. The League raised a rumpus, and ordered the Mel-
bourne Club to dismiss Monagle, because he once had
the impudence to ask the Essendon Club for payment for
football rendered. When the Melbourne Club seemed
disinclined to dismiss Monagle, the League threatened to
withdraw the final matches from the M.C.C. ground unless
Monagle got the bounce. Then the Melbourne Club, with
both eyes fixed on the gate, bounced Monagle with great
precipitancy. But the public is taking quite a large
interest in the case, and the spectacle of the League
demanding a man’s dismissal for asking to be paid is
greeted with coarse guffaws. Certain it is that not one
of the secretaries of the clubs that form the League is
game to make a sworn statement to the effect that his
club does not remunerate players with hard cash. A
hostile press has challenged the secretaries to do so, and
up to the present there is heavy silence on the part of
the scribes. And if there isn’t at least one sworn
statement forthcoming, then the Monagle case will stand
out as a piece of paltry hypocrisy and football snuffle. It
is possible to be a whited football sepulchre just as much
as a whited church sepulchre, and ’ball snuffle is just as
disgusting as Sunday-school snuffle.
The Wednesday Football League—which plays
its games on the mid-week * shops,” halt-holiday—is
making a fast run into Melbourne’s favor. It boasts
seven clubs—St. Kilda, Essendon, Collingwood, South
Melbourne, Footscray, Prahran, and the Police. The
Fire Brigade team is thinking of joining in place of the dis-
banded Tramway team. Wednesday is now such a general
half-holiday in Melbourne, and the form is so good, that
the “gates” begin to be as well worth watching as the
play. The first matches of the season, on May 1, gave
’Wood, Saints and the Police a win over South, Essendon,
and ’Scray, and the “ Johns ” threaten to become a first-
class sporting draw. They run a stone and a-half heavier
than average teams ; yet, though the initial matches
were played in pouring rain on slushy grounds, they beat
Footscray by 42 points to 0.
* * * *
The fact that the Australian army contains only
10 per cent., or thereabouts, of marksmen, as mentioned
in the issue of 9/5/’O7, suggests that something ought to
be done to induce the sporty character to join some
corps or another, and devote some of his spare time to
becoming a rifle-shot of reliability and accuracy. The
Bulletin has no great faith in rifle clubs as schools for
military marksmen. The members of these associations
mostly practise at known ranges —which is fatal to the
military marksman. When the Asiatic army marches on
Sydney, it will not choose the rifle-butts for the battle-
ground. Even the behind-the-times British War Office
does give some slight encouragement to its soldiers to
thoroughly learn to use their weapons, and in the
Imperial forces a marksman may earn from Id. to 3d. per
day extra for good shooting. Nothing of the sort exists
in the Australian army, as far as writercan discover ; and
it is time it did. Also, practice ammunition ought to be
served out more liberally. The trouble about the
rifle association man is that, even when he is a
good marksman, he is not wholly a soldier ; there
are other things than marksmanship necessary to the
soldier. Also, it is necessary that when the scrap starts
the Defence authorities should be able to put a large em-
phatic finger on every good rifle shot in the country, and
have a place in the defence machine ready for him tostep
into.. But the great crowning objection to the rifle club
man, as already mentioned, is that he is a shooter over
known ranges. What is wanted is a system of training
by which the Australian soldier, when he sees an Asiatic
soldier in the distance, will know by the size of the blot
he makes on the landscape at what range to shoot to
kill. And that seems to be the one thing that the rifle
clubman does not learn under existing conditions.
* * * •
N.S.W. Motor Club’s “outside” race on Satur-
day was a howling success. The course was a 17-miles
run from Shark Island, in Port Jackson, to a mark off
Long Reef and back to the island. Members and guests
followed in the steamer Greyhound. But when the
Greyhound got outside the whole company started
schnapper fishing. Apparently it caught so many that
the steamer sank under the weight ; anyhow, the Grey-
hound didn’t return to see the finish of the race, and the
Fishing Editor is still absent without leave. Only six
motor boats started, the limit craft being Hinemoa with
a handicap of 72 minutes. L. J. Davies’ Goo-ee was on
scratch, and the other four started at lengthy intervals.
On paper there was nothing in it but Coo-ee, but the race
is not to the swift, and that fine cruiser blew out the
packing' of the cylinder head, and had to heave-to for
repairs. Having fixed up its engine, the Coo-ee’s crew
started on the long stern chase, and made a gallant effort
to cut down the long-markers. Regal, which started with
53|min. handicap, was leading, closely pursued by that
loveliest of all Australian cruisers, the Simplex, which is
a sort of floating villa. The Coo-ee came snorting through
the foam, but could not make up lost time, and was
beaten on the line by two minutes. The Regal came in
first, with the Simplex ssec. behind. It was a great
finish, with only five minutes between the first and last
boats.
* * • *
The American motor-boat Dixie, which made a
record of 25'532 nautical miles, 0r29'396 statute miles, for
the hour at the Palm Beach carnival, has been sent to
England as challenger for the British International Cup.
In case of accident to the champion a second boat will
also be sent, but the name has not yet transpired. The
Dixie is owned by Commodore E. J. Schroeder, is 40ft.
long, and is fitted with engines of 132'70 h.p. The most
formidable defender will be the new Daimler boat, of
270 h.p.
* * * *
George Whatmore, late secretary of the Motor
Boat Club of N.S.W. (hereinafter known as the Sydney
Club—-the other’s too long), who was Clerk in Charge cf
the N.S.W. Forestry Department, is now on six months’
leave of absence. When his holiday is over he will “ chuck
it,” and turn his attention partly to commerce and partly
to politics. At present, he is whizzing round the
North Coast in an imported motor-car. While he
is whizzing, somebody is perpetrating to his design
a racing freak, which is to be shaped like a shark,
the bow being an exact reproduction of the objec-
tionable creature’s head. This fearful creation will be
19ft. long, with a beam of only 2ft. 9in., and will be driven
by a 16h.p. Aster engine.
* * * *
The sculling match between Ford and Kemp
on the Hunter River, at Raymond Terrace (N.S.W.),
was well worth seeing. Ford, although not in
the same condition as Kemp, was faster and stronger,
and going to the front in the first 100yds., stayed there
till the finish. What made the race exciting was the pro-
found belief of the people along the banks that Kemp was
holding his man nicely, and would come with a final
sprint that would land the glory and the £2OO. As the
finishingline drew near the barrackers howled, “Now,
then, Kemp, make your sprint 1 ” But Kemp couldn’t,
and Ford won by a length and a half.
• * * *
News comes from Port Darwin (N.T.) that
Senators Dobson and Foxton have been buffalo shooting.
No one ever suspected Van Dobson of the Baresark spirit
that drives a man to slay big game. They returned to
the main Parliamentary party per motor-car after three
days of thrilling adventures, but as they did not bring
the dead buffaloes with them, there is a dark suspicion
that the buffaloes pursued Dobson, rather than the other
way about.
* * * ■*
The N.S.W. bowling semi-final for the Rawson
Cup commenced Saturday last. The Cup is at present in
the possession of Ashfield, and that club shows a firm
resolve to hang on to it as long as possible. City came at it
on Saturday, and after as Homeric a struggle as is pos-
sible at bowls, was defeated by 96 to 93, which was veiy
close indeed. Petersham threw Victoria Park out of the
competition by defeating it—the figures were 113 to 84 —
while Chatswood scored full points with the bye. Chats-
wood and Petersham will fight it out on Marrickville
green next Saturday afternoon, and the winner will meet
Ashfield the following Saturday at Randwick, and decide
the ownership of the Cup.
It is definitely decided that a Canadian lacrosse
team will visit Australia, arriving here about the second
week of July. Most of the players will be University
graduates and students having a look round the Empire,
and they are to pay £4O each towards the exes. Lacrosse
enthusiasts expect that football of all sorts will die after
the visit of the Canadian ’crossers.
* * * *
An Adelaide sportswoman, a widow, has had
the shocking bad luck to win a 100 to 1 double with a
bookmaker who was not good for the coin. Undis-
couraged, she charged him in the police court with the
“larceny” of her sovereign; but the P.M. remarked
coldly that there was no evidence to show that when
accused took the pound he did not intend to carry out
the contract; so the lady had no satisfaction that way
either.
* * * *
Adelaide Cup meeting was a great occasion for
the ladies. Mares won six of the twelve races on the
first two days, including the Cup itself, the Goodwood
Handicap, the Leger, and the S.A. Stakes for two-year*
olds. They also ran second and third in the only weight*
for-nge race of the week, the beating of Pius b3 r Red
Streak being a big surprise. Miss Grace Palotta was at
Morphettville on Cup Day to see the Port Admiral filly
named after her win a Nursery Handicap.
* * * *
Yic. country racing clubs are moving to form a
properly-elected board of control to manage the sport in
that State. As this would put the V.R.C.’s nose
grievously out of joint, that self-constituted boss may be
expected to fight the project. As the V.R.C. has no
higher legal status than the V.A.T.C. or the Croajingo-
long Race Club, each having the management of its own
course only, the country clubs have both law and equity
on their side ; but, when the delegates meet in Melbourne
during Grand National week, that won’t prevent a lot
of unpleasant remarks being passed round and several
strenuous resolutions aimed at the self-constituted
governing body. Whatever happens, racing in Victoria
certainly does need a controlling body with power to
enforce its decisions and regulations.
♦ * * »
“0.K.” : The more that Bent’s Anti-Gambling
Act comes to light the more it seems to have been in-
tended to excite public disgust rather than give satisfac-
tion to the vast majority of reasonable Victorians, whose
name is not Judkins. Bent and co. made out their vexa-
tious Bill as though there were no possible means of
squelching “totes” and ready-money betting shops,
without including church-bazaar raffles, charity euchre
parties, and all sorts of harmless gambles in one general
squelch. Because a sprinkling of professional puritans
asked for puritanical legislation, whilst the country in
general wanted nothing more than a simple, honest
treatment of the real gambling evil, the Government
pretended that the whole trouble was a question of Puri-
tanism or lawlessness. And, being bound to do some-
thing, Bent did what was most calculated to excite indig.
nation against Judkinsism, whereas he might as easily
have passed a Bill which would meet the wishes of the
respectable sinful public, and treat the Nonconformist
Conscience as a thing of minor importance. As the
matter stands at present, there is danger of an amending
Bill which, if not carefully watched, will put the old
gambling evil back on its dirty pedestal. What Bent
wanted not to do he deliberately overdid.

Edward Hodges died the other day at Southsea,
England. There was nothing notable about Mr. Hodges;
he merely bobs up owing to the fact that it was his
mother’s way of throwing cricket bails, when a girl, to
her brother, the late John Willis, that suggested to that
person the over-arm bowling of which he was the
originator.

# • * *
The battle between Ed. Williams, list, ljlb.,
and Ted Nelson, lOst. 91b., raised great expectation
in the bosoms of the frequenters of the Melbourne Athletic
Pavilion, but the display was very disappointing. In
previous battles Williams, despite his marked advantage
of weight, height, and reach, won from Nelson only with
a small margin of points, and on this occasion he won,
but with a still smaller margin. Williams should have
done better than this, but with youth and strength and
size to his credit he fights a waiting battle against
a man like Nelson, whom on appearances he should
be able to consume off the shell. But appear-
ances are deceitful, and there isn’t a more puzzling
boxer in the Australian ring than the Sydneysider.
The fight went 20 rounds, and then Referee MTnerny
was unable to decide. Yet in rounds 10 and 11 Williams
had Nelson feeling so tired that he could only just keep
his end up. By a little more enterprise and ferocity at
this stage Williams might easily have finished his op-
ponent, but he did so little that spectators began to scoff
and to laugh the metallic laugh. The battle was a square
deal, however, and the fact that Nelson saw 20 rounds
through was due to the bad tactics drilled into young
Williams, who makes little attempt to get at his man
when he is “foot and point and eye opposed” toafirst-
rater. The Melbourne boy won on points in the opinion
of the referee and two judges. Nelson’s condition never
seemed too good, and he should not have survived the
eleventh round. Considered as a spectacle it was poor.

And at ADELAIDE and BRISBANE.
The S.M. Herald is already annoyed
with its tin god Johnson. In some parts of Ma
State —in the Riverina, for one —there is an
actual state of drought, and railway potentate
Johnson has been deputationised and asked to
allow fodder to be carried at infinitesimal rates.
Johnson said he would knock 25 per cent, off the
rates in special cases only. The Herald is
horrified, and points out that Oliver (who was
sacked through its influence) consented in a simi-
lar crisis to charge a mere nominal rate, and the
least that was expected from Johnson was a
reduction of 75 per cent. Therefore the Herald
is disappointed in Johnson.
Small-boy Carruthers’ incessant mouthing
at the Australian Government has got on the nerves
of even his own political friends at last, and the
other day Bruce Smith was pestered into applying
the slipper where it would do most good :
I have wondered whether Mr. Carruthers has suffi-
ciently considered that in taking' up the “ capital ” ques-
tion he is trespassing' on the domain of Federal functions.
Those of us who have been returned by the people of
N.S.W. to watch the interests of their State in the Com-
monwealth Parliament have had delegated to us the con-
duct of this controversy ; and it seems to me to be a little
irregular, at least, for Mr. Carruthers to assume an attitude
which seems to imply that we are not doing our duty, or
not doing it well enough ; so that it has become necessary
for him as a State authority to brush us aside, and
attempt to do our work for us. I should know what to
expect if the Federal members were to arrogate to them-
selves some important State matter, and thereby imply
that Mr. Carruthers is failing in his duty as a State poli-
tician.
From The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant:
The old stone house at Mosman, known as “ The
Rangers,” is the subject of some newspaper controversy
in Sydney just now. It is said to have been built by Mr.
Henry Stuart Russell, a Queensland pioneer and a
member of the early Legislature of that State. Mr.
Russell arrived in Sydney in 1839, and soon'after made
his way to the Darling Downs, after Patrick Leslie and
other forerunners. The house was built in the very early
’forties, by Mr. Oswald Bloxsome, who had a station at
Rangers Valley, up Inverell way. In the mid-’forties
Oswald Brierly, the marine artist, being ill, was the guest
of Mr. Bloxsome, and while there painted a scene on the
dining-room wall, “The Rattlesnake in a Storm,” and
the picture is there still. In the mid-’sixties Mr. Stuart
Russell was tenant, and since then the house has had
many occupants. It is built after the Gothic fashion.
The ancient bell, which dates back 60 and odd years ago,
still hangs in the belfry, and is said, on occasion, to ring of
its own motion—ghosts probably.
“Hexham”: At Cook anniversary the
O’Sullivan declared April 28th, 1770, to be the
greatest day the Southern world had ever known.
But what about the dates when Da Gama doubled
the Cape of Good Hope, and De Solis entered the
River Plate? The former showed the road to
both S’Africa and Australasia; the latter
pioneered the Way for the establishment of eight
republics, with a present population of 33,000,000,
half of which, at any rate, is white. Are these
discoverers to be left out of reckoning just because
they were Dagos ? By-the-way, contrary to uni-
versal belief here, English is not the predominant
language of the Southern Hemisphere. It only
comes third to Spanish and Portuguese, and as
things are going will find itself behind Kaffir also
in 20 years time.
Move on!

Died last week, “ Bob ” Clark, one of the
old-time fine oarsmen that N.S. Wales pro-
duced. He went early into the Commercial Bank
of Sydney, where, under the benevolent eye of
the veteran T. A. Dibbs, banker and yachtsman,
the staff breathes hard cash and athletics. There
probably isn't another big concern in Australia
which so warmly encourages physical vigor in its
staff; and at 75, after 40 years spent as general
manager, Mr. Dibbs, still firm on his feet and
without a bend in his back, is the finest fruit of
the policy. His assistant, T. B. Gaden, has been
so long in the bank that 40 years ago he was an
inspector; and it looks as though H. H. Massie,
another inspector, whose mighty hitting every-
body who ever took any interest in cricket knows
about, will continue the traditions when he is
moved up.
Premier Kidston, besides being a first-
class ironmoulder, is fascinated with chemistry,
and once had visions of becoming something dis-
tinguished in that line. While working at his
trade at Alloa (Scotland), he attended a technical
school established in connection with the South
Kensington (London) Institution. He entered
himself as a student in the chemistry class, but
as only one other name was put down for that
class it was not formed. Kidston thereupon
determined to study with the aid of text-books
and apparatus made by himself. He sat for the
South Kensington examination and passed, gain-
ing a certificate. He continued his studies, and
to get tuition secured employment in a Glasgow
foundry. _ In the winter he worked at the
foundry; in summer he toiled at the Andersonian
College. His ability attracted the attention of
Professor Thorp, the eminent chemist, who re-
commended him for a position in the chemical
department of a leading English foundry ; but
unpropitious circumstances did not allow him to
take it, and he had to stick to his trade till he left
for Australia.
“ Albatross ” writes :
While the dispute between the Shipowners’ Association
and the Merchant Service Guild was being settled in the
Australian Arbitration Court, one of the officers’ claims
was that all ships should be furnished with proper charts
and navigating instruments ; same to be kept in a chart-
room handy to the bridge, where the officer on watch
could use them at a moment’s notice. But Mr. Justice
O’Connor, after much ponderous thinking, sagely re-
marked that “he saw no reason why the chart should
not be kept in the skipper’s room.” On top of this conies
the case of the Easby, which furnishes a very obvious
(vhy, and further substantiates this claim of the Guild’s.
In giving evidence at the Marine Court Inquiry in Mel-
bourne, the second mate of the Easby said he had never
seen the chart since he had been in the ship, hut the cap-
tain had charts of his own in his room. He furthermore
stated—Are you listening, Mr. O’Connor?— that the owners
supplied no charts for the use of the officers in charge !
I ask Mr. O’Connor to picture himself, without any law-
books to refer to, being required to give a learned judg-
ment at a minute’s notice after he had been awake for 22
hours. That, is the position a ship’s officer is often in. And
if his learned judgment—given suddenly, without a chart,
which is the sailor’s one law book, and given when he has
b-en without sleep for a period that probably Mr.
O’Connor never dreamed of —is wrong, he is sure to lose
his billet ; he may lose his certificate and be disqualified
for any other billet ; and he may st rike something even
worse. How would Mr. O’Connor like it if his billet were
run on those lines'?
The Victorian detective force and its
Chief Commissioner, O’Callaghan, have plumed
themselves no end on the smart capture of young
Kurt Schonherr, the German boy who slew a vast
old reprobate—at least accounts point towards
that noun and these two adjectives—named
William Panton, up in the bush at the back of
Woodend. But really the detective force has
little to pride itself on. The German boy is a
remarkable-looking fellow, only 17J years of age,
who has picked up English in the last 12 months
or so, and speaks it atrociously. He was seen with
Panton by dozens of people, and yet it took the
defectives a whole week to find out that it was a
German they were looking for. Even then they
would not have been sure if Kurt had not told
them at the first time of asking. Much was made
of the fine Sherlock Holmes clues followed up by
the defectives; but nobody has explained why
they preferred to chase infinitesimal bits
of blue quilt instead of simply looking
for that rara avis, a 17-year-old swag-
man who couldn’t speak much English. Kurt
cannot read a word of the British language, and
didn’t even know the force was looking for him.
Had he been able to read he might have been at
Oodnadatta or Timbuctoo before the d’s discovered
that they wanted him. He had a good week’s
start, but he made the. game dead easy by staying
within 30 miles of the scene of the crime. The
case was too simple for words.
As the N.S.W. State election approaches
we get a recrudescence in the constituencies of
such lamentable stuff as the following :
He(Dr. Arthur) now believed in the principle of grabbing
all he could, and letting the devil take the hindmost.
The State’s prospects were good, and the revenue was
crowing ; therefore he thought he might as well join in
the general scramble, and get all he could for his own
district.
Deafening cheers, of course, from the audience of
“convinced anti-Socialists.” And at one time
Dr. Arthur didn’t have to appeal to that sort of
thing, but stood out boldly as an honest, non-
borrowing candidate.
“ A. 8.” : A despicable attempt to arouse
public feeling against the Arbitration Court is
made by that very stout and bingied monopoly,
the Sydney Gaslight Co. (By the way, charac-
teristically it calls itself the Australian Gas-
light Co.) On the circulars which accom-
pany its monthly accounts there appears, in
big, black type, a notification that the prices for
coke are unchanged, but that, owing to the award
of the Arbitration Court in respect to carriers, an
increase has been made in the cost of delivery.
For the ex-Premier of one Administration
to take office in the Government of a political
opponent is not uncommon in N.S.W. William
Forster and John Robertson were given to that
sort of thing. Parkes hankered for coalitions
when he found the foundations going from under
him, but always stipulated that “ Hi shall be
Prime Minister.” Waddell’s move is the least
significant of the lot, however, from a party
standpoint. It is a curious circumstance, by the
way, that ex-Premier Peacock of Victoria took
his bag and baggage into the enemy’s camp a very
short time before N.S.W. ex-Premier Waddell
carried his swag and billy-can to the same desti-
nation. And ex-Premier Propsting (Broken
Prop) of Tasmania crawled over to the enemy a
year ahead of them.
There is nothing to fear from using Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy. It is safe and sure.
For sale everywhere, Is. 6d.*
The Shipping Combine which controls the trade
between Australia and Asiatic ports is raising a
storm which threatens to assume huge propor-
tions. This Combine plays the usual rebate
business. It contracts with the regular shippers
at so much a ton freight, and at the end of 12
months a certain large percentage is refunded in
the way of rebate. But should any shipper, at
anytime in the year, load goods on any steamer
not in the Combine, he forfeits the whole 12
months’ rebate. Lately Melbourne and Sydney
have been rushed with orders from Japan for
Australian flour; but the merchants cannot find
any way of delivering the goods. Every inch of
space in the Combine’s steamers is taken up as lur
ahead as July, and most of it as far ahead as
November. The Combine won’t put on extra
boats. It says the reason is that the back
freights won’t pay, but it is quite possible that it
hasn’t the boats handy, or, more likely still, its
monopoly has made it lazy and unenterprising,
and it is sunk in a rut. For a while the Melbourne
and Sydney millers raged at each other, and
charged each other with annexing too much of the
scanty available space. But that did no good, and
was mere childishness. Now some adventurous
spirits—pillars of commerce, too—say that a State-
owned line is the only remedy, and reckon that
Socialism is a mighty good thing at times.

“San Remo”: Writing 10 days before
the election, and not yet personally heated by the
conflict. I say that, after 18 years spent in
Queensland, I cannot recall one general election
which has smelt so badly of corruption as this
one. I remember the smells of the MTlwraith
elections ; but this is worse than they. In the
MTlwraith day the thing was done openly;
every pub was somebody’s pub. You could see
the thing that smelt; to-day it is hidden from the
eye, but not from the nose. The suddenness with
which various candidates—including one or two
members —retired is part of the smell.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

Years a»go The Bulletin abolished the
words “colony” and “colonial” as far as Aus-
tralasian territory was concerned; and now the
Imperial Conference cuts the terms out of the
Empire’s vocabulary, to the despair and indigna-
tion of Downing-street. Even the Sydney
Herald notifies acquiescence in “the recognition
given that the ‘ colonies ’ are to be regarded as
such no longer, but as self-governing, equal States
with the mother country, facing the world, and
sharing the high privilege of nationhood on equal
terms.” After this, Australians shouldn’t hanker
after “honors ” from any “ equal State.”
The anti-Sosh Yic. farmer can be as Sosh
as you like so long as it is gratis. When it isn’t,
he is a good anti-Sosh again. Lately, the farmers
around Terang arranged for a series of agricultural
lectures, and a number gave in their names as
students; but when the department proposed to
collect a trifle of £2 or £3 in all, to help pay for
advertising, hire of hall, &c., so few turned up
that the class had to be closed. These bucolic
persons weren’t asked to pay for the instruction—
merely a shilling or eighteenpence a head for a hall
to give it in ; and they preferred to lose the in-
struction.
Those who administer the Immigration
Restriction Act are very tender towards the fined
shipmaster as soon as the prohibited immigrant
turns up. Though the usual fine is £lOO the ship-
master can get it all back, less actual expenses, if
a capture is made. In the first nine months of
this financial year 42 shipmasters were fined in the
Commonwealth, and six of the fines were remitted.
So it works out that the fine is not imposed for
allowing the prohibited immigrant to land, but for
not recovering him after he is landed. But the
fine ought to be for subjecting the country to the
risk —just as a shipmaster is fined for bringing
up his boat loaded with gunpowder to the wharf,
even though the gunpwder doesn’t explode.
Six feet of dusky native recently strolled
into the apartments of the M.L. Minister of
Lands at Auckland, announced himself as a
Maori deputation, and stated his views on rabbit
destruction with remarkable lucidity. He de-
clined a seat, but dropped his mussel-bag and
flax hat, and explained : “I te pig chief. I stand
up to korero. No goot te plurry rabbit-poisonin’.
You pay te thruppenny again. That’s all; kia
ora/” After absorbing a “long peer,” hos-
pitably pressed upon him by the Minister, he
gathered up his shellfish and effects, and shuffled
off homewards. There are strange by-ways in the
life of a Maonland Minister.
Significant scrap from a recent address to
his English company by “ Sunlight ” Lever: —
There was increased competition each year for oils and
fats for the manufacture of butter substitutes. These oils
and fats had hitherto been used mainly, if not entirely,
for soapmaking ; the result being that while “ butter ”
was cheaper to the consumer, the price of soap-making
matei'ial was higher.
A lot of things may happen to a country that
goes the whole hog on the dogma of cheapness.
One of them is soap as a substitute for butter.
Some time since a few savage Tory ship-
owners and grain-shippers called the Australian
wharf lumper “a loafer,” because he agitated for
the reduction of weight in bagged grain. Now
Melbourne Age reports of the meeting of the
local Chamber of Commerce
The Cape Town Chamber of Commerce wrote drawing
the attention of the council of the Melbourne Chamber
to the complaints received that bags of wheat shipped
from Australian ports are filled to such an extent that on
arriving at ports in Cape Colony great difficulty is experi-
enced in obtaining laborers to carry them from the ship
during the process of unloading. It was mentioned that
the average weight of bags from South Australia and
Victoria in one shipment went as high as 2721 b., and some
of the bags actually weighed 3001 b.
And men ;who were called loafers lumped those
bags and stowed them ! It’s a pity that the Em-
ployers’ Federation is not forced to one shift a
year at grain lumping.
Characteristic -Australian paragraph, this
time from Cairns, North Queensland :
A German- boat last Friday took from this port 150
tons of ore, and 14 tons of cotton, which is the product
of Mr. D. Thomatis’ cotton, of the celebrated “Cara-
vonica ” variety.
So Australia digs up its copper, its wolfram, its
tin, and its lead, and doesn’t even get the job of
refining it of the dirt with which the things are
mixed; and it grows cotton, and doesn’t get
the job of putting it through even the first pro-
cess of manufacture. Germany, with its high
Protection, and its steamers subsidised to bring it
the raw products of fool-countries, gets all the
work that means skill and high wages. Australia
is just the country with the shovel and the hoe,
and the casual laborer; and G. Reid and theFree-
trade papers talk about “our great natural
industries,” while Germany grunts its satisfaction,
and adds millions to its wealth and population.
In her recently-published book of reminis-
cences, touching the origin of a personage whose
name is so often heard in ordinary conversation,
Lady Dorothy Nevill says : “As a politician,
.John Robinson was a great favorite with George
111. Hisl political career was a long one, for he
was member for Harwich for 26 years. He was,
on one occasion, bitterly attacked by Sheridan,
who, denouncing bribery and its instigators,
replied to cries of ‘Name, name !’ by pointing to
Robinson; on the Treasury bench, exclaiming at
the same time, ‘ Yes, I could nan.e him as soon as
I could say “ Jack Robinson !” ’ Thus originated
the saying still current at the present day.”

where, Is. 6d.*
“F.H.R.”: A Bulletin scribe (B.
11/4/’O7) writes of “ the silence of a deserted
mine.” fie evidently has never been on his lone-
some in a big empty mine, or he’d find more queer
noises than he would care to listen to. I worked
half a night shift on my own in a Gippsland mine
once. My mate had slept in, and I was expect-
ing him every minute, and every minute I heard
some noise which I thought was him. Timber
cracking, ground moving, and other sounds I
could not distinguish, were exaggerated by the
surroundings until it seemed as if a thousand
devils were on the rampage. If I stopped work
for a while the utter loneliness of my position got
on to my nerves, and the weird expectancy of
something awful about to happen filled my soul.
When my mate did arrive it sounded more like an
elephant walking over empty kerosene tins than a
mere man walking along a level. Could Vari-
schetti thoroughly describe his sensations it would
make interesting copy. A mouse nibbling at a
piece of crib paper gave me the fright of my life
that night! .
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. report re wool
“During the past week a good inquiry has existed for
local scoureds, and we have disposed of a few important
lines at highly remunerative rates. Auctions will be re-
sumed on 21st instant, extending over three days, ours
being fixed for 22nd instant, when we purpose bringing
forward about 1000 bales, the bulk of which will comprise
the new clip from Queensland.”*
Thomas Gainford, aged 54, a N.S.W.
Treasury official of over 30 years’ standing, with a
salary of £250, and with seven unsatisfied judg-
ments against him, was on Thursday refused a
certificate of discharge in Bankruptcy. His lia-
bilities when he filed amounted to £1660 19s. 7d.,
and the available assets were £5 10s. 4d., salary
due. It seems an amazing amount of credit to
balance on top of so little salary.
Sydney Evening News has found the
most picturesque anti-Federal perverter to date,
one F. J. Hodges, who wants to know —
How it was that the (Federal) capital site was not de-
cided upon by the first Parliament, which was distinctly
PROVIDED FOR IN THE CONSTITUTION.
Hodges didn’t mention the clause in the Consti-
tution which “distinctly provided” that the
Federal capital should be decided by the first
Parliament. Naturally he didn’t, the alleged
clause being pure invention. What wonderful
inventors these anti-Australians are!
The removal of James Hogue from the
N.S.W. Chief Secretary’s office to the Education
Department is a sort of political come down, but
it will be a great relief to many. Jim used to be
pompous when Minister of Kids on a former
occasion. The dignity of his office was supported
as if it consisted of tiers of belltoppers one above
the other, all resting on his marble brow. But
since he became Chief Secretary the sense of his
responsibilities strained every faculty to the last
limit of stretch. If he hailed a cab he was
conscious that the eyes of the world were on him,
and that unimpeachable conduct was demanded.
He was a Ciesar’s wife (or as Caesar would have had
his wife) in breeches. Some hint that it was the
strain of his presence that broke up O’Conor and
Ashton.
The Age will begin to be a Victorian echo
of Carruthers, if David Syme doesn’t watch out.
A recent sub-leader objected bitterly to Queens-
land and Westralia sending agents to entice Vic-
torian farmers away to the northern and western
States. The wail proceeded :
Even if no particular harm were done, it is a waste of
effort for the larger Australian possessions to endeavor to
tempt people from the smaller and more thickly-peopled
States. The same energy, directed into its proper
channel of securing immigrants from overcrowded Europe,
would confer a national benefit upon the whole of Aus-
tralia, whereas to take a few dozen farmers from Gippsland
to Queensland is a mere transfer that leaves the Common-
wealth stationary, as far as population is concerned, and
arouses a feeling of injury on the part of the State sub-
jected to this “friendly interchange.”
It really doesn’t matter to Australia what State
the Australian lives in, but if David Syme has
the provincial love of country why doesn’t
he help Victoria to retain its farmers by advocating
a graduated land-tax ? That would break up big
estates and supply the farmers with land.
The Melbourne “authorities” are just
now considering a nice point in public policy or
social ethics or something. The Yarra bank
orators, Tom Mann, Chummy Fleming, andat times
sundry even less responsible persons, have been
in the habit of using the slope where the late
Victoria’s statue now stands for the Sabbath ex-
pounding of their platforms. There is still plenty
of room, and the speakers and their immediate
following would probably do no harm to the lawns
and flower-beds ; but the hoodlums, larrikins and
loungers who are ever attracted by a crowd have
no such care for the public property; consequently
the Sunday orators have been assigned a pitch on
the other side of the river, next the Morgue. As
nobody ever willingly visits that dismal locality,
the orators apprehend a considerable shrinkage in
their audiences, and are calling the Heavens to
witness this gross interference with the “ right of
free speech.” The jroint that is causing the
argument is, “ Which is the more important—the
right of free speech, in a place where it will be
widely heard, or the cate of the lawns and gardens
that belong to and are revelled in by the public ? ”
Meantime, while the abstruse point is being
settled, the more indignant Yarra-banker, who
gets wilder as he goes along, seems to be insisting
on the inalienable right of every man to tread on
his neighbor’s cabbage, if there happens to be a
sermon or a speech or anything like that on the
other side of the vegetable.

THE NIGHTMARE OF THE NORTH.
[FOT YHR BULRYIN..]
The more extreme section of the Queensland State
Labor party has broken away from Premier Kidston, and
is opposing him wherever it can manage to unearth a
candidate—thus giving Philp an opportunity to get back
into office.— News item.
From the tomb a spectre rises—
Black and tan its ghostly guise is—
’Tis the resurrected Vampire which did flap in days of
yore ;
’Tis the private spook of Cohen,
Thanks to whom that State is owin’
Unto anxious Isaac Mo, of London town, P.N’s galore !
Lo 1 the banished Spook of Squander
Lifts his banshee-wail o’er yonder,
And the northern sky grows darkened with the cloud of
I O U’s ;
Yea, the wailing, shroud-wrapped loafer
Seeks his old, auspicious Ophir—
Seeks to hand rich Queensland over to the bankers and
the Jews.
Ho! the jobs that stank to heaven—
They were numbered ten times seven ;
Are the bankrupt days returning with their swindle and
their stain ?
Hear the paean of the jobbers—
Hear the singing of the robbers—
Hear the assets whizzing pawnward when the old crowd
comes again.
Lo 1 The wild cats they shall flourish—
Bursting banks the States shall nourish,
And the sheep-like, plundered people once again be driven
forth ;
Pass ! ye spendthrifts of the ages—
Dim hands holding blotted pages—
For the great Arch-Wastrel hastens like a rocket through
the North !
Prate no more of care and thrifting—
Down the stream let us go drifting—
Drifting doomward as we drifted in the boom-cursed days
that were ;
Solvency’s a useless bubble —
Philp shall speed ye at the double-
Speed ye on through mire and trouble to a fitting
sepulchre !

Packet of Ten lOd
Australia is represented at the confer-
ences in London by four politicians—Deakin of
Victoria, Lyne of N.S.W., Hughes of N.S.W.,
and Dugald Thomson of N.S.W., and two of these
four come from Sydney. Not one Queensland,
3.A., Westralian, or Tasmanian is on the delega-
tion. If the matter is to go by States, V ictoria
has a shade less than its share of representation,
and N.S.W. has nearly three times its share,
while the other States have been robbed alto-
gether. Yet Joey Carruthers of Sydney is the
only State Premier who is grumbling. “No
one,” he said, “reading the reports would im-
agine there was such a place as N.S.W.” Well,
who in thunder expected them to know there was
such a place as any individual State? Thomson,
Hughes, and Lyne have sense enough to know
that they simply represent Australia, and so they
talk of Australia.
The Board appointed by N.S.W. Govern-
ment to inquire into the re-granting of the im-
provement leases which Willis and others obtained
for various persons, “at the usual fee of Is. per
acre,” has held its first sitting. When it assem-
bled at the Lands Office it was confronted by what
looked like the whole of the Sydney legal
profession. Every barrister and solicitor of note
seemed to be there on behalf of somebody or
somebody’s mortgagee, and the least that the
legal profession can do is to offer Willis a com-
mission on its takings. As a matter of fact no
one appeared for Willis. The Board, at this
meeting, did nothing but answer a storm of ques-
tions pelted at it by the Bar. One question it
didn’t answer was whether, in determining the
future destination of the land affected, it would
consider present day conditions or those prevail-
ing when the leases were granted, and the legal
profesh seemed anxious about that point.
Melbourne’s divorce cases at the present
sittings, being sought less for desertion than for
misconduct, drew larger crowds than usual. One
giddy little co-re, abarmaid, being discovered in the
dark bar parlor at midnight with her arms round
the boss's neck, was peremptorily escorted to a bed-
room by the indignant wife, who then locked the
door with them both inside, and spoke firmly and
dispassionately to the lady through the rest of
the night, and in the morning induced her to sign
a confession of her (and the husband’s) guilt.
This quaint little fairy actually came into
court and denied the confession, saying the
other had “forced” it from her. But Justice
Hodges, having ascertained that she was still
keeping the bar and the house for her publican,
the wife having departed after' the confession,
cynically remarked that she might now go and
put her arms round the gentleman’s neck ad
libitum, and hoped the pair would appreciate the
process the better now that there was no legal bar
to it. Another case was that of a country station-
master, who, desiring to occupy a certain pretty
cottage with his young wife, was only granted a
ease on condition that the owner was taken in as a
boarder. This petitioner was so trustful that he
wentaway on fishingexcursions without the lodger,
and only suspected mischief when the wife fled
with the said lodger and declined to return.
Even then he Went and -interviewed the pair in a
calm frame of mind, and when the betrayer offered
him £loo—as compensation or bonus or something
—he placidly declined ;he didn't kick him, “ be-
cause he didn’t think of it.” Perhaps the fact
that this hubby abbreviated his wife’s name,
Appoloymee, into the pet-name of “ Snook,”
accounted for much of the trouble. Still a
sympathetic jury awarded him the full damages
claimed, £5OO.

head was one solid sore, all over his
scalp; his hair all came out, and he
suffered very much. I had a physician
treat him, but at the end of three
months he was no better. I remem-

had cured me, and after giving him
two bottles of Cuticura Resolvent,
according to directions, and using
Cuticura Soap and Ointment on him
daily, his eczema left him, his hair
grew again, and neither he nor myself
have had any eczema since.”
As to his own case, Mr. Barnett says:
“ I suffered with eczema, a burning,
itching, breaking out on my face for
more than a year. I took treatment
from physicians for several months,
which' did no good. I was then
advised to try Cuticura. I took
six bottles of Cuticura Resolvent, and
used Cuticura Soap and Ointment
freely every day. I began to improve
soon after starting to use them, and
when I had taken the six bottles of
the Resolvent my face had become
clear, and all eruptions had left me.

Ointment in our family now for
general use, and it keeps our skin
soft and healthy. I cheerfully recom-

cases of eczema. A. C. Barnett,

FIRST AGAIN
In the TARGO PLORIO RACE, de-
cided in Sicily on April 21st—a contest
ranking- second in importance to the
Vanderbilt Cup and Grand Prix—the first
six cars were all fitted with
Michelin Tyres.
This race was run over roads that are more than usually
bad, and through one of the most mountainous
districts in the world, and is the most severe test of
endurance and reliability that a car or tyre can be
subjected to.

THE DIRGE OF THE OLERK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twice a hundred thousand hotly
Struggle city-wards to-day ;
Clerks in office—men in motley
Puppets name them as you maj r ,
They ve surrendered hope and honor
In the commonplace of slaves ;
They’ve betrayed their birth and manhood,
Marching to forgotten graves.
Sons of townsmen, sons of farmers,
follow on the city’s lure 5
Hath the country lost its charmers,
Aye the plains and ridges poor ?
Pity them, ye men of sinew,
Carving out your men’s careers,
There is nothing small within you,
Men of action, pioneers 1
They are artificials—shirkers
Of life’s true primordial rule,
Doing work of women-workers,
Clinging to a tripod stool ;
Bound in service, soul and body,
To the ledger and the desk,
Puffing out their little shoddy
Manhood in a mien grotesque.
They must smoodge and cringe for ever
To self-seeking underlings,
And account their best endeavor
Nothing in the scheme of things ;
Was it thus your great forefather
Pushed the primal forest back—
That his son desert it ? Bather
Be genteel—an office hack 1
Sons of Britain, Celt and Saxon,
Here are lands to conquer still •
Dally not an image waxen
When we’ve empty plains to fill,
Thirsting for the sweat of conquest,
Listening for the tread of man ;
Ye’ve been lured upon the wrong’ quest,
Till ye wither ’neath the ban 1
N.S.W.
W.
