Headache, Indigestion and
Constipation.
+
AN IMPORTANT DISCOVERY.
It will interest sufferers to know
that a valuable medicine, called Froo-
toids, has been discovered, which is
now completely curing each of tile
above-named complaints. Frootoids
are elegant in appearance, and plea-
sant to take, and, what is of the ut-
most importance, are thoroughly reli-
able in affording quick relief. You
do not require to go on taking them
for a prolonged period, as is necessary
with some medicines, which even then
are mostly disappointing; you simply
take a dose of Frootoids when ill and
repeat the dose if necessary, but gene-
rally one dose is quite effective.
.Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so
far that they not only act as an ape-
rient, but do remove from the blood,
tissues* and internal organs all the
waste poisonous matter that is clog-
ging them and choking the channels
that lead to and from them. The bene-
ficial effects of Frootoids are evident
at once by the disappearance of head-
ache, the head becoming clear, and a
bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly,
and by the food being properly di-
gested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when
Congestion of the Brain or Apoplexy
is present or threatening. They have
been tested, and have been proved to
afford quick relief in such cases when
other aperients have not done any
good at all. It is of the utmost impor-
tance that this should be borne in
mind, for in such cases to take an or-
dinary aperient is to waste time and
permit of a serious illness becoming
fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
antibilious pills make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill
by antibilious pills that could have
been cured at once by Frootoids.
People should not allow themselves to
be duped into contracting a medicine-
taking habit by being persuaded to
take daily doses with each meal of so-
called indigestion cures that do NOT
cure. Frootoids have, been subjected
to extensive tests, and have in every
case proved successful in completely
curing the complaints named.
A constipated habit of body will be
completely cured if the patient will on
each occasion, when suffering, take a
dose ot Frootoids, instead of an or-
dinary aperient; by so doing, the pa-
tient will require doses only at longer
intervals, and will so become qiiite
independent of the necessity of taking
any aperient medicine.
Frootoids are only now being placed
on the Australian market, consequently
you may at present have a difficulty
in getting them from your local che-
mist or storekeeper; but ask for them,
and if you cannot get them at once,
send stamps or postal note for price,
1/6, to W. G. Hearne, Chemist, Gee-
long, and a bottle of them will be im-
mediately forwarded to you post free.
Chemists, storekeepers, and whole-
salers can now obtain wholesale sup-
plies from W. G. Hearne, Chemist,
Geelong, Victoria.

Up-to-date Fancy Goods Storo in Australia.
“Cockaigne” : The memoir of W. Crooks,
M.P., of Woolwich (England), is just issued. He
was a real Oliver Twist—a workhouse boy. He
despairs of ever finding anything elite to interest
him as that book did. He knew Bumble well,
and actually himself wanted “ more,” though he
didn’t dare to ask for it. Fortunately, he escaped
Fagin and Sikes.
From a Shanghai correspondent :
China is having a bad time of it lately. About 1,500,000
men, women and children are now dying of cold and
starvation. The cold will decrease, but the want of food
will increase. Smallpox has broken out, and is going
through the starving wretches at a frightful rate. Then
in the centre of China there is a rebellion. At present
there are over 18,000 Chinese students being educated, in
Japan— in other Words being filled up with incendiary
and anti-European ideas, and then let loose in China.
The Chinese returned students are chiefly carrying on
this rebellion. “ China for the Chinese ”is the war cry.
We may soon expect to hear “India for the Indians,”
“Java for the Javanese,” “ Burmah for the Burmese,”
etc., all being but the prelude for the grand war cry of
“ Asia for the Asiatics.” Japan, of course, expects to be
in command, and to gather in the spoils.
A letter from a passing unbeliever :
During the session of the Congregational Union
in Sydney, several speakers were at great pains to
point out the evils that might follow a more lax observ-
ance of the Sabbath, and one reverend gentleman con-
tended that the Church was the greatest friend of the
working man, in protecting-him from Sunday labor. This
sort of argument is all very well for those wdio know no
better. In France, where Sunday labor is a recognised
institution, the working man, using the term in the very
widest sense to include all who work for a living, such as
actors and journalists, are better off than in many coun-
tries where Sabbatarianism runs riot. In France there
is what is known as the Six Days Ordinance. This
enforces the Biblical commandment, “Six days
shalt thou labor,” which Moses laid on the table
of the Hebrew House of Reps. No one is permitted
to work his employees more than six days, and, should
he do so, he has not only to face perdition hereafter, but
a heavy fine at the hands of the nearest magistrate, at
once ; and, as magistrates are on the spot, and perdition
isn’t, the law is more successful in this matter than the
church. The result is that, while theatres, restaurants,
and all places of amusement are in full swing in Paris every
Sunday, they are run by different staffs, and the only result
of the Sunday labor is that a large number of people are
in w'ork who might otherwise be unemployed. The same
thing has been accomplished without the aid of the law
in American newspaper offices. Sunday issues are invari-
able, but the American reporter is sure of one day a week
clear, which he never is in the newspaper offices of Aus-
tralia, though they publish only six issues a week.
“Belaying Pin” continues the nautical
discussion :
Agree with “Sailor” (B. 18/4/’O7) that Australians do
not go to sea in any quantity on deck. But they are
more numerous than he wots of. I know of one first-class
firm of shipowners (sail) in Maoriland who give preference
to Australians and obtain six of them out of every eight
engaged. That firm admits it can get better work from
them, and they are more reliable. After a couple or three
years in one constant trade between Kaipara and Aus-
tralia, the desire to see other lands seizes them, and they
ship on some lime-juicer for other parts. I have
met them all the world over. “Sailor” says: “See-
ing the comparatively good pay and conditions, Aus-
tralian sailors ought to he good.” And they are ;
and therein lies the crux of the whole question.
Better pay, better food, and better conditions will soon
bring a desirable class of men to sea, and stop the
lamentable decrease in the marine J. Bull. The
parsimony of the British shipowner is proverbial. Whilst
Germany has made prodigious strides in the matter of
wages, food, and comfortable quarters, England has gone
hack, and things are worse than they were 20 years ago.
I venture to affirm that were the Orient and other com-
panies to emulate Australian methods of obtaining good
men, good results would assuredly follow; and those
lamentable scenes of drunkenness and obscenity so fre-
quently witnessed in both Melbourne and Sydney would
be things of the past. Prior to the ’9O strike Australia
had the pick of the British mercantile marine. When
the subsequent depression compelled seamen to accept
a reduction until their wages were only £5 per month,
the majority of them went to Westralia, then booming.
Some went up to the fields and made money. One of
them I knew well is now a prominent mine manager.
But most of them worked on the wharves, dredges, and
railway good sheds. This allowed an opening for many
foreigners, who have stayed with us ; and in justice to
thenr they have proved good men and citizens. The
Union Co) of Maoriland does more to encourage the home
product to adopt the sea as a profession than any other,
and something should be done by the Commonwealth
Government in the same direction, either by subsidising
the cos. or otherwise. For we must prepare to man our
own navy.
YE POETIC
EGOTIOST
FOR THE BULLETIN.
He bails me in a corner and lie slings,
On fierce, chaotic breath, Homeric things
Though I fix him with my eye,
Like a pin-stuck butterfly,
He only wags a wider set of wings.
And often I have seen the traffic reel,
Headlong backward, horse and foot, upon its heel,
Just to get another peep
At this gaseous rubbish-heap
Of stale Parisian pips and orange-peel.
He gurgles, and he seeks the treble clef
Of a waiter shouting orders to the chef,
And I feel my bosom sink
As I wonder what he’ll think
If perchance he should discover I am deaf !
N.S.W.
HUHT McCRE

“ Pincher Mick” writes from Pentridge
(Vic.) : —
Dear Mr. Bullyteen, —That cove wot wrote the blanky
verse about the Melbourne City Court is a mate
o mine. Leastways I never seen ’im in me life, but
a cove wot can express my erpinions like wot ’e did is my
pal all the time, and wots more, w’en ’e wants ter bite a
junk outer a piece o’ beer belongin’ ter me, ’e on’y ’as to
say so. That there line about retirin’ ole Panton is a
boshter, and ’its the nail fair on the crumpet, as the poet
says. It just is about time that ole bloke wos sent some-
weres w’ere ’e can be less interferin’. The world ’ud be
a appy place if it wasn’t fer the likes of ’im and ’is pals.
Is idea seems ter be to back up them rotten Johns wot-
ever they say ; and, Lor’ lumnie, wot won’t a John say
w en ’e’s got a pore innercent cove lined up in the Court.
" y, about 3 or 4 months ago, I wos just pokin’ round
quiet and peaceful like, ’opin’ to meet someone wot ’ad
a tanner to do in, and thinkin’ ’ow thirsty I wos,
wen before you could say “ Yow ” a big fat
lazy John grabs me by the neck most vicious, and
if a pair o’ boots, w’ich s’elp me I never seen afore,
didn’t drop outer me clobber. O’ course ’e accuses me o’
pinchin’ them, and that old Panton and ’is crowd gives
"-three months’ ’ard. The cove defendin’ me proved an
alibi wot would ’a’ satisfied nearly anybody, but that there
Push cm the Bench would believe a noo-joined John afore
they’d believe a Archbishop. That’s w’y I’m pals with
your co-respondent (as the noospapers say), and you can
tell im from me that I don’t believe that Shakespeer
isself could ’a’ done up ole Panton and ’is push better.
“ Wang ” : I saw a noble fight, the other
morning, under Duke of Guttersnipe’s rules. A
fishmonger’s barrow stood in the middle of Little
Lonsdale-street, Melbourne, while the owner was
in an adjacent pub. Presently two “lydies”
arrived on the scene, and started to make unkind
remarks about each other. Soon one of them
skipped lightly to the barrow, and, selecting
three feet of barracoota, slapped the other lydy
on the face with it. The other uttered a prayer
or something (rather fancy it was something), and
paid a hasty visit to the barrow and grasped a
weapon. For a few minuted the atmosphere was
filled with hair, fish-scales and strange words.
We formed a ring and settled down for a
period of unquiet enjoyment. Then a fishmongi r
appeared on one side and a policeman on the
other, and I expected a dazzling melee of four,
but the factory whistle blew, and I had to hurry
off to toil.

iron ores and exporting them, just as the Japs
propose to export ore from the Commonwealth ;
wherefore a law has been passed, prohibiting any
foreign person from “owning or using forests,
mines, or waterfalls.” ' ' I

CURABLE DISORDERS.
There are some diseases which are incurable, but these are fortunately rare. There are, on the
other hand, two groups of disorders which afflict a large number of peopie, many of whom seem to
consider that there is no hope of release, for they go on suffering year after year when their cases
would readily yield to treatment if the root of the disorder were attacked. The two groups referred to
are uric and biliary disorders, and they comprise the following common complaints
ITeio
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Gravel and Stone
Bladder Troubles
Biliaby
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice
Sick Headache
Anaemia
General Debility
Blood Disorders
Now all these disorders originate from the same cause—namely, the inability of the kidneys and
liver to properly perform their function of eliminating from the system the urinary and biliary
poisons which produce the disease.
The Kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly
an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily, all this solid matter leaves the body dis-
solved in the urine, but if, through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their duty
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins
contaminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as
Xlheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia,
Gravel, Stone, and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are
healthy is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural
color, or has particles floating about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must imme-
diately be taken to restore their vigor, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifesta-
tions of uric poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver
takes sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again
supply it to the blood as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, whioh is
insoluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles
which have lived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer
from some form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice.
Sick Headache, General Debility, and Blood Disorders.
, So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by the liver, that
where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys, the liver becomes affected in sympathy and vice
versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of those vital organa
wnich resulted m the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be found
w icn would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive search
or sucn a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in compounding a
medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe Cure exhibits a
arvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and liver, and
restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all complaints due to
Jit v®ntion in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys and liver
w. eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease, Cures affected
by Warner s Safe Cure are permanent simply because they are natural. v " - * [4j
Verney’s Objective
or The Bulletin.]
FOB. my part,” Verney was saying,
“if I didn’t have a definite purpose in
life—something to look forward to and
live for, I’d ”
He broke off, drained his glass, and replaced it
quietly on the little cafe table. The traffic-roar
of the city blurred a little space of silence. Cor-
dray, seated opposite, gazed thoughtfully across
with half-shut eyes, inwardly commenting that
his friend had the forehead and eyes of a poet, the
lips and jaw of a mathematician, and the strong,
nervous hands of a surgeon. Cordray was lazily
surprised that he had not remarked these charac-
teristics before. He exhaled three voluminous
mouthfuls of tobacco-smoke and spoke :
“Well, Verney, if you hadn’t a—what do you
call it ?—you’d ”
“Oh ! ” Verney said. “ I’d go—you know
“Mad!” said Cordray. Then he added,
hastilj'', “I beg your pardon, Verney. I was too
brutally direct. I know that isn’t a pleasant—er
—subject.”
“Well, no, Cordray, scarcely, pleasant. A man
whose father died in an asylum naturally doesn’t
revel in the theme of insanity. Still, it wasn’t
your fault, Cordray. I brought the subject up,
and.... see, Cordray— l’m afraid of it.”
Oordray’s well-featured, trim-bearded face ex-
pressed mild astonishment. He waved aside a
lazy smoke-cloud, and peered interestedly across.
“ What! that you might ?.... Pooh ! My dear
fellow, you’re as sound as—l am.”
Verney shook his head, gloomily. “That’s what
I meant when I spoke of a supposititious con-
tingency of having no object in life. I simply
could not exist—and be sane—unless I had some-
thing definite to live for.”
Cordray smoked silently for half-a-minute.
Then he laughed.
“But assuming the very improbable, Verney.
Assuming a time when you hadn’t an object, what
then ? Suicide ? ”
Verney shuddered, and helped himself to
whisky. “ Ugh ! Never! ” he said, intensely.
"I’droafe something to live for, Cordray. I’d
think, plan, dream, scheme, chicane until I found
something.”
“But,” persisted Cordray, “suppose all your
planning , failed—that you were driven back on
yourself. What then ? ”
“I’d go mad.” Verney shivered slightly. “So
depend upon it, I wouldn’t fail.”
“Well!” Cordray laughed pleasantly. “Of
course this is mere academic talk. You have
plenty to live for, old man.”
“ Yes,” said Verney, and his eyes glowed. “ I
have my wife and my home to make snug ” ——
Cordray suddenly stooped to empty the ashes
of his pipe into the spittoon. When he sat up
again he filled his glass, and sipped slowly with-
out speaking.
“Talking of my wife” —Verney looked at his
watch, and got briskly to his feet— “ I must be
jogging along. I have to take her to the florist’s,
to buy flowers. She’s taken a fancy to chrysan-
themums lately—white chrysanthemums—and she
wants to get some good ones. By the way,
pordray, that’s your favorite flower, isn’t it?
That’s a remarkably fine specimen you’ve got in
your coat. Well, s’long.”
Verney strode a few paces, turned, and went
back.
“But—say, Cordray,” he asked, jocularly,
“supposing you lost the grip of things, what
would you do ? ”
Responded Cordray, quietly, “I’ve a trifle of
imagination, too, and I’d go out!”
Verney laughed good-humoredly, and walked
briskly off. “ Queer old character—Cordray,” he
mused, and hailed a cab.
Cordray thoughtfully emptied his glass.
• * * *
It was a week later that ugly Truth leapt out of
the Unsuspected, and struck Verney vitally. An
impulsive man, in similar case, might have
written some crimson-lettered history of a melo-
dramatic kind ; but Verney’s mathematical mouth
negatived haste, and he was able to handle him-
self through the first shock, and consider the
position critically.
He decided that nothing was to be gained by
making a public scandal. Nothing could undo,
or pardon, his wife’s treachery, or Cordray’s base-
ness, and the idea of revenge—having been coldly
analysed—did not appeal to him just then. He
resolved to conceal his knowledge, and “leave
them to it.” He wrote to her, briefly. He was
unexpectedly called away on business, he said ;
date of. his return was uncertain; she need not
allow his departure to interfere with her move-
ments in any way—she would, of course, enjoy
herself as usual during his absence. Verney
chuckled grimly as he wrote “as usual.” He
thought the words a rather good touch, and felt
inclined to underline them, but, after considera-
tion, refrained.
Then Verney went forth, scarcely realising yet
how hard he was hit, and his old environment
knew him no more for a time.
He became a nocturnal lounger of parks, and an
observer of the queer characters he met there;
a prowler of dark streets, and witness of many
unrecorded midnight happenings; a frequenter
of wharves and water-side public-houses, where he
saw much, and heard more, that considerably
enlarged his knowledge of life.
The experience was welcome ; it was something
to live for, he told himself. But interest flagged
with familiarity, and one morning he awoke after
a night of dissipation with some chance com-
panions, to a splitting headache, and the realisa-
tion that the last vestige of interest in the world
had vanished.during his sleep.
He turned from his late associates with dis-
gust, sought solitude, and asked himself scorn-
fully what business he had to be alive. He had
nothing to live for! A glimpse of a big coal-
lieaver, black from his toil from boots to hat, the
whites of his eyes rolling grotesquely like a
negro’s, slouching wearily home through the dusk,
thrilled him with sudden envy. The man was
poor, ignorant, toil-driven—but he had something
to live for! A wife—children maybe; at all
events, something, if it were only beer.
The episode of the coal-heaver was merely the
first of many such. Verney stood aside and
watched the world move past; watched ships sail
out fpr far countries, bearing breathing, hopeful,
interested humanity, and other ships come in,
similarly freighted ; and the sight angered him.
Ihe . trains, the trams, the cabs, the’buses, the
jostypg street crowds, the roar and shriek of the
■oity tortured him. The turmoil evidenced Life —
seething and interested ; from which he alone was
outcast. He alone seemed to have nothing to live
for.
“It won’t do.” Verney dropped into a park
seat, and marshalled his wits. “It will never dp.
I’ve just got to find something to live for, or I’ll
go mad, safe enough.”
For a week Verney was the most interested
man in Sydney—interested by the problem of in-
venting something to be interested in. At the
end of the week he had formulated a scheme, and
became really interested.
His old friends, when he re-appeared among
them, honestly sympathised with Verney. A
diabolical crime, they said; sad, too, to think
that he should have been absent when it hap-
pened. An inexplicable crime; the detectives
could make nothing of it. Still, “ murder will
out, you know,” and so on, and so on. All of
which Verney heard with the sober air of a man
whose sorrow was too deep for utterance ; and
mournfully deprecated further comment with a
pleading gesture of his crape-banded arm. His
public demeanor of grief lacked nothing of verisi-
militude.
When alone, however, the lugubrious exterior
dropped from him like a black cloak ; his figure
straightened, and a look of complacent content
routed the solemn lines from his face. He looked,
then, the perfect antithesis of a bereaved husband.
One day, shortly after the tragedy, Verney
paused upon the steps of his hotel to light a
cigar, and serenely eyed the traffic. “ Something
to live for now, by Jove!” he chuckled, and
stepped briskly out to hunt up Cordray.
Cordray was rather difficult to find—was, in
fact, endeavoring to avoid Verney—but Verney
found him at length, re-assumed, as he ap-
proached, the metaphorical black cloak, and
gloomily suggested drinks.
Oordray would have declined, but Verney’s
inter-locked arm was not easily escaped, and the
men were soon seated at a quiet cafe table. Cor-
dray was obviously not well. His usually well-
groomed frame looked comparatively seedy ; his
beard had grown scraggy from inattention, and
his eyes lacked fire. He had the air of a man
who was fretting about something. Verney,
with great apparent solicitude, remarked on this.
“ What on earth is the matter, Cordray ? Come,
think better of it, and teil me. You look worse
every time I see you. Haven’t lost a dear rela-
tive, or a—er —sweetheart, or anything of that
sort? Not worrying about anything, eh, old
man?”
“ Oh, rubbish, Verney ! ” said Cordray, shortly,
“of course not. How often are you going to ask
me that ? What have Ito worry about ? ”
“True,” said Verney. “What, indeed ! Now
if you were in my place! If you had lost the
woman you loved ” And he shook his head
gloomily.
“Yes, yes, I know, Verney,” Cordray said,
impatiently.
“I beg your pardon, old man,” Verney said.
“I know I must bore you by harping on my
trouble in this fashion, but I really can’t help it.
You see, I’m so horribly cut up, and you’re the
only friend I have, Cordray.”
Cordray silently replenished his glass.
“My only friend, Cordray.”
Co’dray sipped his liquor.
“ You always we: e my best friend, Cordray.”
Cordray sipped his liquor.
“ A good friend, Cordray.”
Cordray sipped his liquor.
“A real, honorable, true friend, Cordray.”
Cordray sipped his liquor.
“A friend I could love —and trust, Cordray.”
Cordray finished his liquor.
“ 1 could trust you with my life, Cordray.”
Cordray’s glass clattered slightly on the table
as he placed it down.
“ I could even have trusted you with my wife,
Cordray.”
“ Verney—will you stop ! ” Cordray’s face was
unusually pale, and little beads of perspiration
glistened on his forehead.
“ She’s dead—murdered—Cordray. Cut down
in her youth and beauty—and innocence, Cor-
dray ! Ah ! Cordray, if you knew how good and
innocent she was!”
Verney was looking straight into Cordray’s
face, and his eyes had a glint of malicious enjoy-
ment. But Cordray did not meet his glance.
“You met her, didn’t you, Cordray? Yes, of
course, I introduced you, I remember. But you
only met her that once, Cordray ? ”
“ Only that once.” Cordray’s voice was husky.
“Only once!” Verney repeated. “Seems
queer now to think that you, my best friend,
should have met my wife only once. But had she
lived, you’d have met her oftener, Cordray. I’m
sure you’d have been good friends—very good
friends—had she lived.”
Verney sipped his liquor with a solemn air, and
looked at the ceiling with half-closed eyes. Cor-
dray hurriedly lit his pipe, and enveloped himself
in smoke with short, spasmodic puffs.
“ By the way, Cordray,” Verney said, suddenly,
“I notice you never wear a white chrysanthemum
in your coat, now. Why? You were seldom
without one, once.”
“Oh!” Cordray said, unsteadily, “I don’t
know. Mislaid the taste. I suppose.”
“ She developed quite an extraordinary love for
white chrysanthemums some time before her
death. My wife, you know, Cordray, used to get
fresh ones every day, and wear one in her bosom.
I told her she couldn’t have been more tender
with the flowers if a lover had sent them. It
was only a joke, you know, Cordray, but she
didn’t like it. She was so pure and honorable
that she couldn’t bear to hear that sort of thing
referred to even in jest.”
Cordray rose as if to end the conversation, but
Verney stopped him with a gesture.
“Don’t be impatient with me, Cordray. I
know I’m a bore, but I must talk about her. I
loved her so, you see. And she loved me so much,
Cordray. I know that when she was dying her
last thought was of me, Cordray. ”
Cordray fidgeted in his chair.
“Of me, Cordray. Not of anybody else in the
world, Cordray. Of her husband, Cordray.”
“ I’ll really have to leave you now, Verney.”
Cordray looked at his watch.
“Did you ever love a woman, Cordray? A
good woman, Cordray ; such a woman, say, as my
wife, Cordray ? Of course not, for there was
only one woman like her—and that was herself.
But if you had loved such a woman, Cordray, and
she were suddenly stricken down like—eh ?”
Cordray was silent.
“ Would you go mad, Cordray ? Do you think
you would want to live afterwards? Do you
think you’d have anything to live for, Cordray?”
Still Cordray was silent; he was nervously
stuffing tobacco into his pipe.
“Did you hear how she died, Cordray? She
was stabbed, Cordray—stabbed to the heart.
They found her, Cordray, with her once beautiful
face turned up to the mocking stars—all stark
and unlovely in death; her hands, that Iso loved
to kiss, were clenched as with pain, and all
crimsoned with her blood."
“ For Heaven’s sake, Yerney
There were flowers at her bosom, and they
Theyhadbeen
“ Yerney ! ”
“ White chrysanthemums, Cordray.”
“ Verneyl” J y
“ Your favorite flowers, Cordray! What.
Cordray! 111, old man? Take my arm, then ;
I’ll get you a cab.”
#
. * *
I hat night Cordray suddenly decided that he
had lost the grip of things, and that trifle of
imagination, which he had aforetime mentioned
to Yerney, suggested that he should “go out.”
So “ go out ” he did.
“ Hang him, what was he in such a hurry for ?”
said Yerney, irritably, when he heard the news.
“ I didn’t anticipate he’d do that—so soon. He
has disarranged my scheme. What am I to amuse
myself with now ? What am Ito live for?”
For just one week Yerney pondered that
question. Then he astonished the country by
confessing to the law that it was he who had killed
his wife. The rest is told, in his diary by the
I** John Allensby, gaol chaplain: * *

A Few Handy First-Aid Hints.
The unique healing powers of Zam-Buk Balm having
established it as the standard “ first-aid” for home use, the
following (necessarily brief) hints on the
proper treatment of common injuries will
prove useful to housewife and bread-
winner alike
Cuts. —Rinse with cold water and bind up
tightly with clean linen, on which Zam-Buk Balm has
been smeared. Apply second dressing a few hours
later.
Bruises. —Rub Zam-Buk well in,
rubbing only one way, towards the heart.
Swelling will be lessened, pain soothed, and
discoloration prevented.
Burns and Scalds. Apply
the Balm direct to wound or spread on strips
of lint. Apply with least possible delay, the exclusion
of air being of first importance.
Jagged Cuts, Scrapes, Etc.— Wash
carefully in tepid water with soft clean rag, and apply
Zam-Buk as for cuts.
Sprains. —ln serious cases foment to reduce
swelling. Then rub Zam-Buk gently in for several
minutes, afterwards bandaging firmly but not tightly.
A few days’ rest of the injured joint is all that
should be necessary in the average case.
A pot of Zam-Buk Balm should be
always on a handy shelf ready for use
at a moment’s notice.

Manly By The Sea.
[For The Bulletin.]
A SPIRIT-FREE Bohemia
Is Manly bv the sea,
Where Punchinello laughs to scorn
Conventional decree ;
A Pagan village set apart
From morbid sites of man,
Where life drifts on in artless art
And never stops to plan.
The tawny men are lithe of limb
At Manly by the sea,
Their loins are cast in Grecian mould
As God meant men to be,
And Hebes gay, with taunting lips
And love-expectant face,
Roam o’er the beach with swinging hips—
The parents of the race.
We take no thought of pomp and power
At Manly by the sea;
None careth who or what you are,
Or asks your pedigree.
So that you love a rosy lip
And a surf-whitened shore,
And drink a toast with fellowship—
We ask for nothing more.
We never know the time o’ day
At Manly by the sea.
It is a new Arcadia,
And none there asks for fee ;
For no one thinks of anything
More stern than love and play ;
The night slips onward banqueting,
The day is dreamed away.
No sounds of battle-stresses come
To Manly by the sea,
We speak the speech of wand’ring waves,
And sweet it is to me.
Tho wooing sea, O Manly, spills
Its glory at your feet,
And somewhere ’yond the drowsy hills
The shut-in cities greet.
Your soul is steeped in indolence,
O Manly by the sea !
What time the soft north-easters blow
You idle fancy-free;
The white surf sings a lullaby
That soothes your sins with sleep,
And all the lords of earth may die,
But you would never weep.
N.s.w.
W. M. Whitney.
“Doogan’s Little Harse.”
[For The Bulletin.]
IT was race day. The silence of the little
bush settlement was broken. The crowd-
rough, bearded pioneers of the bush ; lank,
loose-made boys with trousers several inches too
short, and broad-leafed felt hats supported mainly
by large, contorted ears, and blucher boots laced
with string ; women who were carrying babies, and
women who were not; and dogs in great number
and variety—had been arriving since nine in the
morning. Some of the humans arrived in carts;
some transpired in drays; some chanced along on
horseback with saddles, and some occurred on
plough horses with winkers and a bag. Some came
pulling lean-looking horses along. The dogs came
on foot. The crowd always left the pub veran-
dah, and walked to the rails, when one of the
led horses came, and men said to each other;
“Wonder what horse is that?” Anyone who
was able to supply the information was treated
with special respect.
There were only three starters in the first race
—they were all holding back for the “Big Han-
dicap ”of five sovereigns. We went up with an
old screw, and brought young Jimmy Dunn to
ride for us. We were standing a bit away from the
crowd on the verandah, when big Doolan came
up. He didn’t know us, but he knew Jimmy.
He shook hands with that brief object, and said t
“Now, you’re a shmall little b’y, Jimmy. Do
you ever go above on aharse ?”
Jimmy reckoned he did—had been riding for
three years.
“Ah, then, you have? Well, av coorse it
have nothing to ;do wid me, Jimmy, for I’m one
av the cummittee, but Doogan have no one to ride
his little harse. I suppose, now, you can ride
purty well? What I mane: You wouldn’t lave
’em come up inside o’ ye, or lose the start, we’ll
say ? ”
“ What’re you givin’ us?” said Jimmy, with an
injured look. „
“ Well, see Jimmy, I’m the starter (and he
shut one eye hard), “ so’t have nothing to do wid
me; but I know Doogan have no one to ride his
little harse. Now, suppose you arrange with
Doogan. What’s the lightest you ride, if it’s a fair
question ? You’re a shmall little b’y, Jimmy, and
can ride very light, I’ll engage.”
“Oh ! ’bout seven stun two.
“You can? It’s a nice weight. But I can’t
interfere in any way, for you see, Jimmy, I’m one
of the handicappers. See, there’s Janny Ragan,
the judge, standin’ over ferninst us. He’s a nice
obligin’ man. He’ll go wid you to Doogan. Tell
him I sint you.”
We decided to put our few pence on Duggan’s
horse. . , , ,
The crowd waited anxiously on the verandah
for the weights. Doolan came out with a piece
of paper and a pencil, jumped on an empty beer
cask, and read: “Luther, twelve-stun-thirteen;
Dare Divil, twelve-stun-five.” And so he went
on through the list till he came to “ O’Connell,
seven-stun-two.” And we at once realised that
this must be “ Doogan’s little harse.”
The excitement increased. The crowd walked
on to the course just at the back of the pub. The
solitary bookmaker was roaring out the odds.
Boys with sleeves above their elbows, handker-
chiefs tied on their heads, and trousers stuffed
inside their socks, were prancing down the course
to take their “ preliminary.” The first two came
up in great style, flinging dirt behind them, and
making much noise until they got nearly level
with the orowd. Then they bolted off, but
stopped suddenly when they came to the fence.
The riders went over the fence. They weren’t
used to people, these horses of the bush, and the
dense crowd of fully 200 persons had a great
terror for them.
“Two to one Dare Devil 1 Even money
Luther! Five to one bar two 1 Eight to one
O’Connell! ”
Duggan was struggling to get away from his
father and brother-in-law. .
“ Let me go, I tell you. Let me get it on while
he’s eight to one.” , (T
“ Don’t be a fool,” the old man was saying. I
know they’ll do what they can, as they’re part-
ners with ye, but he might fall. Don’t put so
much on. You’ll ruin yourself, man.
But Duggan jerked himself away. He was
determined to plunge. He was very pale and all
of a tremble, as he handed over every fraction ot
17s. 6d. and took the bookmaker’s ticket. #
They drew for places at the start. Duggans
horse had inside running. Doolan drew for Dug-
gan . He warned the boys about making false
starts. Then he shut both eyes hard at Jimmy.
Jimmy dug both spurs hard into O Connell
and shot away. Doolan dropped the nag, and
shouted “Aff.” Then he wheeled his old pie-
bald, and came galloping across the course.
People clambered into carts and on to the
fence. Some of the people on the fence, for-
getting in their excitement where they were,
started to clap their hands, and fell backwards,
each dragging three or four others with him.
Several others were engaged pelting dogs off the
coura*.
O’Connell led about half the journey, and was
then left behind. Dare Devil and Luther passed
the post together, with O’Connell 10 lengths
away. Most of the others ran off the course
when the crowd began to yell. Doolan had tried
to watch the race as he galloped across, but lie
collided with a stump, and was nearly knocked
off. Then he only watched where he was going,
but kept on shouting, “Doogan’s little harse !
Doogan’s little harse ! ” But “Dead ’eat! Dare
Devil! Luther!” was being yelled from scores
of throats. Doolan ran over a man and two dogs,
and pulled up at the winning-post, and,, looking
very fierce, shouted again, “Doogans little
“Duggan’s horse be blowed. He was no-
where,” someone said.
“Janny! Janny Ragan,” Doolan called out
“Doogan’s harse have won. You saw the foul
ridin’ at the turn.” . IIT .
“ Certainly ! Certainly ! ” said Ragan. Doo-
gan’s horse was jostled.” ~ , <•
“What’re vou gettin at?” said the owner ot
Luther. “Why, they was three lengths ahead
when they took his runnin’. We’ll show you
the rules. Bring the rules,” he shouted to no
one in particular. ._ _ ,
“Look here, me bould hero, ’ said Doolan to
the man, with a look of importance, “allow me
to inforum you that we have min upon this cum-
mittee that are capable of settlin’ all disputes
widout a rule or a regilation.” .
By this time the boys had got together, their
horses—spur-marked from shoulder to stifle
prancing sideways, throwing their heads down
and covering everybody with froth. Doolan
glared at the boys, and spoke severely : “It is
my painful jooty to inforum ye that the riders
of Dare Divil and Luther are disqualified for
ridin’ unsatisfactory to the cummittee for the
rest av the meetin’. An’ ye may thank a lament
cummittee that ye’re not disqualified for life.
Doogan’s little harse have won.”
Several men made a rush at Doolan they all
seemed to want him at once. He darted across
towards an old bullock-dray, and tried to pick up a
fence-rail as he went, but hadn t time, lie put
his back to the dray, and glared round him for
something. Then he gripped a dog by the hind
legs and smote his enemies to the earth with it
as fast as they came up. In a few minutes the
fight became nice and general. The policeman
lost his head and fired a shot over the heads of the
combatants. Some who were near him fell with
fright. , ...
A bullock-bell rang, and a man called out:
“ Saddle hup fer the Cocky’s ’Andicap.”
And we went to saddle up.
Paul Cupid.
The Girl of the Day-Before-
Yesterday.
[FOR THE ULLETIN]
SOME bard’s been shedding a scalding tear
O’er the dead, cold ashes of “yester-year
A sort of figure of speech, you know,
For the frozen corpse of the long ago.
He tunes his wail in a doleful key,
And he tries to prejudice you and me
In a mean and pessimistic way
’Gainst all that’s bright in the world to-day.
He claims, in a general way, you see,
That nothing’s as good as it used to bo ;
That all his poetical soul holds dear
Went out with the days of “ yester-year ” ;
That naught will now with those days compare,
When men were gallant, and maidens fair—
But, what’s the matter, we humbly pray,
With the girl of the day-before-yesterday ?
Say, isn’t she fixed with a “ shell-like ear,
As small as those others of “ yester-year” ;
And isn’t she fitted with eyes as bright,
And isn’t her cuticle just as white ?
And hasn’t she gotten a “ chiselled nose,”
And cheeks as red as the “ damask rose ?
Or, if she hasn’t, why rouge ain’t dear—
It’s as cheap as it was in “ yester-year.
Her dainty waist is as small, you know,
As those of the maids of long ago;
And still some lover, on some pretence,
Discovers its true circumference—
Of course it’s the same old pantomime
The cave-man played in the dawn of time—
And the vows he vows are the same to-day,
And he keeps ’em much in the cave-man’s way.
The girl of the day-before-yesterday
Can wheedle a man in the old, old way ;
Her lips are sweet, and they cling to you
In the same old way that they used to do.
And the bard is blind, and his eyes are blear,
If he thinks that the maids of “ yester-year
Can give any (adjective) points away
To the girl of the day-before-yesterday.
But what of the wives of “ yester-year ” ?
We can well believe they were sweet and dear
And that, when their hubbies a-sleeping lay,
They searched their clothes in the old, old way
But odds in shillings we’d like to lay,
That the wife of the day-before-yesterdav
Can sweep your pockets as clean and clear
As her ancestresses of “ yester-year.
N.S.W.
